The Twenty Impossible Attributes as Opposites: Linguistic vs. Technical
Al-Bajuri explains why the twenty impossible attributes are called opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the twenty impossible attributes are called 'opposites' in a broad linguistic sense and how they differ from strict technical opposites.

Core Idea
‘Opposite’ here means absolute contradiction, not the technical rule of two existents with utmost difference.

Al-Bajuri begins by explaining the author’s use of the term 'opposites' for the twenty impossible attributes. He shows that the author chose a broad, linguistic meaning to avoid implying that God’s attributes are temporal. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and previews the various logical relationships among the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the key terms like 'ضد' and 'نقيض' and how they are used.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Bajuri argues that 'opposite' here is used in its linguistic sense, not the technical sense. Find the sentence where he states this explicitly.
Look for the phrase 'المراد بالضد هنا: المعنى اللغوي'.
3
Understand the technical definition
The passage gives the technical definition of two opposites: two existent things with utmost difference, not coexisting but both absentable. Compare this with the linguistic sense.
Example: blackness and whiteness.
4
Note the variety among the twenty
Al-Bajuri mentions that among the twenty, some are true opposites, some are contradictories, etc. This sets up a more detailed classification later.
This is a preview, not a full breakdown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَهِيَ أَضْدَادُ الْعِشْرِينَ الْأُولَى أي: الأَوَّل ضد للأول، والثاني للثاني، وهكذا على الترتيب المتقدّم في الواجبات وأطلق المصنف الأضداد على المقابل لصفاته تعالى، ولم يَعْكِس؛ لأن صفاته تعالى قديمة، فلا تكون ضدّاً لغيرها. هكذا يُؤْخَذُ من كلام الشيخ يس. ويُحِثَ فيه: بأن التضاد: نسبة من الجانبين فكلُّ منهما ضِدُّ للآخر، ولا يلزمُ من ذلك كَوْنُ صفاته تعالى حادثةً؛ لأن الضدَّ كما يُطْلَقُ على الحادث؛ يُطْلَقُ على القديم والمراد بالضد هنا: المعنى اللغوي، وهو مُطْلَقُ المُنَافي، وإلا؛ فليست هذه العشرون كلُّها أضداداً للعشرين الأولى بالمعنى الاصطلاحي؛ لأن الضدين في الاصطلاح هما الأمران الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غايةُ الخلاف، لا يجتمعان، وقد يرتفعان كالسواد والبياض وليست هذه العشرون كلُّها كذلك، بل بعضُها ضد، وبعضها نقيض، وبعضُها مُسَاءٍ للنقيض، وبعضُها أخصُّ من النقيض كما ستقف عليه إن شاء الله تعالى.
His statement: 'And they are opposites of the first twenty' meaning: the first is opposite to the first, the second to the second, and so on according to the order that preceded in the necessary attributes. And the author used 'opposites' for what corresponds to His attributes, exalted is He, and did not reverse, because His attributes, exalted is He, are eternal, so they cannot be opposite to other than them. Thus it is taken from the words of Sheikh Yas. And it is urged therein: that opposition is a relation from both sides, so each of them is opposite to the other, and that does not necessitate that His attributes, exalted is He, be temporal, because 'opposite' as applied to the temporal is also applied to the eternal. And what is meant by 'opposite' here is the linguistic meaning, which is absolute contradiction; otherwise, these twenty are not all opposites to the first twenty in the technical sense, because the two opposites in terminology are the two existent things between which there is utmost difference, they do not coexist, and they may both be absent, like blackness and whiteness. And these twenty are not all like that, rather some of them are opposites, some are contradictories, some are similar to the contradictory, and some are more specific than the contradictory, as you will come to know if God wills.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a terminological classification. Al-Bajuri explains the author's statement that the twenty impossible attributes are 'opposites' (أضداد) of the first twenty necessary attributes. He clarifies that 'opposite' here is used in a broad linguistic sense, meaning any contradictory attribute, not the strict technical sense of two co-existent things that cannot meet and may both be absent. He reasons that using the technical sense would imply God's attributes are temporal, which is false. Instead, the term 'opposite' is applied to both eternal and temporal. He then notes that in technical terminology, opposites are existent things with utmost difference (like black and white). He concludes that among these twenty, some are true opposites, some are contradictories, some are similar to contradictories, and some are more specific than contradictories, promising further detail.

Key Terms
ضد
نقيض
معنى لغوي
معنى اصطلاحي
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The twenty impossible attributes are all technically opposite to the twenty necessary attributes.
Correction: Only some are technical opposites; others are contradictories or other logical relations. The term 'opposite' here is used in a broad linguistic sense.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use the linguistic meaning of 'opposite' rather than the technical meaning?
A) To avoid implying that God's attributes are temporal
B) Because the technical meaning only applies to created things
C) Because the author preferred the linguistic meaning
D) Because the twenty are all non-existents

Key Takeaway
The term 'opposite' for the impossible attributes is used linguistically to mean absolute contradiction, not the strict technical sense, and among the twenty, various logical relationships exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you sometimes slip into assuming that human language captures them perfectly? How does this lesson's distinction between linguistic and technical meaning challenge that assumption? What will you do to guard against sloppy thinking about the Divine?
?
The rebuttal in this passage protects the eternity of Allah's attributes. Have you ever felt uncomfortable that theological terms might imply something unworthy of Allah? How will you from today insist on precise language when speaking about your Lord?
?
This lesson shows that not all 'opposites' are the same. In your own understanding of tawheed, are there areas where you have lumped different concepts together in a way that blurs important distinctions? Commit to studying one such area this week.

The Ten Types of Likeness to Created Things
Sanusi enumerates ten types of likeness to created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten types of likeness (mumāthala) to created things that are impossible for Allah, and the logical relation between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa).

Core Idea
Likeness to creation is impossible for Allah in ten distinct ways.

Sanusi begins by explaining that the term 'likeness' (mumāthala) extends to bodies and accidents, and that it stands in opposition to 'otherness' (mukhālafa) to created things. He then lists ten specific ways in which likeness to originated things would be impossible for Allah, forming a foundational taxonomy in Ash‘ari theology. This lesson presents that enumeration and explains each type.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical opposition
Read the opening lines explaining how mumāthala and mukhālafa are opposites. Note that mukhālafa is the negation of mumāthala, so they are true negations of each other.
This mirrors earlier logic in the text.
2
Learn the ten types
Go through each of the ten listed types of likeness: being a body, accident, direction, etc. Notice the order and what each type entails.
These are all impossible for Allah.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why does the author list these ten? Consider that they cover all possible ways a creature could be similar to its Creator, and negating them fully protects tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: الشاملة للأَجْرَام والأعراض أخذاً مما بعده، والتقابل بينها وبين المخالفة للحوادث من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه على نَسَقٍ ما قبله؛ لأن نقيض المخالفة للحوادث : لا مخالفة للحوادث، وهي عينُ المماثلة للحوادث. واعلم أنَّ أنواع المماثلة عَشَرَةُ: الأول: أن يكون جزماً، الثاني: أن يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم، والثالث: أن يكون في جهةٍ، الرابع: أن يكون له هو جهةً، الخامس أن يكون في مكان السادس أن يكون في زمان، السابع: أن يكون مَحَلّاً للحوادث، الثامن: أن يكون متّصفاً بالصَّغَر التاسع أن يكون متصفاً بالكبر، العاشر : أن يكون متصفاً بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام وقد ذكرها المصنف على هذا الترتيب. فتدبر. (قوله: بأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا تصوير للمماثلة للحوادث بأنواعها العشرة المذكورة. (قوله: (جزماً) هو ما مَلَاً ،فراغاً، سواء كان مركباً أو مفرداً، بخلاف الجسم، فإنه يختص بالمركب. والصحيحُ : أن مُعْتَقِدَ الجِسْمِيَّةِ لا يَكْفُرُ إِلا إن قال: إنه جِسْم كالأجسام، فالمكفِّرُ في الحقيقة إنما هو التشبيه. (قوله: أَيْ: تَأْخُذُ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ ... إلخ) تفسير لمدخول (أن) باللازم؛ لأنه يلزمُ من كونه جزماً أخذُهُ قدراً من الفراغ واستفيد من كلامه: أنه يجوز إطلاق الذات عليه تعالى وهو الصحيح. وقيل: لا يجوز ذلك. وقيل بالوقف. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: تَضويرٌ لِلْمُمَاثَلَةِ) أي: بيان لها ببيان أسبابها، فالباء في قوله بأن يكون جزماً) سببية لا تصويرية كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: تَفْسِيرٌ لِمَدْخُولِ أَنْ بِاللَّازِم الظاهر أنه تفسير حقيقي؛ لأن الكون جرماً هو الجرمية، وحقيقة الجرمية: أخذ الذات قدراً من الفراغ.
His saying: 'and likeness (al-mumāthala) to originated things' means encompassing bodies and accidents, taken from what follows. The opposition between it and otherness (al-mukhālafa) to originated things is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, in the pattern of what preceded, because the negation of otherness to originated things is 'no otherness to originated things', and it is identical to likeness to originated things. Know that the types of likeness are ten: first: that He be a body (jazm), second: that He be an accident subsisting in a body, third: that He be in a direction, fourth: that He be a direction, fifth: that He be in a place, sixth: that He be in a time, seventh: that He be a locus for accidents, eighth: that He be described as small, ninth: that He be described as large, tenth: that He be described as having purposes in actions or judgments. The author has mentioned them in this order. So reflect. His saying: 'by His being... etc.' This is a depiction of likeness to originated things by its ten mentioned types. His saying: 'a body (jazman)' is that which fills a void, whether compound or simple, unlike the body (jism), which is specific to compound. The correct view is that the believer in corporeality (al-jismiyya) does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies; indeed, the cause of disbelief in reality is comparison (al-tashbīh). His saying: 'i.e., His exalted essence takes... etc.' is an interpretation of the object of 'an' (that) by the necessary consequence, because from His being a body necessarily follows that His essence takes up a measure of void. And it is understood from his statement that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (al-dhāt) to Him, exalted, and this is correct. Some say it is not permissible. Some say to stop. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī: His saying: 'a depiction of likeness' means an explanation of it by stating its causes. So the 'ba' (ب) in his statement 'by His being a body' is causal, not depictive, as is apparent. His saying: 'an interpretation of the object of 'an' by the necessary consequence'—apparently it is a real interpretation, because being a body is corporeality (al-jarmiyya), and the reality of corporeality is the essence taking up a measure of void.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the ten types of likeness (mumathala) to created things that are impossible for God. It begins by noting that the opposition between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa) to created things is of the same logical type as earlier: equivalence. Then it lists ten types: being a body, being an accident, being in a direction, being a direction, being in a place, being in a time, being a locus for accidents, being qualified as small, being qualified as large, and being qualified by purposes. Then al-Bajuri comments on the first type (being a body): a body (jazm) is what fills a void, whether simple or compound, whereas jism is specifically compound. He then states the correct position on takfir: a person who believes God is a body (jismiyya) is not a disbeliever unless he explicitly says God is a body like other bodies, because the actual cause of disbelief is tashbih (direct comparison). Then he explains that the phrase 'takes His essence...' is an interpretation by necessary consequence: being a body necessarily entails occupying space. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'depiction' means explanation by causes, and the 'ba' is causal. He also says that the interpretation is real because being a body is essentially the same as occupying space.

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumāthala)
مخالفة (mukhālafa)
جزم (jazm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of likeness to created things are enumerated?
A) 8
B) 9
C) 10
D) 7

Key Takeaway
There are ten distinct ways in which likeness to created things is impossible for Allah, covering all dimensions of similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah hearing your prayers, do you unconsciously imagine Him in a certain direction or place? Which of the ten types of likeness is most subtle in your own mind, and what practical step will you take today to purify your concept of Allah from it?
?
The tenth type warns against attributing purposes in a human sense. Are you ever tempted to question or assume you fully know Allah's wisdom behind His decrees? How does this lesson call you to submit to His transcendence even when you don't understand?
?
If someone accused you of having a flawed understanding of Allah's oneness, would your defense be rooted in the precise negation of these ten likenesses? Write down one way you will teach this categorization to someone else to protect them from subtle shirk.

The Ruling on Divine Corporeality and the Boundary of Takfir
Al-Bajuri comments on the first type of likeness and the ruling on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between believing God is a body (jismiyya) and explicitly comparing Him to bodies (tashbīh), and why only the latter constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Believing God is a body is not kufr unless one says He is like other bodies.

In this lesson, al-Bajuri comments on the first type of likeness—being a body (jazm). He clarifies the distinction between the terms jazm and jism, and then addresses a crucial theological question: when does belief in divine corporeality become disbelief? He explains that the mere belief that God is a body does not necessarily entail tashbīh (comparison), and that only explicit comparison (like bodies) leads to kufr. This lesson also touches on the permissibility of using the term 'essence' (dhāt) for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand jazm vs jism
Al-Bajuri defines jazm as anything that fills a void, whether compound or simple, while jism is specifically compound. Note that the first type of likeness is being a jazm.
This distinction is important for the takfir ruling.
2
Learn the takfir ruling
Read the statement: 'the correct view is that the believer in corporeality does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies.' Understand why: the cause of disbelief is tashbīh, not mere corporeality belief.
This guards against excessive takfir.
3
Reflect on the term 'essence'
Al-Bajuri mentions that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (dhāt) to Allah, contrary to some views. Consider how this relates to negative theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: الشاملة للأَجْرَام والأعراض أخذاً مما بعده، والتقابل بينها وبين المخالفة للحوادث من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه على نَسَقٍ ما قبله؛ لأن نقيض المخالفة للحوادث : لا مخالفة للحوادث، وهي عينُ المماثلة للحوادث. واعلم أنَّ أنواع المماثلة عَشَرَةُ: الأول: أن يكون جزماً، الثاني: أن يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم، والثالث: أن يكون في جهةٍ، الرابع: أن يكون له هو جهةً، الخامس أن يكون في مكان السادس أن يكون في زمان، السابع: أن يكون مَحَلّاً للحوادث، الثامن: أن يكون متّصفاً بالصَّغَر التاسع أن يكون متصفاً بالكبر، العاشر : أن يكون متصفاً بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام وقد ذكرها المصنف على هذا الترتيب. فتدبر. (قوله: بأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا تصوير للمماثلة للحوادث بأنواعها العشرة المذكورة. (قوله: (جزماً) هو ما مَلَاً ،فراغاً، سواء كان مركباً أو مفرداً، بخلاف الجسم، فإنه يختص بالمركب. والصحيحُ : أن مُعْتَقِدَ الجِسْمِيَّةِ لا يَكْفُرُ إِلا إن قال: إنه جِسْم كالأجسام، فالمكفِّرُ في الحقيقة إنما هو التشبيه. (قوله: أَيْ: تَأْخُذُ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ ... إلخ) تفسير لمدخول (أن) باللازم؛ لأنه يلزمُ من كونه جزماً أخذُهُ قدراً من الفراغ واستفيد من كلامه: أنه يجوز إطلاق الذات عليه تعالى وهو الصحيح. وقيل: لا يجوز ذلك. وقيل بالوقف. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: تَضويرٌ لِلْمُمَاثَلَةِ) أي: بيان لها ببيان أسبابها، فالباء في قوله بأن يكون جزماً) سببية لا تصويرية كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: تَفْسِيرٌ لِمَدْخُولِ أَنْ بِاللَّازِم الظاهر أنه تفسير حقيقي؛ لأن الكون جرماً هو الجرمية، وحقيقة الجرمية: أخذ الذات قدراً من الفراغ.
His saying: 'and likeness (al-mumāthala) to originated things' means encompassing bodies and accidents, taken from what follows. The opposition between it and otherness (al-mukhālafa) to originated things is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, in the pattern of what preceded, because the negation of otherness to originated things is 'no otherness to originated things', and it is identical to likeness to originated things. Know that the types of likeness are ten: first: that He be a body (jazm), second: that He be an accident subsisting in a body, third: that He be in a direction, fourth: that He be a direction, fifth: that He be in a place, sixth: that He be in a time, seventh: that He be a locus for accidents, eighth: that He be described as small, ninth: that He be described as large, tenth: that He be described as having purposes in actions or judgments. The author has mentioned them in this order. So reflect. His saying: 'by His being... etc.' This is a depiction of likeness to originated things by its ten mentioned types. His saying: 'a body (jazman)' is that which fills a void, whether compound or simple, unlike the body (jism), which is specific to compound. The correct view is that the believer in corporeality (al-jismiyya) does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies; indeed, the cause of disbelief in reality is comparison (al-tashbīh). His saying: 'i.e., His exalted essence takes... etc.' is an interpretation of the object of 'an' (that) by the necessary consequence, because from His being a body necessarily follows that His essence takes up a measure of void. And it is understood from his statement that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (al-dhāt) to Him, exalted, and this is correct. Some say it is not permissible. Some say to stop. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī: His saying: 'a depiction of likeness' means an explanation of it by stating its causes. So the 'ba' (ب) in his statement 'by His being a body' is causal, not depictive, as is apparent. His saying: 'an interpretation of the object of 'an' by the necessary consequence'—apparently it is a real interpretation, because being a body is corporeality (al-jarmiyya), and the reality of corporeality is the essence taking up a measure of void.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the ten types of likeness (mumathala) to created things that are impossible for God. It begins by noting that the opposition between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa) to created things is of the same logical type as earlier: equivalence. Then it lists ten types: being a body, being an accident, being in a direction, being a direction, being in a place, being in a time, being a locus for accidents, being qualified as small, being qualified as large, and being qualified by purposes. Then al-Bajuri comments on the first type (being a body): a body (jazm) is what fills a void, whether simple or compound, whereas jism is specifically compound. He then states the correct position on takfir: a person who believes God is a body (jismiyya) is not a disbeliever unless he explicitly says God is a body like other bodies, because the actual cause of disbelief is tashbih (direct comparison). Then he explains that the phrase 'takes His essence...' is an interpretation by necessary consequence: being a body necessarily entails occupying space. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'depiction' means explanation by causes, and the 'ba' is causal. He also says that the interpretation is real because being a body is essentially the same as occupying space.

Key Terms
جزم (jazm)
جسم (jism)
تشبيه (tashbīh)
ذات (dhāt)
جرمية (jarmiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief that Allah is a body constitutes disbelief (kufr).
Correction: Al-Bajuri states that the believer in corporeality does not disbelieve unless he explicitly compares God to bodies (saying 'like bodies'). The cause of disbelief is tashbīh, not mere belief in corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, under what condition does a person who believes in divine corporeality (الجسمية) become a disbeliever?
A) If they say God is a body in any sense
B) If they say God is a body like other bodies
C) If they say God is not a body
D) If they deny divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Believing that Allah is a body does not automatically lead to kufr; disbelief occurs only when one explicitly compares Allah to created bodies (tashbīh).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about Allah in a way that implicitly compares Him to creation? What does this passage teach you about the gravity of such speech, and how will you guard your tongue from today onward?
?
The passage rules that takfir is only for explicit tashbih, not for holding a belief in corporeality. How does this affect your judgment of other Muslims who may express anthropomorphic ideas about Allah? Will you now be more cautious before accusing anyone of disbelief?
?
Your concept of Allah affects your entire deen. Is there any subtle resemblance to creation in your understanding of His attributes? What concrete step will you take this week to purify your tawhid from even the hint of tashbih?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Impossibility of Allah being described with accidents
Sanusi negates temporal accidents from Allah's essence with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be described with temporal accidents and how this principle is fundamental to the Sunni doctrine of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal; He cannot acquire new ones.

In this concise passage, Sanusi lays down an essential principle of Sunni theology: Allah's essence is not subject to temporal change. He cannot be described with accidents—new, originated qualities that come into being. To illustrate, he gives examples of a new power, new will, or new knowledge. This doctrine safeguards the belief in Allah's perfection and eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its English translation. Notice how the text uses the term 'ḥawādith' (accidents) and gives specific examples.
2
Understand the concept of 'ḥawādith'
Reflect on what it means for something to be an accident—a quality that arises in time. Contrast this with essential attributes of Allah.
In kalam, 'ḥawādith' refer to temporal events or attributes that are created.
3
Apply the principle
Ask yourself: why can't Allah have a new knowledge? Because His knowledge is eternal and perfect; any new knowledge would imply deficiency or change.
This connects to the broader doctrine that Allah's attributes are not distinct from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ بِالْحَوَادِثِ) أي: كأن تتصف بقدرة حادثة، أو إرادة حادثة، أو علم حادث إلى غير ذلك.
Or that His exalted essence be qualified with accidents – i.e., such as being qualified by a new power, or a new will, or a new knowledge, and so on.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage negates from Allah that His essence be described with temporal accidents (hawadith). It gives examples: a new (i.e., originated) power, a new will, a new knowledge. Since Allah is eternal and His attributes are eternal, it is impossible for Him to acquire any new attribute that did not exist before. This is a fundamental principle in Sunni kalam: Allah's attributes are eternal, not created in time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can acquire new attributes or knowledge over time.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Allah cannot be described with any temporal accident, including new knowledge, new power, or new will. All of Allah's attributes are eternal and unchanging.

Allah's Essence Does Not Receive Temporal Events
Sanusi enumerates examples of temporal events impossible for Allah's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's essence cannot be described with temporal events and how this protects divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal; they cannot be created or acquired.

In this brief but powerful passage, Sanusi clarifies a cornerstone of Islamic theology: that Allah's essence is not a locus for temporal, created attributes. He lists examples like temporal power, will, and knowledge to emphasize that whatever originates cannot attach to the Eternal. This guards against any suggestion that Allah acquires new attributes over time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully, noting the key terms.
Pay attention to the examples Sanusi gives.
2
Identify the examples of temporal events

These examples illustrate what 'ḥawādith' means.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why Allah's essence cannot be described with any of these. Think about the nature of divine attributes.
This connects to the concept of divine immutability.

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement explaining the impossibility of Allah's essence being described with temporal events (ḥawādith). The phrase 'temporal events' refers to accidents or attributes that come into existence, such as a power that is created (ḥāditha), a will that is created, or a knowledge that is created. The implication is that Allah's essence is eternal and does not become a locus for things that originate; His attributes are all eternal, not temporally originated. This guards against any notion that Allah could acquire new attributes after having lacked them.

Allah's Actions Are Free from Purposes but Have Wisdom
Sanusi explains that Allah's actions have no purpose but have wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's actions and rulings are free from purposes (aghraḍ) yet always have wisdom (ḥikmah), and the crucial difference between these two concepts.

Core Idea
Allah acts with wisdom, not for any purpose or benefit to Himself.

The classical Sunni position holds that Allah's actions and rulings are not motivated by purposes (aghraḍ) that would imply need or deficiency. However, this does not mean they are random or without wisdom. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the distinction between purpose and wisdom, addresses a potential scriptural objection, and explains that Allah's actions always have wisdom even if we do not perceive it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the text's own terms and rhythm sink in.
2
Identify the core distinction
Focus on where the text explains the difference between gharaḍ (purpose) and ḥikmah (wisdom). Notice that purpose motivates an action, while wisdom is a beneficial outcome that does not motivate.
This is the key conceptual hook of the lesson.
3
Examine the scriptural objection
Notice how the Qur'anic verse 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' is handled. The commentator explains that the lām here indicates consequence, not purpose.
4
Understand the tree analogy
Read the analogy of planting a tree for its fruit (purpose) and getting shade (wisdom). Ensure you see how the shade corresponds to Allah's wisdom in His actions.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Consider why it is important to maintain that Allah has wisdom in His actions. Without wisdom, actions would be absurd (ʿabath), which is impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أَوْ يَتَّصِفَ بِالْأَغْرَاضِ فِي الْأَفْعَالِ) أي: كإيجاد زيد وعمرو مثلاً. :(وقوله: أَوِ الْأَحْكَام أي كإيجاب الصلاة والزكاة مثلاً، فأفعاله تعالى وأحكامُه مُنَزَّهَةٌ عن الغَرَض، ولا يَرِدُ على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُون ) (٢) لأن اللام فيه للعاقبة والصَّيْرُورة. واعلم أن أفعاله تعالى وأحكامَهُ وإن كانت مُنَزَّهَةٌ عن الغرض؛ لكن لا تخلو عن حِكْمَةٍ؛ وإن لم تَصِلْ إليها عقولنا؛ لأنها لو لم تكن لحكمة لكانت عبثاً، وهو مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى والفرق بين الغرض والحكمة أن الغرض يكون مقصوداً من الفعل أو الحكم بحيث يكون باعثاً وحاملاً عليه، والحكمة لا تكون كذلك. (قوله: لِلْعَاقِبَةِ وَالصَّيْرُورَةِ) والمعنى حينئذ وما ترتب على خلقهم مصلحة عائدة عليهم إلا عبادتهم. (قوله: وَالْحِكْمَةُ لَا تَكُونُ كَذَلِكَ مثلوها تقريباً بما لو غرس شجرة لثمرها، فثمرها غرض باعث على الغرس، وظلها يُعَدُّ حكمة، أي مصلحة مترتبة على الفعل من غير أن تكون باعثة عليه، فحكم الله تعالى بالنسبة لأفعاله وأحكامه كالظل بالنسبة للغرس.
His saying: 'or that He be described with purposes in actions' means: like bringing Zayd and Amr into existence, for example. :(His saying: 'or rulings' means like making prayer and zakah obligatory, for example. So His actions and rulings, Exalted is He, are free from purpose (gharaḍ), and the verse 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' (Qur'an 51:56) does not contradict that, because the lām in it is for consequence and outcome. Know that His actions and rulings, Exalted is He, although free from purpose, are not devoid of wisdom (ḥikmah), even if our intellects do not attain it, because if they were not for wisdom, they would be absurd, and absurdity is impossible for Him, Exalted. The difference between purpose (gharaḍ) and wisdom (ḥikmah) is that purpose is intended from the action or ruling such that it is a motive and driver for it, while wisdom is not like that. (His saying: 'for consequence and outcome') The meaning then is: what results from their creation is a benefit returning to them, only their worship. (His saying: 'And wisdom is not like that. An example is if someone plants a tree for its fruit; its fruit is a purpose that motivates the planting, and its shade is considered wisdom, i.e., a benefit resulting from the action without being a motive for it. So the wisdom of Allah, Exalted, with respect to His actions and rulings is like the shade with respect to the planting.)
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage explains the impossibility of Allah being described with purposes (aghraḍ, sg. gharaḍ) in His actions and rulings. It gives examples: creating specific individuals like Zayd and Amr, or prescribing obligations like prayer and zakah. The author states that Allah's actions and rulings are free from gharaḍ, meaning that He does not act for a motive or to achieve some benefit for Himself. To the objection that the Qur'an says 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me', he responds that the lām (li) in this verse indicates consequence (ʿāqibah) and outcome (ṣayrūrah), not purpose; the benefit of worship is for the creatures, not for Allah. The passage then distinguishes gharaḍ from ḥikmah (wisdom). Gharaḍ is a motive that drives the agent to act, while ḥikmah is a good result that follows from the action but is not the agent's motivation. Allah's actions and rulings always have wisdom, even if we do not perceive it, because acting without wisdom would be absurd (ʿabath), which is impossible for Allah. The marginal note gives the analogy of planting a tree for its fruit: the fruit is a purpose (gharaḍ) motivating the planting, while the shade is wisdom (ḥikmah)—a beneficial outcome not intended as motive. Thus, Allah's wisdom in His actions is like the shade, whereas creatures act for purposes. This preserves Allah's transcendence from being driven by needs or objectives.

Key Terms
أغراض (aghraḍ: purposes)
غَرَض (gharaḍ: purpose)
حكمة (ḥikmah: wisdom)
عبث (ʿabath: absurdity)
عاقبة (ʿāqibah: consequence)
صيرورة (ṣayrūrah: outcome)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah has no purpose, His actions must be random or pointless.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah's actions always have wisdom (ḥikmah), which is a beneficial outcome, even if not a motivating purpose. Wisdom ensures actions are not absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between gharaḍ (purpose) and ḥikmah (wisdom) according to this passage?
A) Gharaḍ is beneficial, but ḥikmah is not.
B) Gharaḍ motivates the agent, but ḥikmah is a beneficial result that does not motivate.
C) Gharaḍ applies only to humans, ḥikmah to Allah.
D) They are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions and rulings have wisdom (ḥikmah) but not purposes (aghraḍ), because purpose implies a need, while wisdom is a beneficial result that does not motivate Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own acts of worship, do you find yourself seeking a personal benefit (like reward or avoiding punishment) as a motive? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your intention so that your worship is not driven by purpose but by the sheer recognition of Allah’s worthiness? What specific change will you make this week to align your intention with the principle that Allah needs nothing from you?
?
The lesson teaches that Allah’s actions contain wisdom even when our intellects do not grasp it. Think of a trial you are facing or have faced that you struggled to understand. Write a one-sentence commitment to surrender to His wisdom without demanding to see the purpose.
?
If Allah's actions are not motivated by purposes, then He does not create or command because He needs anything. How does this reshape your understanding of why you exist? Does your life feel like it has a 'purpose' in the sense of a motive for Allah, or are you living as a recipient of His wisdom and grace? What would change if you saw every moment of your life as a manifestation of His wisdom rather than a transaction for your benefit?

Depicting the Negation of Qiyam bi-Nafs
Al-Bajuri explains how Sanusi depicts the negation of qiyam bi-nafs based on its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Bajuri explains Sanusi's depiction of the negation of qiyam bi-nafs through two scenarios and the consistency with the definition of self-subsistence.

Core Idea
The negation of self-subsistence directly mirrors its definition.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri clarifies a critical point about how Sanusi depicts the negation of God's self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafs). He shows that the depiction is directly tied to the earlier definition of self-subsistence. If self-subsistence means not needing a locus and not needing a specifier, then its negation means being an attribute subsisting in a locus and needing a specifier. Al-Bajuri also addresses potential misconceptions about the phrasing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri distinguishes between the depiction of negation and the negated itself.
The phrase 'bi an yakuna...' is key.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Sanusi depicts negation of qiyam bi-nafs as being an attribute subsisting in a locus and needing a specifier. Note that these correspond to the two parts of the definition of self-subsistence.
3
Understand the conditional consistency
Al-Bajuri explains that if self-subsistence were defined only as not needing a locus, its negation would only be needing a locus. This shows logical consistency.
4
Analyze the commentary on 'sifatan yaqumu bi-mahall'
Al-Bajuri clarifies that the qualification 'subsisting in a locus' is descriptive, not restrictive. It may involve an ellipsis of the explicative 'an'.
This prevents misinterpretation.
5
Grasp the coordination of 'or needs a specifier'
This phrase is coordinated with 'be an attribute', not with 'subsists in a locus'. Also, 'specifier' means the bringer into existence.
Understanding coordination is crucial for grammatical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: بِأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) تصوير للنفي لا للمنفي. ولما جرى المصنف فيما تقدم على تفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المحلّ، وبـ عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المخصّص كما هو اصطلاح لبعض المتكلمين، وهو المشهور؛ جَرَى هنا على تصوير عدم قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ كونه صِفَةً يقوم بمحلّ، وب: كونه يحتاج إلى مخصص. ولو جَرَى فيما تقدم على تفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ: عدم افتقاره تعالى إلى المحلّ فقط كما هو اصطلاح لبعضهم؛ لجرى هنا على تصوير عدم قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ: كونه يحتاج إلى المحلّ فقط كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: صِفَةً يَقُومُ بِمَحَلّ) تقييد الصفة بقوله: (يقوم بمحلّ) ليس للاحتراز، بل لبيان الواقع، ويحتمل أنه على حذف (أني) التفسيرية، ويكون تفسيراً باللازم لقوله: (أن يكون صفة) على نَسَق ما تقدم. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةً أي قديمة، وأما استحالة كونه صفة حادثة؛ فقد تقدم في قوله: (أو يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم). والمراد من المحلّ: الذاتُ التي يقوم بها كما يُعلم مما مر في القيام بالنفس. (قوله: أوْ يَحْتَاجَ إِلَى مُخَصّص) معطوف على قوله: (يكون صفة)، لا على قوله: (يقوم بمحلّ كما لا يخفى والمراد من المخصص: الموجد كما يعلم مما تقدم في القيام بالنفس.
His saying: 'that He be... etc.' is a depiction of the negation, not of the negated. And since the author previously proceeded to interpret His self-subsistence as His not needing a locus and His not needing a specifier, as is the terminology of some theologians and it is well-known; he proceeded here to depict the negation of His self-subsistence as His being an attribute subsisting in a locus, and as His needing a specifier. And if he had previously proceeded to interpret His self-subsistence as only His not needing a locus, as is the terminology of some others; then he would have proceeded here to depict the negation of His self-subsistence as only His needing a locus, as is apparent. His saying: 'an attribute subsisting in a locus'—the restriction of the attribute by his saying 'subsisting in a locus' is not for exclusion, but to indicate the reality; and it is possible that it is based on the deletion of the explicative 'an' (أني), and it becomes an interpretation by the necessary implication of his saying 'that He be an attribute', in line with what preceded. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'that He be an attribute' i.e., an eternal attribute; as for the impossibility of His being a temporal attribute, it has preceded in his saying: 'or He be an accident subsisting in something definite.' And what is meant by 'locus' (al-mahall) is the essence in which it subsists, as known from what has passed on self-subsistence. His saying: 'or needs a specifier' is coordinated with his saying: 'be an attribute', not with his saying: 'subsists in a locus', as is not hidden; and what is meant by 'specifier' (al-mukhasis) is the bringer into existence, as known from what has passed on self-subsistence.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of Sanusi's phrase 'bi an yakuna...' (that He be...). Al-Bajuri clarifies that this phrase depicts the negation (of qiyam bi-nafs) not the negated itself. He then explains the consistency: Sanusi had defined God's self-subsistence (qiyamuhu bi-nafsihi) as both not needing a locus (mahall) and not needing a specifier (mukhasis). Consequently, the negation of self-subsistence is depicted as being an attribute subsisting in a locus and needing a specifier. Had he defined it only as not needing a locus, the negation would be only needing a locus. Al-Bajuri then comments on 'sifatan yaqumu bi-mahall': the qualification 'subsisting in a locus' is not for exclusion but to describe the reality; possibly there is an ellipsis of the explicative 'an'. Then he includes al-Ajhuri's gloss noting that the attribute here is eternal, not temporal, and defines 'mahall' as the essence in which it subsists. Finally, he clarifies that 'or needs a specifier' is coordinated with 'be an attribute', not with 'subsists in a locus', and that 'mukhasis' means the bringer into existence (mujid).

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
تصوير النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'sifatan yaqumu bi-mahall' introduces a restriction that excludes some attributes.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that the qualification is merely descriptive, indicating the reality, and not for exclusion.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, what would the depiction of the negation of qiyam bi-nafs be if Sanusi had defined it only as not needing a locus?
A) Being a substance needing no locus
B) Needing only a specifier
C) Needing only a locus
D) Being an accident

Key Takeaway
The depiction of the negation of a divine attribute is directly derived from how that attribute is defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe what Allah is not (like not needing a locus), does that description truly arise from a deep understanding of what He is (self-subsistent), or is it just a phrase you repeat? This lesson shows that even the negation must be carefully derived. How will you ensure that your affirmations about Allah are just as precise?
?
If the negation of self-subsistence is depicted as needing a locus and a specifier, then consider: what does affirming Allah's self-subsistence demand from you in terms of relying on Him alone? Are there areas in your life where you act as if you need a 'locus' or 'specifier' other than Allah?
?
The scholars debated the precise definition of qiyam bi-nafs and its negation. Does your own faith have that level of exactness, or are you content with 'Allah is independent' without understanding what that truly entails? Commit today to learn the precise meanings of Allah's attributes, even if it takes effort.

The Six Quanta Negated by Oneness
Al-Bajuri enumerates six quanta negated by tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six types of quantity (continuous and discrete) that the attribute of oneness negates in Allah's essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
Oneness negates six kinds of quantity—not just number, but composition too.

Al-Bajuri explains that the attribute of oneness (wahdaniyya) negates not only numerical plurality but also any composition or divisibility in Allah. He lists six quanta that are negated: continuous and discrete in essence, continuous and discrete in attributes, and discrete and continuous in actions (with a nuance). This foundational concept clarifies what it means for Allah to be absolutely one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains
The negated quanta are distributed across three domains: essence (dhat), attributes (sifat), and actions (af‘al). Note how each domain can have two types of quantity.
Essence and attributes each have both continuous and discrete; actions have discrete plus continuous under one condition.
2
Distinguish continuous and discrete
Continuous quantity (kamm muttasil) refers to connected parts (e.g., composition), while discrete quantity (kamm munfasil) refers to separate parts (e.g., numerical multiplicity). Both are negated by oneness.
3
Observe the conditional for actions
Continuous quantum in actions is negated only if it implies participation with another; if it merely means multiple actions, it is affirmed (not negated). This nuance is important.
Tawhid does not negate the multiplicity of Allah's actions themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أنْ لا يَكُونَ وَاحِداً) أي: في ذاته أو صفاته أو من قوله بأن يكون... إلخ). والتقابل بين ذلك وبين الوحدانية من أفعاله أخذاً التقابل بين الشيء ونقيضه كما لا يخفى. ودخل تحت قوله: (أن لا يكون واحداً) جميع الكموم المنفية وهي الكم المتصل في الذات والكم المنفصل فيها، والكم المتصل في الصفات، والكم المنفصل فيها، والكم المنفصل في الأفعال، وكذا الكم المتصل فيها إن صور بمشاركة غيره تعالى له في فعل من الأفعال بخلاف ما لو صور بتعدد أفعاله تعالى فإنه ثابت لا منفي. إذا علمت ذلك علمت أن في قوله: بأن يكون.. إلخ) قصوراً؛ لأنه إنما ذكر فيه: الكم المتصل في الذات والكم المنفصل فيها، والكم المنفصل في الصفات، والكم المنفصل في الأفعال، وكذا الكم المتصل فيها على ما تقدم، ولم يذكر الكم المتصل في الصفات ويُمكن أن يُجعل كلامه شاملاً لذلك أيضاً بأن يُجعل قوله: أو صفاته معطوفاً على (ذاته) في الموضعين، أو يُجْعَلَ من باب الحذف من الأول لدلالة الثاني، والتقدير بأن يكون مركباً في ذاته أو صفاته.... تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. قوله: مَعْطُوفاً عَلَى: ذَاتِ فِي الْمَوْضِعَيْنِ) لعل المعنى: أنه معطوف على الأَحَدِ الدائر فبالنظر لعطفه على ذات الثانية يُسْتَفَادُ منه : نَفْي الكمّ المنفصل في الصفات، وبالنظر لعطفه على ذات الأولى يستفاد منه : نفي الكمّ المتصل فيها، فيُعْلَمُ من مجموع الأمرين: نَفْي الكمين، وليس مرادُهُ: أن العطف على ذات في الموضعين معاً؛ إذ لا يُعْطَفُ شيء واحد على شيئين، وعلى هذا لا خلاف في الكلام بخلاف الوجه الذي بعده. أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته أو صفاته... إلخ. والحاصل أن الكموم سنةً، وكلُّها مَنْفِيَّةٌ بالوحدانية على ما تقدم في الكمّ المتصل في الأفعال. فتنبه. قوله: بأنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) تصوير للنفي لا للمنفي كما تقدم نظيره.
His saying: 'And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He not be one,' i.e., in His essence or His attributes or from His saying 'that He be... etc.' And the opposition between that and oneness from His actions is taken as the opposition between a thing and its opposite, as is not hidden. And under His saying 'that He not be one' include all the negated quanta, which are: the continuous quantum in the essence, the discrete quantum in it, the continuous quantum in the attributes, the discrete quantum in them, the discrete quantum in the actions, and likewise the continuous quantum in them if conceived by the participation of other than Him, exalted, with Him in an action, contrary to if it is conceived by the multiplicity of His actions, for it is affirmed, not negated. If you know that, you know that in his saying 'that He be... etc.' there is deficiency; because he only mentioned therein: the continuous quantum in the essence, the discrete quantum in it, the discrete quantum in the attributes, the discrete quantum in the actions, and likewise the continuous quantum in them as aforementioned, and he did not mention the continuous quantum in the attributes. It is possible to make his statement inclusive of that as well by making his saying 'or His attributes' conjoined to 'essence' in both places, or by making it from the category of deletion from the first due to the indication of the second, with the appreciation: that He be composite in His essence or His attributes... Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying 'conjoined to essence in both places' – perhaps the meaning is that it is conjoined to the one that is operating; for by virtue of its conjunction to the second essence, negation of the discrete quantum in the attributes is derived, and by virtue of its conjunction to the first essence, negation of the continuous quantum in them is derived, so from the total of the two matters the negation of the two quanta is known, and it is not his intention that the conjunction is to essence in both places simultaneously, because one thing cannot be conjoined to two things. On this there is no disagreement in the speech, unlike the subsequent aspect. 'Or there be for Him a similitude in His essence or His attributes... etc.' The summary is that the quanta are six, all negated by oneness as previously mentioned regarding the continuous quantum in the actions. So pay attention. His saying: 'that He be... etc.' is a depiction of the negation, not of the negated, as its counterpart previously occurred.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary explains the exhaustive negation of all types of quantity (kumum) by the divine attribute of oneness (wahdaniyya). It begins by clarifying that 'not being one' means not being one in essence, attributes, or actions. The author then states that under this negation fall six quanta: continuous and discrete in essence, continuous and discrete in attributes, discrete in actions, and continuous in actions (if conceived as sharing an action with another). He notes a deficiency in al-Sanusi's original wording, which omitted the continuous quantum in the attributes. Al-Bajuri offers two solutions: either treat 'or attributes' as conjoined to 'essence' in both occurrences, or consider it an ellipsis. Al-Anbabi's gloss further explains the first solution, showing how it covers both quanta in attributes. The passage concludes by reaffirming that all six quanta are negated by oneness, and that the phrase 'that He be...' depicts the negation itself, not the negated thing.

Key Terms
وحدانية
كم متصل
كم منفصل
ذات
صفات
أفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Oneness only negates numerical plurality (discrete quantity).
Correction: Oneness also negates continuous quantity (composition and parts) in essence, attributes, and actions.

Checkpoint
How many types of quantity (kumum) does al-Bajuri state are negated by the attribute of oneness?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The attribute of oneness negates all six types of quantity—continuous and discrete—across essence, attributes, and actions, ensuring absolute divine simplicity and uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever conceptually partitioned God's essence or attributes in a way that implies composition—even subconsciously? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your understanding of divine oneness?
?
In your daily life, do you ever attribute to yourself or others a shared role in God's exclusive actions, such as ultimate control over outcomes? What concrete step can you take today to affirm that only Allah is truly effective?
?
When you reflect on the multiplicity of God's actions—creating, sustaining, forgiving—do you ever think of them as separate 'parts' of His power? How can you cultivate a sense of awe at His absolute unity while still marveling at the diversity of His acts?

Resolving the Missing Continuous Quantum in Attributes
Al-Bajuri identifies and resolves a textual omission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri resolves the apparent omission of the continuous quantum in attributes from al-Sanusi's text.

Core Idea
A careful reading of al-Sanusi's phrase covers all six quanta through either conjunction or ellipsis.

Al-Bajuri points out that al-Sanusi's original statement omitted the continuous quantum in attributes. He then provides two interpretive solutions that show the text actually includes it. This highlights the precision of kalam texts and the importance of careful reading.

Guided Path
1
Notice the omission
Al-Bajuri states that al-Sanusi mentioned five quanta: continuous and discrete in essence, discrete in attributes, discrete and continuous in actions—but omitted continuous in attributes.
2
First solution: conjunction
Treat 'or His attributes' as conjoined to 'essence' in both occurrences. This way, the phrase 'in His essence or His attributes' implicitly negates both continuous and discrete in attributes.
Al-Anbabi clarifies that this requires careful grammatical interpretation.
3
Second solution: ellipsis
Consider the first part as deleted, with the second part indicating what is omitted. The full meaning would be 'that He be composite in His essence or attributes.'

Incapacity as the Opposite of Power: Sunni vs Mu'tazili
Al-Bajuri begins discussion of incapacity as opposite of power, contrasting Sunni and Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Sunni and Mu'tazili views on the attribute of incapacity and why this distinction matters for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Incapacity is not just lack of power; Sunni theology affirms it as an existent opposite.

Al-Bajuri introduces the discussion of the opposites of the attributes of meanings, starting with incapacity. He highlights a key theological disagreement: are incapacity and power true contraries, or is incapacity merely the absence of power? This passage sets the stage for understanding how Sunni theology grounds divine attributes in real existent qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms used.
The passage is short but packed with theological nuance.
2
Identify the two schools' definitions
Locate the Sunni definition of incapacity as an existent thing that opposes power, and the Mu'tazili definition as the mere absence of power. Underline each.
The difference is ontological: existent vs. non-existent.
3
Understand the two types of opposition
Note that the Sunni view involves 'opposition of contraries' (ḍiddān), while the Mu'tazili view involves 'opposition of privation and possession' (al-ʿadam wa al-milka).
This distinction reflects deeper metaphysical commitments.
4
Consider the evidence mentioned
The passage says 'they supported the first [view] in the witnessed [world].' Reflect on what this means: Sunni arguments from observable reality (e.g., life and death as contraries).
Though the passage cuts off, the reference to shāhid (the witnessed world) is a common kalam method.
5
Examine a common misunderstanding
Many assume incapacity is simply the absence of power (the Mu'tazili view). But for Sunnis, it is a real attribute. Reflect on why this matters for Allah's perfection.
If incapacity were just a lack, then its impossibility in Allah would be trivial. The Sunni view makes it a stronger impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْعَجْزُ) هذا شروع في أضداد صفات المعاني. والتقابل بين العجز والقُدْرَة من تقابل الضّدين عند أهل السنة، ومن تقابل العَدَم والمَلَكة عند المعتزلة؛ لأن العجز عند أهل السنة: أمرٌ وجودِي يُضَاد القدرة وعند المعتزلة: عدم القدرة عما من شأنه أن يكون قادراً. ووجهوا الأول في الشاهد
His saying: 'And likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be incapable (al-‘ajz)' – this is the beginning of the opposites of the attributes of meanings. And the opposition between incapacity and power is from the opposition of contraries according to Ahl al-Sunna, and from the opposition of privation and possession according to the Mu‘tazila; because incapacity according to Ahl al-Sunna is an existent thing that opposes power, and according to the Mu‘tazila is the non-existence of power in that which is supposed to be powerful. And they supported the first [view] in the witnessed [world].
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This short passage begins the discussion of the opposites of the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma‘ani). Specifically, it addresses the attribute of incapacity (al-‘ajz), which is the opposite of power (al-qudra). Al-Bajuri explains that the relation between incapacity and power is two different types of opposition depending on the school. According to Ahl al-Sunna, they are true contraries (ḍiddān): both are existent realities that oppose each other. According to the Mu‘tazila, the relation is that of privation and possession (al-‘adam wa al-milka): incapacity is simply the absence of power in something that could potentially be powerful. The passage then notes that the Sunni view is supported by evidence from the witnessed world, though the text cuts off there. This distinction is important for understanding the nature of divine attributes: for Ahl al-Sunna, incapacity is not a mere lack but a real quality, and since it cannot exist in Allah, it is impossible for Him.

Key Terms
عجز
قدرة
ضدان
عدم وملكة
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think incapacity is simply the absence of power (as Muʿtazila hold), and that this is the obvious view.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm that incapacity is an existent quality opposite to power. The passage clarifies that the Sunni position is grounded in reason and observed reality, making it a stronger theological claim about Allah's perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, how is the opposition between incapacity and power classified?
A) Opposition of privation and possession
B) Opposition of contraries
C) Opposition of relation
D) Opposition of contradiction

Key Takeaway
Incapacity is a real, existent attribute opposite to power according to Sunni theology, not just a lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm 'Allah is All-Powerful' in your daily prayers, do you truly sense that His power is a real, positive attribute that has no shadow of weakness? Or does 'power' sometimes feel like a mere label to you? Today, as you say 'Allahu Akbar', pause and actively remind yourself: God's power is absolutely perfect, with no possibility of its opposite ever touching Him. How can you make this conviction a living reality in your worship?
?
The Mu'tazila viewed incapacity as a simple absence—nothing real. Have you ever treated God's attributes as mere concepts, thinking 'He is powerful' means only 'He is not weak'? The Sunni tradition demands we see His attributes as true, distinct, existent perfections. Search your heart: do you relate to God as a Being with real, positive qualities, or do you reduce Him to a collection of negations? What change in your understanding does this lesson call you to make today?
?
This lesson shows two different ways of thinking about God. Perhaps you have never questioned whether your own beliefs align with Ahl al-Sunna or the Mu'tazila on this subtle point. But theology shapes worship. Ask yourself: if incapacity is merely absence, then God's power might be seen as a default state, not a glorious perfection. If it is a real opposite, then God's power shines as a deliberate, infinite quality. Which view makes your heart tremble more in awe? And are you willing to embrace the Sunni view even if it requires deeper thought?

The Nature of Incapacity: Sunni vs. Mu'tazili View
Al-Bajuri defines 'ajz and explains the disagreement on its nature between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Sunni and Mu'tazili definitions of incapacity ('ajz) and the rational proof used to support the Sunni position.

Core Idea
Incapacity is not just lack of power—it is a real, positive attribute according to Ahl al-Sunna.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary tackles a fundamental disagreement in Islamic theology: is incapacity a real thing or just the absence of power? The answer shapes how we understand God's attributes. Al-Bajuri defines 'ajz and presents an argument from everyday experience to support the Sunni view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'ajz, qudra, taqabul al-diddayn, taqabul al-'adam wa al-milk.
The word 'القُذرة' in the OCR is likely 'القدرة' (power).
2
Identify the two views on 'ajz
The passage contrasts Ahl al-Sunna (who say 'ajz is an existent opposite of power) with the Mu'tazila (who say it is the absence of power in something that should be capable). Underline these definitions.
3
Examine the proof from the witnessed world
Al-Bajuri gives an example: an old man (al-zaman) cannot stand, and a person prevented from standing also cannot stand. Both lack the ability, but the old man has an additional existent hindrance. This shows 'ajz is a real attribute.
Understand how the Sunni view explains the difference between the two cases.
4
Compare with the Mu'tazili explanation
The Mu'tazila explain the same two cases by saying the old man lacks power entirely, while the prevented person has power but is externally constrained. This shows their view that 'ajz is simply absence of power.
Notice the contrast: Sunni sees a positive hindrance; Mu'tazila sees just absence of power.
5
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this matters: if 'ajz is a real attribute, then God cannot have it because He is perfect. If it is just absence of power, then its impossibility on God is about logical consistency. This connects to the doctrine of divine attributes.
The passage begins by stating that 'ajz is impossible for God, confirming His absolute power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْعَجْنُ هذا شروع في أضداد صفات المعاني. والتقابل بين العجز والقُذرة من تقابل الضّدين عند أهل السنة، ومن تقابل العَدَم والمَلَكة عند المعتزلة؛ لأن العجز عند أهل السنة: أمرٌ وجودِي يُضَاد القدرة وعند المعتزلة: عدم القدرة عما من شأنه أن يكون قادراً. ووجهوا الأول في الشاهد أعني الحادث - بأن في الزمن معنى لا يوجَدُ في الممنوع من القيام مع اشتراكهما في عدم التمكن منه. (قوله: بأَنَّ فِي الزَّمَن مَعْنى أي وجودياً كثقل أعضائه، فالفرق بينهما عند السنة: أن الزمن فيه معنى وجودي يمنعه من القيام بخلاف الممنوع من القيام، فهو خال عن ذلك المانع، وفرقت المعتزلة بينهما بانتفاء القدرة عن الأول دون الثاني.
His statement: 'and likewise incapacity ('ajz) is impossible for Him, exalted'—this is the beginning of [discussion of] the opposites of the attributes of meanings. And the opposition between incapacity and power is of the opposition of two contraries according to Ahl al-Sunna, and of the opposition of privation and possession according to the Mu'tazila; because incapacity according to Ahl al-Sunna is an existent thing that opposes power, and according to the Mu'tazila is the absence of power from that which is apt to be powerful. And they justified the first view in the witnessed [realm], meaning the contingent, by [saying] that in the aged there is a meaning not found in the one prevented from standing, despite their sharing in the lack of ability to do it. (His statement: 'that in the aged there is a meaning'—i.e., an existent [meaning] like the weight of his limbs; so the difference between them according to the Sunna is that the aged has an existent meaning that prevents him from standing, unlike the one prevented from standing, for he is free of that hindrance; and the Mu'tazila distinguished between them by the absence of power in the first but not in the second.)
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the opposites of the attributes of meanings, starting with 'ajz (incapacity) as the opposite of qudra (power). The author explains that the opposition between 'ajz and qudra is considered by Ahl al-Sunna as the opposition of two contraries (diddayn), meaning both are real, positive attributes. In contrast, the Mu'tazila consider it as the opposition of privation and possession (adams and milk), where 'ajz is simply the absence of power in something that should be capable. To support the Sunni view, a proof is offered from the observable world: an old man (al-zaman) is unable to stand, while a person who is simply prevented from standing (al-mamnu‘ min al-qiyam) also cannot stand. Both lack the ability, but the old man has an additional existent quality (like heaviness of limbs) that hinders him, which is the positive reality of 'ajz. Thus, the difference is that in the aged there is an existent hindrance, while in the prevented person there is no such hindrance. The Mu'tazila, however, explain the difference by saying the old man lacks power (interest) whereas the prevented person has power but is externally constrained.

Key Terms
العجز
القدرة
أضداد صفات المعاني
تقابل الضدين
تقابل العدم والملكة
الزمن
الممنوع من القيام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity is simply the lack of power, and there is no real attribute of incapacity.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, incapacity ('ajz) is a real, existent attribute that opposes power. The example of the aged vs. prevented person demonstrates that incapacity involves a positive hindrance, not just absence of power.

Checkpoint
How do Ahl al-Sunna view the opposition between 'ajz (incapacity) and qudra (power)?
A) As opposition of privation and possession.
B) As opposition of two contraries (diddain).
C) As opposition of existence and non-existence.
D) As opposition of correlation and simultaneity.

Key Takeaway
Incapacity, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is a real, positive attribute that opposes power, as evidenced by the example of the aged person who has an existent hindrance to standing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely free from incapacity, what does that mean for your own reliance on Him? Is there any situation you are facing where you are acting as if God is incapable of helping you? Repent and put your trust in the All-Powerful.
?
The aged person could not stand due to a real, existent hindrance. Look at your own life: are you using your weaknesses as real, existent excuses to disobey God, or are you like the prevented person who actually has power but is choosing not to use it? Be honest: what is truly preventing you from standing in prayer, giving charity, or leaving a sin?
?
The Mu'tazila treat incapacity as a mere absence—something not real. Are there sins you downplay as 'just a lapse' when, in fact, they represent a real and dangerous condition in your heart? Confess to God that your spiritual incapacity is real and ask Him to replace it with true power through His grace.

Al-Ghazali's Statement and Al-Biqa'i's Objection
Al-Bajuri explains and refutes al-Biqa'i's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-Ghazali's famous statement and how al-Bajuri resolves al-Biqa'i's objection by distinguishing between dispositional and actual divine power.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's statement does not deny God's power, only actual creation of a better world due to His will.

Al-Ghazali's statement 'There is nothing more wondrous than what is' has sparked debate. Al-Biqa'i objected that it implies God's incapacity. In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies the intended meaning and defends al-Ghazali by distinguishing between what God could do in principle and what He actually does.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'imkan'
Al-Bajuri first explains that 'imkan' in al-Ghazali's statement refers to God's power, not possibility. Note the two possible meanings and their implications.
The term is ambiguous, so clarifying it is key.
2
Analyze al-Biqa'i's objection
The objection arises only if 'imkan' is taken as 'ability'. If taken as the opposite of impossibility, the objection changes. Grasp the difference.
Al-Biqa'i's criticism is based on a specific interpretation.
3
Learn the resolution
Al-Bajuri answers that al-Ghazali negates the actual (tanjizi) attachment of power, not the dispositional (saluhi). The non-existence is due to God's knowledge and will, not lack of power.
This avoids attributing incapacity to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لَيْسَ فِي الْإِمْكَانِ أي إمكان الله أي تمكنه بمعنى: اقتداره، كأنه قال: ليس في اقتدار الله عالم أبدع من هذا العالم، وليس المراد بالإمكان: ما قبل الوجوب والاستحالة؛ لأن معنى حينئذ ليس من الممكنات، بل هو من المستحيلات، وهذا ليس فيه نسبة العجز إلى الله قطعاً، فاعتراض البقاعي (۱) عليه بأن فيه نسبة العجز إلى الله لا يتم إلا إذا جعل الإمكان بمعنى الاقتداء بخلاف ما إذا جعل مقابلاً للوجوب والاستحالة، فإنه يعترض عليه بكونه مخالفاً للواقع، لا بأن فيه نسبة عجز. ثم إن اعتراض البقاعي عليه مبني على أن مراده نفي تعلق القدرة الصلوحي. وحاصل جوابه أن الغزالي لم يرد نفي التعلق الصلوحي، وإنما أراد نفي التعلق التنجيزي بعالم أبدع من هذا العالم لتعلق علم الله وإرادته بعدم وجوده، وإنما حملنا الجواب على ذلك؛ لأن تعلق العلم والإرادة بعدم الشيء إنما يمنع من التعلق التنجيزي لا من التعلق الصلوحي. (۱) البقاعي إبراهيم بن عمر برهان الدين أبو الحسن شافعي مفسر له: "نظم الدرر" و"تهديم الأركان من ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان"، توفي سنة (٨٨٥هـ).
His statement: 'There is not in al-imkan'—i.e., God's imkan, meaning His ability (tamakkun) in the sense of His power, as if He said: There is not in God's power a world more wondrous than this world. And what is meant by al-imkan is not that which is prior to obligation and impossibility; because its meaning then would be 'it is not among the possibles, rather it is among the impossibles', and this involves no attribution of incapacity to God at all. So the objection of al-Biqa'i (1) against him, that it contains attribution of incapacity to God, is only valid if al-imkan is understood in the sense of al-iqtida' (ability), contrary to if it is understood as the opposite of obligation and impossibility; for then it would be objected to as being contrary to reality, not as containing attribution of incapacity. Moreover, al-Biqa'i's objection is based on [the assumption] that his intention is to negate the dispositional (saluhi) attachment of power. The gist of his answer is that al-Ghazali did not intend to negate the dispositional attachment, but rather he intended to negate the actual (tanjizi) attachment [of power] to a world more wondrous than this world, because of the attachment of God's knowledge and will to its non-existence. And we have only interpreted the answer in this way because the attachment of knowledge and will to the non-existence of a thing only prevents the actual attachment, not the dispositional attachment. (1) Al-Biqa'i: Ibrahim ibn 'Umar Burhan al-Din Abu al-Hasan al-Shafi'i, a mufassir; he has "Nazm al-Durar" and "Tahdim al-Arkan min laysa fi al-imkan abda' mimma kan", died in 885 AH.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage discusses the famous statement of al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing better [literally: more wondrous] in possibility than what has occurred' (laysa fi al-imkan abda' mimma kan). Al-Bajuri explains that 'imkan' here refers to God's ability (qudra), i.e., His power, not possibility as opposed to impossibility. Thus, al-Ghazali means that in God's power there is no world more wondrous than this world. He clarifies that if 'imkan' were taken as the opposite of obligation (wujub) and impossibility (istihala), then the statement would mean that a more wondrous world is impossible, which would not attribute incapacity to God but would be contrary to reality. Therefore, al-Biqa'i's objection that the statement implies God's incapacity is invalid unless 'imkan' is interpreted as 'ability'. The answer to al-Biqa'i is that al-Ghazali's intended meaning is the negation of the actual (tanjizi) attachment of divine power to a more wondrous world, because God's knowledge and will are attached to its non-existence. The dispositional (saluhi) attachment remains; i.e., God could create a more wondrous world in principle, but He does not due to His knowledge and will. Thus, no incapacity is implied. The footnote identifies al-Biqa'i as a Shafi'i scholar and author of works on this topic.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الاقتداء
التعلق الصلوحي
التعلق التنجيزي
علم الله
إرادة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghazali's statement means that God could not create a better world, implying God is not all-powerful.
Correction: Al-Bajuri explains that al-Ghazali only denies the actual creation of a better world due to His will and knowledge, not His ability. God has the power (dispositional attachment) but does not exercise it (actual attachment) for reasons known to Him.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what does al-Ghazali mean by 'laysa fi al-imkan abda' mimma kan'?
A) There is no possibility of a world more wondrous than this one.
B) God does not actually create a world more wondrous than this one due to His knowledge and will.
C) God is incapable of creating a better world.
D) This world is the best possible world in terms of goodness.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's statement does not limit God's power; it affirms His will and knowledge as the reason why this specific world exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation you wish were different—like a hardship or missed opportunity—do you ever doubt that God's power could have made it easier for you? How does this lesson's distinction between what God can do (dispositional power) and what He actually does (actual attachment) challenge you to trust His wisdom more than your own desires? What is one disappointment you will consciously surrender to His will this week?
?
If you truly believed that God's will is not a limitation but a perfect expression of His wisdom, how would your reaction to unanswered prayers change? Think of a specific prayer you've made that hasn't been answered. Can you say from the heart: 'God, You have the power to grant this, but You chose not to, and I accept that as better for me'? If not, what holds you back?
