Understanding al-Ghazali's Statement and al-Biqa'i's Objection
Explains al-Ghazali's statement and refutes al-Biqa'i's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of al-Ghazali's phrase 'ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان' and why al-Biqa'i's objection is refuted through the distinction between actual and potential divine power.

Core Idea
al-Ghazali denies actual creation of a better world, not God's potential power.

Al-Ghazali's famous statement is often misunderstood as denying God's ability to create a better world. Al-Biqa'i objected that this implies impotence. In this lesson, we examine the original text and the scholar's commentary to clarify the intended meaning, distinguishing between the two types of divine power attachment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the two possible interpretations of 'الإمكان' mentioned early on.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'الإمكان'
The passage explains that 'الإمكان' can mean either divine power (اقتدار) or contingency (ما قبل الوجوب والاستحالة). Understand how each leads to a different objection.
If it means power, the objection is about impotence; if it means contingency, the objection relates to reality.
3
Understand the objection and the answer
Al-Biqa'i's objection assumes al-Ghazali denied potential power (تعلق صلوحي). The answer clarifies that al-Ghazali only denied actual creation (تعلق تنجيزي) due to God's will.
The key distinction is between potential and actual power attachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لَيْسَ فِي الْإِمْكَانِ أي إمكان الله أي تمكنه بمعنى: اقتداره، كأنه قال: ليس في اقتدار الله عالم أبدع من هذا العالم، وليس المراد بالإمكان: ما قبل الوجوب والاستحالة؛ لأن معنى حينئذ ليس من الممكنات، بل هو من المستحيلات، وهذا ليس فيه نسبة العجز إلى الله قطعاً، فاعتراض البقاعي عليه بأن فيه نسبة العجز إلى الله لا يتم إلا إذا جعل الإمكان بمعنى الاقتدار بخلاف ما إذا جعل مقابلاً للوجوب والاستحالة، فإنه يعترض عليه بكونه مخالفاً للواقع، لا بأن فيه نسبة عجز. ثم إن اعتراض البقاعي عليه مبني على أن مراده نفي تعلق القدرة الصلوحي. وحاصل جوابه أن الغزالي لم يرد نفي التعلق الصلوحي، وإنما أراد نفي التعلق التنجيزي بعالم أبدع من هذا العالم لتعلق علم الله وإرادته بعدم وجوده، وإنما حملنا الجواب على ذلك؛ لأن تعلق العلم والإرادة بعدم الشيء إنما يمنع من التعلق التنجيزي لا من التعلق الصلوحي. إنه لا يمكن أن يوجَدُ أبدعُ من هذا العالم لعدم تعلق قدرة الله وإرادته بإيجاده، ولو شاء الله تعالى لأَوْجَدَ أبدع منه، فليس في كلامه ما يقتضي نسبة العجز إليه تعالى كما توهمه البقاعي، فاعترض عليه.
His statement: 'There is not in possibility' i.e., the possibility of God, i.e., His capability in the sense of His power. It is as if he said: There is not in God's power a world more perfect than this world. And what is meant by 'possibility' is not that which precedes obligation and impossibility, because the meaning then would be that it is not among possible things, rather it is among impossibilities. And this does not entail attributing impotence to God at all. So al-Biqa'i's objection against him that it entails attributing impotence to God is not sustained unless possibility is taken in the sense of power, contrary to when it is taken as opposite of obligation and impossibility, for then the objection would be that it contradicts reality, not that it entails attributing impotence. Then, al-Biqa'i's objection against him is based on the assumption that he intended to deny the potential (suluhi) attachment of power. The essence of the answer is that al-Ghazali did not intend to deny potential attachment, but rather he intended to deny the existential (tanzizi) attachment of a world more perfect than this world, because God's knowledge and will are attached to its non-existence. And we interpreted the answer in that way because the attachment of knowledge and will to the non-existence of a thing only prevents existential attachment, not potential attachment. Indeed, it is impossible that a more perfect world than this world should exist, because of the non-attachment of God's power and will to its creation. And if God willed, He would have created a more perfect one. So there is nothing in his statement that entails attributing impotence to God, as al-Biqa'i imagined. So he objected to him.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Ghazali's famous statement 'There is nothing more perfect in the realm of possibility than what exists.' The commentator explains that 'الإمكان' here refers to divine power (اقتدار), not possibility in the sense of contingency (ما قبل الوجوب والاستحالة). Thus, al-Ghazali means that God's power does not extend to creating a world more perfect than this one in terms of actual creation (تعلق تنجيزي), but it does extend potentially (تعلق صلوحي). Al-Biqa'i's objection that this implies impotence is based on misunderstanding; al-Ghazali only denies actual creation of a better world due to God's knowledge and will, not potential power. Therefore, no attribution of impotence is intended.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الاقتدار
التعلق التنجيزي
التعلق الصلوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghazali denied that God has the power to create a better world than this one.
Correction: Al-Ghazali only denied that God actually created a better world in this existence; God retains the potential power to do so.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does 'ليس في الإمكان' mean in al-Ghazali's statement?
A) Not possible in the sense of contingency
B) Not within God's power in the sense of potential power
C) Not within God's power in the sense of actual creation
D) It means that the world is the best possible world

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's statement does not imply divine impotence; it only denies actual creation of a more perfect world due to God's will, while affirming potential power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the imperfections and suffering in the world, do you ever struggle to believe that God could have created a better one? How does al-Ghazali's distinction between potential and actual power confront that struggle? Will you now trust that God's will, not His ability, determines what exists?
?
Al-Ghazali says if God willed, He would create a more perfect world. Have you ever felt that God 'could have' given you something better than what you have? How does this lesson challenge you to accept that His will, not a lack of power, is behind your circumstances? Are you ready to submit to that will even when it feels less than ideal?
?
Reflect on a time you questioned God's power because of a difficult situation. After reading this passage, do you see that the issue is not power but divine wisdom and will? What practical step can you take this week to strengthen your trust in God's decisions over your life?

Ruling on Saying 'God Cannot Take Me Out of His Kingdom'
Q&A on a statement about divine power with a refinement on phrasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying divine power over an impossibility is not disbelief and how to phrase such statements more precisely.

Core Idea
Negating divine power over impossibles is not disbelief, but precise wording matters.

This lesson examines a specific theological question: if someone says 'God cannot take me out of His kingdom,' does that constitute disbelief? The answer reveals a key principle about divine power and impossibilities, and also teaches us how to phrase such statements more precisely to avoid any suggestion of impotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the complete argument.
Notice the structure: a question, an answer with reasoning, then a refinement.
2
Understand the reasoning
Identify why the scholar ruled it not disbelief: the act is impossible, and divine power does not relate to impossibles. Also note the example of 'cannot take a son or wife.'
The key principle is that power is only relevant to possible actions.
3
Learn the preferred phrasing
The passage advises against using 'لا يقدر' (cannot) because it suggests impotence. Instead, say 'لا تتعلق قدرته' (His power does not relate to it) since denying a function is more accurate.
This distinction protects divine transcendence and avoids misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسُئِلَ بعضهم عمن قال: لا يَقْدِرُ الله أن يُخْرجني من مملكته، هل يَكْفُرُ أو لا؟ فأجاب بأنه لا يَكْفُرُ؛ لأن خروجه من مملكته تعالى مستحيل لعدم إمكان وجود مملكة لغيره يُخْرِجُه إليها، والقدرة لا تتعلَّق بالمستحيل فلا ضير في ذلك، كما لا ضَيْرَ في أن يقال: لا يقدر الله على أن يتخذ ولداً أو زوجةً أو نحو ذلك. (قوله: وَإِيجَادِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ... إلخ) لم يقل: وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى إيجاد شيء من العالم... إلخ كما فعل في غيره؛ لعدم طُولِ الكلام على ما قبله ولا يخفى أن المقابل للإرادة: إنما هو الكراهية وما عُطِفَ عليها على ما يأتي. ينبغي أن لا يقال: لا يَقْدِرُ على أن يتَّخِذَ ولداً مثلاً لإيهامه العجز؛ إذ لا يثبت الشيء للشيء أو يُنفى عنه إلا إذا كان من وظيفته، بل يقال: لا تتعلق قُدْرَتُه تعالى باتخاذ الولد مثلاً؛ لكونه ليس من وظيفتها.
Someone was asked about one who says: 'God cannot take me out of His kingdom' – does he become a disbeliever or not? He answered that he does not become a disbeliever, because his leaving the kingdom of God – exalted is He – is impossible, due to the impossibility of the existence of a kingdom belonging to someone else to which He could take him out. And power does not attach to the impossible, so there is no harm in that, just as there is no harm in saying: 'God cannot take a son or a wife or the like.' His statement: 'AND THE CREATION OF SOMETHING FROM THE WORLD... etc.' He did not say: 'And likewise it is impossible for Him – exalted is He – to create something from the world... etc.' as He did regarding other things, in order to avoid prolixity of speech on what preceded it. And it is not hidden that the counterpart of will is only dislike, and what is conjoined with it as will come. It is appropriate that one should not say: 'He cannot take a son, for example,' because it suggests impotence; indeed, a thing is not affirmed or denied of something unless it is within its function. Rather, one should say: 'His power – exalted is He – does not attach to taking a son for example,' because it is not within its function.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses a question: if someone says 'God cannot take me out of His kingdom,' does he become a disbeliever? The answer is no, because it is impossible for there to be another kingdom to take him out to, and divine power does not relate to impossibilities. Similarly, one can say 'God cannot take a son or wife.' However, the commentator advises that it is better not to say 'لا يقدر' (He cannot) because it suggests impotence; rather, one should say 'لا تتعلق قدرته' (His power does not relate) since negating power over something not within its function is more precise. The passage then notes that the opposite of will is dislike (كراهية), not something impossible.

Key Terms
القدرة
المستحيل
الكراهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any negation of divine power constitutes disbelief.
Correction: Negating divine power over an impossibility (mustaḥīl) is not disbelief, because power only relates to possible things. However, one should use precise language to avoid implying impotence.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholar rule that saying 'God cannot take me out of His kingdom' is not disbelief?
A) Because the speaker is not serious
B) Because it is a permissible figure of speech
C) Because the act is impossible for God, and power does not relate to impossibles
D) Because the kingdom is not God's

Key Takeaway
Denying divine power over an impossibility is not disbelief, but it is better to say 'His power does not relate to it' rather than 'He cannot' to avoid implying impotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's power, do you ever use careless phrases like 'God cannot' without considering the implications? Based on this lesson, how will you refine your speech starting today?
?
This lesson teaches that what we say about Allah must be precise. Is there a belief or statement you have held about God that, upon reflection, might be inaccurate or disrespectful? What step will you take to correct it?
?
The scholar warns that saying 'God cannot' can imply weakness. Have you ever, in moments of hardship, doubted God's power over your situation? How can you replace such thoughts with the certainty that nothing is beyond His power except what is impossible by nature?

Divine Power and the Impossibility of a More Perfect World
Al-Bajuri refutes the claim that impossibility implies incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri defends divine power by distinguishing between inability and lack of will.

Core Idea
Impossibility for God arises from will, not weakness.

The objection that God cannot create a more perfect world than this one seems to suggest a limit on divine power. Al-Bajuri addresses this by clarifying that the impossibility is due to the absence of divine will, not any incapacity. This distinction safeguards God's absolute power while acknowledging the fixed nature of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: قدرة, إرادة, أبدع, العجز.
The passage is a single sentence; read it slowly.
2
Identify the objection and response
Find where al-Baqa'i's objection is mentioned and how al-Bajuri refutes it. The response hinges on the phrase 'لعدم تعلق قدرة الله وإرادته'.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on why 'not willed' does not equal 'cannot'. This is crucial for understanding divine attributes.
In theology, power and will are distinct; something can be possible rationally but not willed.
4
Apply the lesson
Explain in your own words why the objection fails. This will reinforce the logical distinction.
Try to anticipate a counterargument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنه لا يمكن أن يوجَدُ أبدعُ من هذا العالم لعدم تعلق قدرة الله وإرادته بإيجاده، ولو شاء الله تعالى لأَوْجَدَ أبدع منه، فليس في كلامه ما يقتضي نسبة العجز إليه تعالى كما توهمه البقاعي، فاعترض عليه.
Indeed, it is impossible that a more perfect [world] than this world could exist because God's power and will are not attached to its creation. And if God had willed, He would have created a more perfect [world] than it. So there is nothing in his statement that implies attributing incapacity to Him, as al-Baqa'i imagined, and he objected to him.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that the statement 'God cannot create a more perfect world' implies incapacity. It begins by asserting that a more perfect world cannot exist because God's power and will are not attached to its creation. This means that the impossibility is not due to a lack of power but because God's will is not directed to it. The passage then notes that if God had willed, He could have created a more perfect world. Therefore, the statement does not attribute incapacity to God, contrary to what al-Baqa'i imagined. The phrase 'fai'tarada 'alayhi' indicates that al-Baqa'i objected to this reasoning, but the text implies that his objection is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that saying something is impossible for God implies He lacks power.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that impossibility can be due to lack of divine will, not power. God has full power to create a more perfect world, but He has not willed it.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is incapable of creating such a world.
B) Because God's power and will are not attached to its creation.
C) Because such a world would be evil.
D) Because God has no knowledge of such a world.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a more perfect world is due to God's will, not His power, thus preserving His absolute capability.

When 'God Cannot' Is Not Disbelief
Al-Bajuri answers whether saying 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom' constitutes disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why saying 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom' does not constitute disbelief, based on the principle that power does not attach to impossibilities.

Core Idea
Negating God's power over impossibilities is not disbelief.

It might sound shocking to say 'God cannot'—but the key is whether the thing in question is possible or impossible. In this passage, Al-Bajuri addresses a specific case: someone says God cannot remove him from His kingdom. At first glance, this might seem to deny God's power. However, the answer shows that since removal is impossible (there is no other kingdom to go to), saying so does not deny God's absolute power. Let's explore why.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a question is posed, then an answer is given. The answer explicitly states why the statement is not disbelief.
Pay attention to the reasoning: 'because his removal... is impossible'.
2
Identify the principle
Identify the key principle Al-Bajuri invokes: 'power is not attached to the impossible'. This is the core doctrinal point that justifies the ruling.
This principle is also used when saying God cannot take a son or wife.
3
Understand the analogy
Notice the analogy: just as it is permissible to say 'God cannot take a son' (because it's impossible), so too here. This reinforces that the statement is not kufr.
The analogy helps to cement the rule in a familiar context.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: some think any negation of God's power is disbelief. But this passage shows the nuance: it depends on whether the thing negated is possible or impossible.
This guards against over-applying takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسُئِلَ بعضهم عمن قال: لا يَقْدِرُ الله أن يُخْرجني من مملكته، هل يَكْفُرُ أو لا؟ فأجاب بأنه لا يَكْفُرُ؛ لأن خروجه من مملكته تعالى مستحيل لعدم إمكان وجود مملكة لغيره يُخْرِجُه إليها، والقدرة لا تتعلَّق بالمستحيل فلا ضير في ذلك، كما لا ضَيْرَ في أن يقال: لا يقدر الله على أن يتخذ ولداً أو زوجةً أو نحو ذلك.
And some were asked about one who said: 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom,' whether he disbelieves or not. He answered that he does not disbelieve, because his removal from His kingdom is impossible due to the impossibility of the existence of a kingdom belonging to another to which He would remove him, and power is not attached to the impossible, so there is no harm in that, just as there is no harm in saying: God cannot take a son or a wife or the like.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents a question-and-answer about a statement that might seem to attribute incapacity to God. Someone asks: if a person says 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom,' does that person become a disbeliever? The answer is no, because the removal is impossible—there is no other kingdom to which God could remove him. Since power does not extend to impossible things, saying so does not deny God's power. The passage draws an analogy: just as it is permissible to say 'God cannot take a son or a wife' because those are impossible and not indicative of incapacity, similarly saying 'God cannot remove me' is permissible. This guards against the misunderstanding that any negation of power over the impossible is disbelief.

Key Terms
قدرة
مستحيل
كفر
إخراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying 'God cannot' about anything is always disbelief because it negates God's power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that negating God's power over impossibilities (like removal to a non‑existent kingdom) is not disbelief, because power does not attach to the impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say that saying 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom' is not disbelief?
A) Because the speaker was joking.
B) Because removal is impossible as there is no other kingdom to remove to.
C) Because God does not have power over kingdoms.
D) Because the speaker was referring to a metaphorical kingdom.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'God cannot' is only problematic if it denies God's power over something possible; if the act is impossible, it is not disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about God's power carelessly, assuming that any limitation on what He 'can' do is disrespectful? How does this lesson's distinction between the possible and the impossible change your understanding of what it means to honor God's omnipotence?
?
Think of a trial you are facing that feels impossible to change. Does the fact that God's power does not attach to impossibilities give you peace or frustration? How might you pray differently knowing this truth?
?
When you say 'God cannot' (e.g., God cannot sin, God cannot be unjust), do you intend to honor His perfection, or have you ever felt uneasy? After this lesson, will you be more confident in using such phrases? Why or why not?

Dislike as the Opposite of Divine Will
Al-Bajuri explains the opposite of will and its two types of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced opposition between divine will and dislike, and how creation with dislike only negates the universality of will, while creation by compulsion or nature negates will itself.

Core Idea
Dislike is the privation of will, not its positive opposite.

In classical Islamic theology, the relationship between divine attributes is carefully defined. Here, al-Bajuri clarifies that the true opposite of will (irada) is not power or compulsion, but dislike (karaha). He then explains how different types of divine action relate to will: creation with dislike only limits will's scope, while creation without will (by compulsion or nature) would contradict its very essence. This distinction preserves both God's perfect will and the universality of its attachment.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the opposite of will
Identify what al-Bajuri says is the opposite of will (irada) and why he says it's not creation (ijad).
Notice the mention of 'adam wa malaka'.
2
Understand the type of opposition
Grasp the concept of privation and possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka): karaha is the absence of will.
This is different from a positive opposite.
3
Examine the estimation of the text
Read the reconstructed meaning: 'creation with His dislike'. Understand why this opposes will in terms of universality.
The ellipsis (hazf) is important.
4
Contrast with compulsion and nature
Compare creation with karaha (negates universality) to creation by ta'leel or tab' (negates essence).
This shows the spectrum of opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَإِجَادِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ... إلخ) لم يقل: وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى إيجاد شيء من العالم... إلخ كما فعل في غيره ؛ لعدم طُولِ الكلام على ما قبله ولا يخفى أن المقابل للإرادة: إنما هو الكراهية وما عُطِفَ عليها على ما يأتي

لا الإيجادُ المذكورُ. والتقابل بينهما من تقابل العدم والملكة؛ لأن الكراهة : عدم الإرادة كما قاله المصنف. وفي الكلام حذف أوّلاً وآخراً، والتقدير: وإيجاد شيءٍ من العالم أو إعدامه مع كراهته لوجوده أو عدمه، وإنما كان ذلك منافياً للإرادة؛ لأن خروج شيء من العالم عنها يَنْفِي عموم تعلقها، وأخرى خروج جميع العالم عنها، فمنافاة هذا للإرادة من حيث عموم تعلقها، لا من حيث ذاتها، بخلاف الإيجاد بالتعليل أو بالطبع فإنه مُنَافٍ لها من حيث ذاتها.
(His saying: 'And the creation of something from the world... etc.') He did not say: 'And similarly it is impossible for Him to create something from the world... etc.' as he did elsewhere, to avoid prolonging the discussion on what came before. And it is not hidden that the opposite of will is only dislike (karaha) and what is conjoined with it, as will come.

The mentioned creation [does not oppose will]. The opposition between them is the opposition of privation and possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka), because dislike is the absence of will, as the author said. And in the speech there is ellipsis at the beginning and end, and the estimation is: And the creation of something from the world or its annihilation with His dislike for its existence or non-existence. And indeed that is opposed to will because the removal of something from the world from it [i.e., will] negates the universality of its attachment, and more so the removal of the entire world from it. So the opposition of this to will is in terms of the universality of its attachment, not in terms of its essence, unlike creation by compulsion (al-ta'leel) or by nature (al-tab'), for it is opposed to it in terms of its essence.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a gloss and main text that explains the relationship between will (irada) and its opposite, dislike (karaha). It begins by noting that the author did not repeat the phrase 'and similarly it is impossible...' for the sake of brevity. The key point is that the opposite of will is karaha, which is defined as the absence of will. The opposition is of the type 'privation and possession' (adam wa malaka), meaning it is a privative opposite. The text then explains that if God creates something while having a dislike for it (karaha), that does not negate will itself but negates its universality—meaning that God's will does not encompass that particular thing. However, if God creates by compulsion or by nature (i.e., not by will), that would negate will itself. Thus, creation with karaha only limits the scope of will, while creation without will (by compulsion or nature) would contradict the very essence of will. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

كراهية
عدم الملكة
تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any divine action contrary to will negates will entirely.
Correction: Creation with dislike (karaha) only negates the universality of will's attachment, not will itself. Only creation without will (by compulsion or nature) negates the essence of will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of opposition exists between will and dislike?
A) Opposition of contradiction (taqabul al-tanqud)
B) Opposition of privation and possession (taqabul al-'adam wa al-malaka)
C) Opposition of correlation (taqabul al-tadayuf)
D) Opposition of opposition (taqabul al-tadadd)

Key Takeaway
Dislike is the privative opposite of will; creation with dislike only limits the scope of will, whereas creation without will (by compulsion or nature) would negate will itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If dislike is merely an absence of will in you, not an active opposition to Allah, how should that reshape your reaction to trials you hate but cannot escape?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Divine Will and Evil
Al-Bajuri refutes Mu'tazila on God not willing evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in Sunni theology, God wills both good and evil without being considered unjust or evil.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all things; moral categories apply only to creatures.

Al-Bajuri tackles a challenge from the Mu'tazila, who argued that God cannot will evil because that would be evil or unjust. This passage clarifies the Sunni position by distinguishing between divine and human standards. Read on to see the arguments and their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the Mu'tazila claims and Al-Bajuri's response.
Notice that the passage begins by affirming no difference between good and evil in divine will.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila arguments
List the four arguments the Mu'tazila used: willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, prohibiting what is willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice.
These are all based on applying human moral categories to God.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Bajuri refutes by stating that these categories apply only to creatures, not to God, because God is not questioned. The purpose of commands and prohibitions is to test obedience.
The key phrase: 'بأن ذلك إنما يُعَدُّ شرّاً أو قبيحاً أو سَفَها أو ظُلْماً بالنسبة إلى الحادث لا إليه تعالى'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فَرْقَ بين الخير والشر كما شَمِلَهُ كلامُ المصنف، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه تعالى لا يريد الشرور والقبائح واحتجوا بأن إرادة الشرِ شَرٌّ، وإرادة القبيح قبيحةً، وبأن النَّهْيَ عما يُرَادُ والأمر بما لا يُرَاد سَفَة، وبأن العقاب على ما أريد ظُلم، والله مُنَزَّة عن ذلك كلّه. ورُدَّ بأن ذلك إنما يُعَدُّ شرّاً أو قبيحاً أو سَفَها أو ظُلْماً بالنسبة إلى الحادث لا إليه تعالى؛ لأنه لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل، وحكمة أمره أو نهيه ظهورُ الامتحان هل يطيعُ العبد أو لا؟
And there is no difference between good and evil, as the author's speech includes, contrary to the Mu'tazila, for they held that He does not will evils and abominations, and they argued that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and that prohibiting what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and that punishing for what is willed is injustice, and God is transcendent above all that. And it was refuted that that is considered evil, abominable, foolish, or unjust only with respect to the creature, not with respect to Him, because He is not asked about what He does, and the wisdom of His command or prohibition is the manifestation of the test: whether the servant obeys or not.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila position that God does not will evil. It begins by affirming that there is no distinction between good and evil in terms of divine will—God wills both, as the author's general statement covers. The Mu'tazila argued that willing evil is evil, willing abominable things is abominable, prohibiting what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolish, and punishing for what is willed is unjust. They concluded that God is free from such things. The refutation is that these moral categories (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) apply only to creatures, not to God. God is not subject to external moral standards; He cannot be questioned about His actions. Moreover, the purpose of God's commands and prohibitions is to test humans—to see whether they will obey or disobey. Thus, God may will something and still prohibit it, and punish those who do it, because the test is meaningful.

Key Terms
خير
شر
إرادة
معتزلة
قبيح
ظلم
امتحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, He would be evil or unjust.
Correction: Moral categories like 'evil' and 'injustice' apply only to creatures, not to God, because He is the Creator and cannot be judged by human standards.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in the passage, why is it not problematic for God to will evil?
A) Because evil is actually good.
B) Because God does not actually will evil.
C) Because moral categories like 'evil' and 'injustice' apply only to creatures, not to God.
D) Because God's will is beyond good and evil.

Key Takeaway
God wills all things, good and evil, and His actions are not subject to human moral judgment; His commands and prohibitions serve to test human obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or evil in your life, do you ever feel that God is unjust or that He should not have willed that? How does this lesson challenge you to trust His wisdom even when you do not understand?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that willing evil would make God evil. In your own heart, do you sometimes judge God's actions by human standards? What would it mean for you to truly accept that He is not asked about what He does?
?
This passage says God commands and prohibits to test whether you obey. Are you living as though your obedience matters—that it is the very purpose of your test? Or do you live as if your actions have no weight before an all-determining God?

The Opposition Between Will and Aversion in Divine Attributes
Sanusi defines the opposition between will and aversion as privation/disposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key concepts: how will and aversion oppose one another as privation/disposition, why bringing into existence with aversion negates the generality of will's attachment but not its essence, and how this differs from causation or nature models.

Core Idea
Will and aversion are not opposites of equal strength; aversion is simply the absence of will.

Sanusi begins a technical discussion about the nature of divine will and its relationship to what God brings into existence. He clarifies a point in the matn about an opposition and explains the precise type of opposition involved. This distinction is crucial for understanding why God's will includes everything that happens, even those things He does not like.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style; Sanusi uses ellipsis.
2
Identify the two types of opposition
The passage presents two types of opposition: one between will and aversion (privation/disposition) and one between will and causation/nature (essential opposition). Find each and understand the difference.
3
Ask: why does this matter?
Ask yourself why this technical distinction is important for the broader debate about divine will. The commentary will connect it to the Mu'tazila controversy.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي
لا الإيجادُ المذكورُ. والتقابل بينهما من تقابل العدم والملكة؛ لأن الكراهة : عدم الإرادة كما قاله المصنف. وفي الكلام حذف أوّلاً وآخراً، والتقدير: وإيجاد شيءٍ من العالم أو إعدامه مع كراهته لوجوده أو عدمه، وإنما كان ذلك منافياً للإرادة؛ لأن خروج شيء من العالم عنها يَنْفِي عموم تعلقها، وأخرى خروج جميع العالم عنها، فمنافاة هذا للإرادة من حيث عموم تعلقها، لا من حيث ذاتها، بخلاف الإيجاد بالتعليل أو بالطبع فإنه مُنَافٍ لها من حيث ذاتها.
ولا فَرْقَ بين الخير والشر كما شَمِلَهُ كلامُ المصنف، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه تعالى لا يريد الشرور والقبائح واحتجوا بأن إرادة الشرِ شَرٌّ، وإرادة القبيح قبيحةً، وبأن النَّهْيَ عما يُرَادُ والأمر بما لا يُرَاد سَفَة، وبأن العقاب على ما أريد ظُلم، والله مُنَزَّة عن ذلك كلّه. ورُدَّ بأن ذلك إنما يُعَدُّ شرّاً أو قبيحاً أو سَفَها أو ظُلْماً بالنسبة إلى الحادث لا إليه تعالى؛ لأنه لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل، وحكمة أمره أو نهيه ظهورُ الامتحان هل يطيعُ العبد أو لا؟
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: الْمَلَكَةِ) هي الصفة الراسخة.
(قوله: وَأَحْرَى) أي: في نفي تعلق الإرادة، لا في نفي عمومه؛ لأنه ينفيه من أصله.
(قوله: بِخِلافِ الْإِيجَادِ بالتَّعْلِيلِ... إلخ) المنافي لذات الإرادة إيجاد العالم كله بطريق التعليل أو الطبع، وكلام المصنف في إيجاد شيء منه بذلك الطريق، وهو إنما ينافي التعلق كالذي قبله والجواب أن القائل بالتعليل أو الطبع يجريه في جميع العالم لا عموم في بعضه دون بعض فمنافاة ذلك الإرادة بالنظر إلى الواقع لا بالنظر إلى كلام المصنف. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي -
هو إيجادُ شيءٍ من العالم أو إعدامه مع الكراهة كما قال المصنف، فلذلك عَدَلَ إلى هذا الصنيع، لكنَّ مَحَطَّ المقابلة قوله: (مع كراهته وما عطف عليه. نعم؛ كان الأنسب أن يقول: وكراهته لشيءٍ أَوْجَدَه، أو أعدمه، أو ذهوله، أو غَفْلَتُه عن ذلك... إلخ، إلا أن يقال: هذا هو مراد المحشي. تأمل.
(قوله: وَأَخرَى) أي: أحقُّ وأولى، وهو خبر مقدَّم، وما بعده مبتداً مؤخَّرٌ.

الممسوحة ضوئيا بـ CamScanner

١٣٤
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية
ولا يَرِدُ على مذهب أهل السنة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر ) ( ) ؛ لأن الإرادة غيرُ الرّضَا، والتمسك بالآية مبني على ترادفهما، وهو باطل. وبالجملة، فيلزم على مذهب المعتزلة: أن أكثر ما يقع في الوجود على غير مراده تعالى، وقد حُكِي أن بعض أئمة أهل السنة حَضَرَ مع بعض المعتزلة للمناظرة، فلما جلس المعتزلي قال سبحان من تَنَزَّه عن الفحشاء، فقال السُّني: سبحان من لا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِهِ إلا ما يشاء، فقال المعتزلي: أيشاءُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى؟ فقال السُّنّي: أيُعْصَى ربنا قَهْراً؟ فقال المعتزلي: أرأيتَ أن مَنَعَنِي الهُدَى ، وقَضَى عليَّ بالرَّدَى، أحسنَ إِليَّ أم أسا؟ فقال: إن مَنَعَكَ ما هو لك فقد أسا، وإن مَنَعَكَ ما هو له؛ فيختص برحمته من يشا. فانقطع المعتزلي عن المناظرة.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَلا يَرْضَى لعل المراد بالرضا ترك المؤاخذة أي لا يترك المؤاخذة بالكفر، بل يؤاخذ عليه بالعذاب المخلد.
(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات
(قوله: عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ) أي: عن إرادتها.
(قوله: (قهراً المراد بالقهر عدم الإرادة حتى تظهر المقابلة بين كلام السني والمعتزلي.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(قوله: أَرَأَيْتَ إِنْ مَنَعَنِي الْهُدَى... إلخ) مقصودُهُ : أنه لا يَصِحُ أن يقال: إنه أَحْسَنَ إليَّ أو أساء إلا في هذه الحالة، فيتعين أنه لم يَقْضِ علي بالرَّدَى، ولم يقع بإرادته حتى يقال: إنه أحسن أم أساء. تدبر.
.V
(1) من سورة الزمر: ٧.
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abdillah al-Sanusi: Not the aforementioned bringing into existence. And the opposition between them is of the opposition of privation and disposition; because aversion (karāha) is the absence of will (irāda) as the author said. And in the speech there is ellipsis at the beginning and end, and the estimation is: and bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it while having aversion to its existence or non-existence. And that was only opposed to will because the emergence of something from the world from it negates the generality of its attachment, and another [reason] is the emergence of the entire world from it. So this opposes will in terms of the generality of its attachment, not in terms of its essence, contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation (ta‘līl) or by nature (tab‘), for it is opposed to it in terms of its essence. And there is no difference between good and evil as the author’s speech includes it, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who held that He, exalted, does not will evils and abominations, and they argued that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and Allah is exalted above all of that. And it was refuted that this is only considered evil or abominable or foolishness or injustice relative to the contingent, not to Him, exalted; because He is not asked about what He does, and the wisdom of His command or prohibition is the manifestation of the test: whether the servant obeys or not? Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'the disposition') it is the firmly rooted attribute. (His statement: 'and more appropriate') i.e., in negating the attachment of will, not in negating its generality; because he negates it from its root. (His statement: 'contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation... etc.') That which opposes the essence of will is bringing the entire world into existence by way of causation or nature, and the author’s speech is about bringing something of it into existence by that way, and it only opposes attachment like the preceding one. The answer is that the one who asserts causation or nature applies it to the entire world, not generality in some without others, so its opposition to will is from the perspective of reality, not from the perspective of the author’s speech. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: It is bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it with aversion as the author said, therefore he turned to this construction. But the point of the comparison is his statement: 'with aversion to it' and what is coordinated with it. Yes, it would have been more suitable for him to say: 'and his aversion to something he brought into existence, or annihilated, or his heedlessness, or his forgetfulness of that... etc.' unless it is said: this is what the commentator intended. Consider. (His statement: 'and another') i.e., more worthy and prior, and it is a fronted predicate, and what follows it is a delayed subject. (The rest of the text including page 134: Commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusia) And there does not apply to the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: {And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief} (Q. 39:7); because will (irāda) is other than pleasure (riḍā), and adherence to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. In summary, it follows upon the school of the Mu‘tazila that most of what occurs in existence is not according to His will, Exalted. And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. Commentary of al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and He is not pleased') perhaps what is meant by pleasure (riḍā) is refraining from taking to task, i.e., He does not refrain from taking to task for unbelief, rather He takes to task for it with eternal punishment. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irāda)
الكراهة (karāha)
التقابل (taqābul)
العدم والملكة (ʿadam wa al-malaka)
التعلق (taʿalluq)
التعليل (taʿlīl)
الطبع (ṭabʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposition between will and aversion is like the opposition between existence and non-existence or heat and cold.
Correction: The passage states it is the opposition of privation and disposition, meaning aversion is simply the absence of will, not an opposite attribute.

Checkpoint
What type of opposition exists between the divine will (irāda) and aversion (karāha) according to Sanusi?
A) Opposition of existence and non-existence
B) Opposition of privation and disposition
C) Opposition of contradiction
D) Opposition of correlatives

Key Takeaway
Aversion is the absence of will, so the opposition is one of privation, not equality; this protects the principle that all things occur by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart feels aversion to something God has decreed. According to this lesson, that aversion is merely the absence of will. But is your heart truly indifferent, or does it actively resist? What would it mean to acknowledge that your aversion is a lack, not a positive force, and to turn that lack into submission?
?
The lesson says that bringing into existence by nature negates the essence of will. In your daily life, do you attribute events to 'nature' or 'luck' rather than to God's will? How does this lesson expose a hidden shirk in your thoughts? Will you consciously remind yourself that every detail is by His will alone?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between negating the generality of will and negating its essence. When you sin, do you act as though God's will is not comprehensive—that your sin happens outside His will? How does this lesson force you to either accept His will fully or deny it entirely? Repent and realign your heart to His universal will.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
God's Will Encompasses Good and Evil: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila arguments that God does not will evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theology affirms that God wills both good and evil, and how the Mu'tazila arguments are refuted by distinguishing between relative and absolute judgments.

Core Idea
God wills evil, but that does not make Him evil because He is beyond such relative attributions.

Having established the scope of divine will, Sanusi now tackles the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot will evil. He presents their four proofs and then gives the Sunni response. This is a crucial point of difference between the two schools, resting on a profound understanding of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila arguments
The passage lists four reasons why the Mu'tazila thought God cannot will evil. Read them carefully and see the logic behind each.
The arguments are: willing evil is evil, willing abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed is foolish, punishing for what is willed is unjust.
2
Understand the Sunni refutation
The refutation is that these judgments only apply to contingent beings, not to God. God is not subject to such evaluations.
Key phrase: 'because He is not asked about what He does'.
3
Connect to the earlier lesson
Notice how the earlier discussion about opposition prepares the ground: God can will something and still not be pleased with it.

متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي
لا الإيجادُ المذكورُ. والتقابل بينهما من تقابل العدم والملكة؛ لأن الكراهة : عدم الإرادة كما قاله المصنف. وفي الكلام حذف أوّلاً وآخراً، والتقدير: وإيجاد شيءٍ من العالم أو إعدامه مع كراهته لوجوده أو عدمه، وإنما كان ذلك منافياً للإرادة؛ لأن خروج شيء من العالم عنها يَنْفِي عموم تعلقها، وأخرى خروج جميع العالم عنها، فمنافاة هذا للإرادة من حيث عموم تعلقها، لا من حيث ذاتها، بخلاف الإيجاد بالتعليل أو بالطبع فإنه مُنَافٍ لها من حيث ذاتها.
ولا فَرْقَ بين الخير والشر كما شَمِلَهُ كلامُ المصنف، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه تعالى لا يريد الشرور والقبائح واحتجوا بأن إرادة الشرِ شَرٌّ، وإرادة القبيح قبيحةً، وبأن النَّهْيَ عما يُرَادُ والأمر بما لا يُرَاد سَفَة، وبأن العقاب على ما أريد ظُلم، والله مُنَزَّة عن ذلك كلّه. ورُدَّ بأن ذلك إنما يُعَدُّ شرّاً أو قبيحاً أو سَفَها أو ظُلْماً بالنسبة إلى الحادث لا إليه تعالى؛ لأنه لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل، وحكمة أمره أو نهيه ظهورُ الامتحان هل يطيعُ العبد أو لا؟

And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الشر (sharr)
القبيح (qabīḥ)
السَّفَه (safah)
الظلم (ẓulm)
النسبة (nisba)
الحادث (ḥādith)
الامتحان (imtiḥān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then He must be evil or unjust.
Correction: Sunni theology teaches that these attributions are relative; God's will is beyond such judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the core Sunni response to the Mu'tazila argument that 'willing evil is evil'?
A) God does not actually will evil.
B) Evil is not truly evil from God's perspective.
C) Such judgments are relative to the contingent, not to God.
D) God's will only applies to good actions.

Key Takeaway
God wills both good and evil, but moral judgments like 'evil' and 'unjust' do not apply to God because He is the standard, not subject to standards.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something evil happens in your life, do you ever find yourself accusing God of injustice? How does the truth that God is not subject to the standard of justice—He is the standard—change your reaction? Will you commit to never questioning His decree again?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that willing evil is evil. Have you ever subconsciously judged God by human moral standards? What practical step will you take to train your heart to accept that God's will is beyond human categorization?
?
God wills both good and evil, and He holds you accountable. This test of obedience is the purpose of His commands. Are you living as if you are being tested, or do you act as though your actions are outside His will? What will you change today to align with this reality?

Distinguishing Will from Pleasure: The Debate with Mu'tazila
Sanusi distinguishes will from pleasure and recounts a debate that refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between God's will (irāda) and His pleasure (riḍā), and see how a famous debate illustrates the Sunni position.

Core Idea
God can will something without being pleased with it; the two attributes are distinct.

Sanusi now addresses a potential objection from the Qur'an: Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. If He wills it, wouldn't that contradict His displeasure? The answer is that will and pleasure are different. He then recounts a striking debate that powerfully illustrates the Sunni position on divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Qur'anic verse
The verse (Q. 39:7) says Allah is not pleased with unbelief. Read the passage and see how the Sunni scholar explains it.
The key is that pleasure (riḍā) here means refraining from punishment, not a synonym for will.
2
Understand the distinction
The passage explicitly states that will and pleasure are not synonymous. This is why the verse does not contradict the Sunni position.
3
Read the debate
Follow the exchange between the Sunni and the Mu'tazili. Notice how each statement undercuts the other's assumptions.
The Mu'tazili is silenced by the end.
4
Reflect on the implications
This debate shows the practical consequences of the Mu'tazila view: most of what happens would not be willed by God.

(1) من سورة الزمر: ٧.
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abdillah al-Sanusi: Not the aforementioned bringing into existence. And the opposition between them is of the opposition of privation and disposition; because aversion (karāha) is the absence of will (irāda) as the author said. And in the speech there is ellipsis at the beginning and end, and the estimation is: and bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it while having aversion to its existence or non-existence. And that was only opposed to will because the emergence of something from the world from it negates the generality of its attachment, and another [reason] is the emergence of the entire world from it. So this opposes will in terms of the generality of its attachment, not in terms of its essence, contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation (ta‘līl) or by nature (tab‘), for it is opposed to it in terms of its essence. And there is no difference between good and evil as the author’s speech includes it, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who held that He, exalted, does not will evils and abominations, and they argued that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and Allah is exalted above all of that. And it was refuted that this is only considered evil or abominable or foolishness or injustice relative to the contingent, not to Him, exalted; because He is not asked about what He does, and the wisdom of His command or prohibition is the manifestation of the test: whether the servant obeys or not? Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'the disposition') it is the firmly rooted attribute. (His statement: 'and more appropriate') i.e., in negating the attachment of will, not in negating its generality; because he negates it from its root. (His statement: 'contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation... etc.') That which opposes the essence of will is bringing the entire world into existence by way of causation or nature, and the author’s speech is about bringing something of it into existence by that way, and it only opposes attachment like the preceding one. The answer is that the one who asserts causation or nature applies it to the entire world, not generality in some without others, so its opposition to will is from the perspective of reality, not from the perspective of the author’s speech. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: It is bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it with aversion as the author said, therefore he turned to this construction. But the point of the comparison is his statement: 'with aversion to it' and what is coordinated with it. Yes, it would have been more suitable for him to say: 'and his aversion to something he brought into existence, or annihilated, or his heedlessness, or his forgetfulness of that... etc.' unless it is said: this is what the commentator intended. Consider. (His statement: 'and another') i.e., more worthy and prior, and it is a fronted predicate, and what follows it is a delayed subject. (The rest of the text including page 134: Commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusia) And there does not apply to the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: {And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief} (Q. 39:7); because will (irāda) is other than pleasure (riḍā), and adherence to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. In summary, it follows upon the school of the Mu‘tazila that most of what occurs in existence is not according to His will, Exalted. And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. Commentary of al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and He is not pleased') perhaps what is meant by pleasure (riḍā) is refraining from taking to task, i.e., He does not refrain from taking to task for unbelief, rather He takes to task for it with eternal punishment. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills something, He must be pleased with it.
Correction: Will and pleasure are distinct; God can will something (like unbelief) without being pleased with it.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni position, why does Q. 39:7 ('And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief') not contradict that God wills unbelief?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because will and pleasure are distinct attributes.
C) Because unbelief is not actually willed by God.
D) Because the verse only applies to believers.

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolute; His pleasure is conditional. Their separation allows for God to will even what He dislikes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that it happened only because God willed it, even though He is not pleased with it? How does that realization affect your humility before Him and your reliance on His mercy?
?
If everything you have—including your ability to obey—belongs to God and could be withdrawn at any moment, what kind of gratitude and dependence should fill your heart right now? Is that what you actually feel?
?
The Sunni debater silenced the Mu'tazili with the truth that God owes nothing to anyone. Does your heart accept this truth without resentment, or do you still harbor a sense of entitlement toward God's favor?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Distinguishing Divine Will from Divine Pleasure
Sanusi rebuts Mu'tazili objection using the will/pleasure distinction and a debate anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'aris distinguish between divine will (irada) and divine pleasure (rida) to refute the Mu'tazili objection from the Quranic verse, and how this distinction is illustrated in a historical debate.

Core Idea
Allah may will what He does not approve; will and pleasure are not the same.

The Mu'tazilites argued that since Allah does not approve of disbelief, He cannot will it. The Ash'ari response hinges on a crucial distinction: will (irada) and pleasure (rida) are not synonymous. In this passage, Sanusi presents this distinction and recounts a famous debate where a Sunni imam silenced a Mu'tazili opponent. Let's explore this rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from logical argument to narrative debate.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where Sanusi distinguishes will (irada) from pleasure (rida). This is the core of the rebuttal.
The verse is used by Mu'tazila as evidence, but Sanusi shows it does not apply.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazili argument and the Sunni counter
Understand the Mu'tazili position: that will and approval are the same, so if Allah does not approve of disbelief, He cannot will it. Then see how Sanusi counters by separating the two concepts, leading to the conclusion that much of what happens is not willed according to them (which is problematic).
4
Examine the debate narrative
Follow the exchange between the Sunni imam and the Mu'tazili. Notice how each question and answer builds on the distinction. Focus on the final question about 'preventing what is yours' and the decisive answer.
The debate illustrates the practical implications of the distinction.

ولا يَرِدُ على مذهب أهل السنة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر﴾؛ لأن الإرادة غيرُ الرّضَا، والتمسك بالآية مبني على ترادفهما، وهو باطل. وبالجملة، فيلزم على مذهب المعتزلة: أن أكثر ما يقع في الوجود على غير مراده تعالى، وقد حُكِي أن بعض أئمة أهل السنة حَضَرَ مع بعض المعتزلة للمناظرة، فلما جلس المعتزلي قال سبحان من تَنَزَّه عن الفحشاء، فقال السُّني: سبحان من لا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِهِ إلا ما يشاء، فقال المعتزلي: أيشاءُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى؟ فقال السُّنّي: أيُعْصَى ربنا قَهْراً؟ فقال المعتزلي: أرأيتَ أن مَنَعَنِي الهُدَى، وقَضَى عليَّ بالرَّدَى، أحسنَ إِليَّ أم أسا؟ فقال: إن مَنَعَكَ ما هو لك فقد أسا، وإن مَنَعَكَ ما هو له؛ فيختص برحمته من يشا. فانقطع المعتزلي عن المناظرة.
And it is not an objection against the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' because will (irada) is other than pleasure (rida), and clinging to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. And in summary, it follows upon the Mu'tazilite school that most of what occurs in existence is contrary to His will, exalted. And it has been narrated that one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with a Mu'tazilite. When the Mu'tazilite sat down, he said: 'Glory be to Him who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to Him in whose domain nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu'tazilite said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu'tazilite said: 'Do you see, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevented what is yours, then He has done evil; but if He prevented what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu'tazilite was silenced from debate.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of a Mu'tazili objection against the Ash'ari position on divine will. The objection cites the verse 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Quran 39:7) to argue that Allah does not will disbelief. Sanusi responds by distinguishing between will (irada) and pleasure (rida), stating that they are not synonymous. Thus, Allah may will disbelief without approving of it. Furthermore, Sanusi points out that the Mu'tazili position, which makes will follow command, implies that most occurrences in existence (sins, permissible acts) are not willed by Allah, which is problematic. He then narrates a debate where a Sunni imam outmatches a Mu'tazili. The Mu'tazili first exclaims that Allah is free from indecency, to which the Sunni replies that nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The Mu'tazili then asks if Allah wills disobedience, and the Sunni counters by asking if Allah is disobeyed by force (i.e., against His will). The Mu'tazili then raises a moral argument: if God prevents guidance and decrees ruin, has He done good or evil? The Sunni's final response is crucial: if God prevented what is 'yours' (something you are owed), that would be evil; but since everything is God's, He may grant mercy to whom He wills. This shows that the Mu'tazili's assumption of moral obligation on God is false. The Mu'tazili is thereby silenced.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
رضا (rida)
مذهب أهل السنة (madhhab ahl al-sunna)
معتزلة (mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe Allah approves of evil actions.
Correction: No, Ash'aris distinguish between will and approval. Allah may will something without approving it, so He does not approve of evil even though He wills its occurrence.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that Sanusi uses to refute the Mu'tazili objection from the verse 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief'?
A) Will and pleasure are synonymous
B) Will and pleasure are distinct
C) The verse is abrogated
D) Disbelief is not willed by Allah

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari rebuttal against Mu'tazilites on divine will rests on the distinction between will (irada) and pleasure (rida), showing that Allah may will what He does not approve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever secretly think, 'If Allah truly willed this, He wouldn't have let it happen'? How does the distinction between will and pleasure reshape your understanding of your own failures—and your repentance?
?
The Mu'tazilite asked if he deserved good treatment from God. His feeling: 'Has Allah been unjust to me by not guiding me?' Do you ever hold resentment toward Allah for trials or lack of blessings, assuming you are owed something? What does the Sunni's answer—'If He prevented what is His'—reveal about the danger of entitlement before your Creator?
?
Allah wills your existence and actions, including your sins, yet He does not approve of sin. Will you let this truth drive you to despair or to humility? Can you still love and obey a Lord who wills things He hates—and does that not make His pleasure a greater gift than you could ever earn?
