The Meaning of Divine Pleasure (Rida)
Al-Ajhuri defines 'pleasure' as leaving off accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'pleasure' (rida) in theological debates can refer to leaving off accountability rather than emotional approval.

Core Idea
Divine pleasure is not approval—it is leaving off punishment.

When the Qur'an states that Allah is not pleased with disbelief, it does not mean He has an emotional state that is disapproving. Sunni theologians reinterpret 'pleasure' in this context as 'leaving off accountability' to avoid anthropomorphism and to clarify that punishment is justly deserved. In this lesson, we examine al-Ajhuri's concise gloss on this key term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of al-Ajhuri's gloss.
2
Identify the new definition of rida
Al-Ajhuri says 'perhaps what is meant by pleasure (rida) is leaving off accountability (tark al-mu'akhadha).' This is the key point.
3
Reflect on why this definition matters
This interpretation avoids anthropomorphism and preserves divine transcendence. It also shows that Allah's non-approval is active (punishment) rather than passive (displeasure).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَلا يَرْضَى لعل المراد بالرضا ترك المؤاخذة أي لا يترك المؤاخذة بالكفر، بل يؤاخذ عليه بالعذاب المخلد.
(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات
(قوله: عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ) أي: عن إرادتها.
(قوله: (قهراً المراد بالقهر عدم الإرادة حتى تظهر المقابلة بين كلام السني والمعتزلي.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(قوله: أَرَأَيْتَ إِنْ مَنَعَنِي الْهُدَى... إلخ) مقصودُهُ : أنه لا يَصِحُ أن يقال: إنه أَحْسَنَ إليَّ أو أساء إلا في هذه الحالة، فيتعين أنه لم يَقْضِ علي بالرَّدَى، ولم يقع بإرادته حتى يقال: إنه أحسن أم أساء. تدبر.
Hashiya of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'And He does not approve' – perhaps what is meant by pleasure (rida) is leaving off accountability, i.e., He does not leave off accountability for disbelief, but rather He punishes for it with eternal torment. His saying: 'So it follows upon the school...' – because they made will (irada) dependent on command, so He only wills what He commands; thus prohibitions, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. His saying: 'from indecency' – i.e., from willing it. His saying: 'by force' – what is meant by force is the absence of will, so that the contrast between the Sunni's and Mu'tazilite's statements becomes clear. Taqrirat of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'Do you see, if He prevents me from guidance...' – his intent is that it is not correct to say 'He did good to me' or 'He did evil' except in this situation; so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will such that it could be said He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the glosses of al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi on specific phrases from the previous debate. Al-Ajhuri first explains the word 'pleasure' (rida) in the verse: he proposes that it means 'leaving off accountability,' so Allah does not leave off punishing disbelief, but punishes with eternal torment. This clarifies that divine disapproval does not negate will. Next, al-Ajhuri comments on 'so it follows upon the school...' explaining that the Mu'tazila made will (irada) dependent on command (amr), thus only willing what He commands; consequently, prohibited, disliked, and permissible acts are not willed by Allah, which is clearly false since such acts are more numerous. Then he glosses 'from indecency' as 'from willing it,' showing that Allah's transcendence is from willing indecency, not from its occurrence. He also explains 'by force' as 'absence of will,' so the Sunni's response highlights that disobedience occurs with Allah's will, not by force. Al-Anbabi then comments on the Mu'tazili's final question. He states that the Mu'tazili's question about good or evil is invalid because the premise assumes that God has an obligation to give guidance; in reality, everything is God's, so His prevention is not evil. He concludes that it is determined that God did not decree ruin (rada) nor did it occur by His will, so the question is misguided. The lesson ends with 'reflect' (tadabbar).

Key Terms
رضا (rida)
مؤاخذة (mu'akhadha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'pleasure' (rida) means Allah loves or approves of something.
Correction: In this debate, rida is interpreted as 'leaving off accountability,' not emotional approval.

Checkpoint

A) Divine love
B) Leaving off accountability
C) Will
D) Command

Key Takeaway
Divine pleasure in theological debate can mean leaving off accountability, not emotional approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear that Allah does not approve of disbelief, do you feel a sense of urgency to align your life with His command? Or do you treat it as a passive statement?
?
This lesson defines divine rida in a way that emphasizes accountability. How does this affect your understanding of repentance? Do you truly seek to leave off actions that incur His mu'akhadha?

The Mu'tazili Position on Divine Will and Its Refutation
Al-Ajhuri explains and refutes the Mu'tazili equation of will with command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili doctrine that will follows command, why it leads to absurd conclusions, and how Sunni theologians respond.

Core Idea
If Allah wills only what He commands, then most of creation is unwilled—an absurdity.

The Mu'tazila argued that divine will (irada) is tied to command (amr): Allah wills what He commands and nothing else. This seems logical but leads to a problematic conclusion: that acts like prohibitions, disliked things, and permissible actions are not willed by Allah. Since such acts are numerous, this would imply that most of human actions occur outside divine will—contradicting Islam's emphasis on divine sovereignty. In this lesson, we explore al-Ajhuri's gloss on this point and the clarifying terminology that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the Mu'tazili position
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the Mu'tazili position is presented as a logical deduction.
2
Understand the reasoning: will dependent on command
The Mu'tazila made will dependent on command, so they only accept that what Allah commands is willed.
3
Note the conclusion: only commanded acts are willed
This excludes prohibitions, disliked, and permissible acts from divine will.
4
Consider the refutation: prohibitions etc. are more numerous
Al-Ajhuri points out that these uncommanded acts are far more numerous, making the position absurd.
5
Examine the glosses on 'from indecency' and 'by force'
These clarifications define key terms and contrast Sunni and Mu'tazili views.

Refuting a False Premise: God's Obligation to Guide
Al-Anbabi exposes the false premise in the Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Anbabi's refutation of the Mu'tazili question about God's guidance and the principle that God has no obligation to guide anyone.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to guide; His prevention is not evil.

The Mu'tazili debater asks: if God prevents me from guidance, has He done good or evil to me? This question assumes that God has a duty to guide everyone. Al-Anbabi points out that this premise is false. Allah is the owner of all, and His acts are not subject to such moral evaluation. He is free to give guidance or withhold it without being called 'good' or 'evil.' By examining this incisive comment, we see the Sunni insistence on divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Anbabi's gloss on the Mu'tazili question
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the context: the Mu'tazili is trying to corner the Sunni.
2
Identify the hidden premise: God must guide
The question only makes sense if God owes guidance. Premise is false.
3
Understand why the premise is false
God is the owner of all; He has no obligations to His creation. His actions are not subject to human moral evaluation.
4
See the conclusion: God did not decree ruin, and the question is invalid
Al-Anbabi says 'it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will such that it could be said He did good or evil.'
5
Reflect on the broader principle
This underscores divine sovereignty and the rejection of any notion of obligation on God.
The word 'tadabbar' (reflect) encourages contemplation.

Definition of Karaha and Refutation of Mu'tazili Error
Definition of karaha and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that karaha (dislike) in divine context means absence of will, and why the Mu'tazili equation of command and will is mistaken.

Core Idea
Karaha is not hatred but absence of will.

The passage opens by clarifying a key divine attribute term: karaha. The author explicitly defines it as 'absence of will' to prevent confusion with its legal meaning. He also uses this definition to correct a major theological error of the Mu'tazila regarding the relationship between God's command and His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic sentence defining karaha as 'the absence of His will for it'. Note the author's caution against misunderstanding.
The legal meaning of karaha is a request to leave, which is different.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili error
Identify why the Mu'tazila claimed that what is disliked (makruh) is not willed, based on their equation of command and will.
The author refutes by showing no necessary link between command and will.
3
Reflect on the four possibilities
Notice the four cases: command with/without will, will with/without command. This disproves the Mu'tazili claim.
This was clarified earlier in the text.

قوله: أَيْ: عَدَمِ إِرَادَتِهِ لَهُ تَعَالَى إنما أتى المصنف بذلك ـ مع أن التفسير ليس من وظيفة المتون ؛ لئلا يُتَوَهَّمَ أن المراد بالكراهة معناها الشرعي: وهو طَلَبُ تَرْكِ الشيء طلباً غير جازم.
لا يُقال: إن المقامَ يقتضي تفسيرها بما ذُكِرَ فلا حاجة للتنصيص عليه؛ لأنا نقول : المصنف لاحَظَ الاحتياط، وأيضاً قصد التنبية على خطأ المعتزلة في قولهم: إن الإرادة على وفق الأمر وبناؤهم على ذلك أن المكروة شرعاً ليس بمراد، ووجه خطئهم في ذلك: أنه لا ملازَمَةَ بين الأمر ،والإرادة، فقد يأمُرُ ولا يُرِيدُ، وقد يُرِيدُ ولا يأمُرُ، كما أنه قد يريدُ ويأمرُ ، وقد لا يريد ولا يأمرُ كما تقدم توضيحه.
قوله: أَوْ مَعَ الدُّهُولِ أَوِ الْغَفْلَةِ معطوف على قوله: (مع كراهته، وكذا قوله: (أو بالتعليل أو بالطبع، وعَطْفُ ذلك على الكراهة بالمعنى المذكور من عطف الخاص على العام لدخوله فيها.
فإن قيل: إذا كانت هذه الأمور داخلةً في الكراهة بذلك المعنى؛ كان مستغنى عنها، فلا حاجة إلى ذكرها أجيب بأنه إنما ذكرها المصنف ـ مع كونها مستغنى عنها ؛ لأن المقصود في هذا العلم: ذِكْرُ العقائد على وجه التفصيل؛ لأن خَطَرَ الجهل فيه عظيم، فلا يُكْتَفَى فيه بعامّ عن خاص، ولا بملزوم عن لازم.
واعلم أنه اختلف فقيل : الذُّهُولُ والغَفْلَةُ متساويان. وقيل: الغفلة أعم من الذهول؛ لأن الذهول: هو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء مع تقدم العلم به، والغفلة: عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقاً، وهذا هو ما ظهر للمؤلف.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: أَنَّ الْمَكْرُوهَ شَرْعاً) وكذا المحرم والمباح.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: مِنْ عَطْفِ الْخَاصِّ عَلَى الْعَامِّ فيه أنه لا يكون ب(أو)، إلا أن تُجْعَلَ بمعنى الواو. :(قوله: وَهَذَا مَا ظَهَرَ لِلْمُؤلّف) راجع للقول الثاني، فحينئذٍ ينبغي حَمْلُ المتن عليه.
His saying: 'i.e., the absence of His will for it, Exalted' – the author only brought this, despite interpretation not being the function of matns, lest it be supposed that what is meant by 'karaha' (dislike) is its legal meaning: which is the request to leave a thing in a non-assertive manner. It cannot be said that the context requires its interpretation as mentioned so there is no need to specify it; because we say: the author observed caution, and also intended to alert to the error of the Mu'tazila in their saying that will is in accordance with command, and their building upon that that what is legally disliked (makruh) is not willed. The face of their error in that: there is no necessary connection between command and will. He may command and not will, and may will and not command, just as He may will and command, and may not will and not command, as has been clarified previously. His saying: 'or with heedlessness (dhuhul) or forgetfulness (ghafla)' is conjoined to his saying 'with His dislike,' and likewise his saying 'or by cause or by nature,' and the conjoining of that to dislike in the mentioned sense is a conjunction of the specific to the general because of its inclusion in it. If it is said: since these things are included in dislike in that sense, they are rendered superfluous, so there is no need to mention them. The answer is that the author only mentioned them – despite being superfluous – because the purpose in this science is the mention of beliefs in detail; for the danger of ignorance in it is great, so one is not satisfied with a general over a specific, nor with a necessary over a necessary. And know that there is disagreement: it is said that dhuhul and ghafla are synonymous. And it is said that ghafla is more general than dhuhul; because dhuhul is lack of knowledge of a thing with prior knowledge of it, and ghafla is lack of knowledge of a thing absolutely. And this is what appeared to the author. Hashiya of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'that which is legally disliked (makruh') – and likewise the prohibited (haram) and permissible (mubah). Taqrirat of al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'from the conjunction of the specific to the general' – there is a problem with using 'aw' (or) for that, unless it is made to mean 'waw' (and). His saying: 'and this is what appeared to the author' refers to the second opinion; therefore, it is appropriate to interpret the matn according to it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and explanation of the term 'karaha' (dislike) in the context of divine attributes. It explains that the author explicitly defines karaha as 'absence of His will,' anticipating possible misunderstanding that it might mean the legal sense (request to leave). He does this to caution against the Mu'tazili error of equating will with command. Then it discusses the inclusion of 'with dhuhul or ghafla' as specific instances of karaha. An objection is raised that if they are included, they are superfluous. The answer is that in theological matters, detailed mention is necessary due to the gravity of ignorance. Then it reports a disagreement on the relationship between dhuhul and ghafla: some say they are synonymous; others say ghafla is more general, and dhuhul is forgetfulness after knowing. The author opts for the second. Finally, al-Ajhuri expands that makruh, haram, and mubah are all not willed (according to the Ash'ari view). Al-Anbabi comments on the grammatical issue of using 'or' for the specific and general, suggesting it means 'and,' and notes that the matn should be understood according to the second opinion.

Key Terms
كراهة (karaha)
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karaha means God dislikes something in a human emotional sense, or that it always refers to the legal definition.
Correction: In divine attribute context, karaha simply means absence of will, not human-like dislike or legal request.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of karaha (dislike) attributed to Allah?
A) The request to leave a thing non-assertively
B) The absence of His will for a thing
C) Divine hatred
D) The absence of His command

Key Takeaway
Karaha in divine context means absence of will, and there is no necessary link between command and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of God that feels difficult, do you ever secretly doubt that God truly wills it for you? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to obey even when you cannot see the will behind it?
?
Think of a time you desired something that was not commanded or even prohibited. How does the truth that God may will without commanding (like death) reshape your understanding of His sovereignty and your own desires?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded a neat logical system where God's will always matches His command. Are there areas in your own faith where you insist on tidy explanations, leaving no room for divine mystery? What would it cost you to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot connect the dots?

Inclusion of Dhuhul and Ghafla and Their Distinction
Defense of detailed mention and distinction of dhuhul/ghafla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dhuhul (heedlessness) and ghafla (forgetfulness) are specifically mentioned as cases of karaha, and the scholarly disagreement on their relationship.

Core Idea
Specific instances of karaha are detailed because theological ignorance is dangerous.

The passage now explains why the author added 'with dhuhul or ghafla' and similar phrases despite them already being included in the general definition of karaha. It defends detailed exposition in theology. Then it addresses a disagreement about whether dhuhul and ghafla are synonyms or if ghafla is more general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection: if dhuhul/ghafla are already included in karaha, why mention them separately?
This is a common logical concern.
2
Understand the answer
The answer: in theology, detail is necessary because ignorance is grave; general cannot substitute for specific.
The author cites 'purpose of this science'.
3
Analyze the disagreement on dhuhul vs. ghafla
Two opinions: (1) synonymous, (2) ghafla is broader (absence of knowledge) while dhuhul is specific (absence after prior knowledge).
The author prefers the second opinion.
4
Reflect on marginal comments
Al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi add that makruh, haram, mubah are all not willed, and clarify the grammar of 'aw' (or) meaning 'and'.
These reinforce the matn interpretation.

Impossibility of Ignorance for God: Compound and Simple Ignorance
Sanusi defines the two types of ignorance and states both are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of ignorance and why both are impossible for God, affirming His perfect knowledge.

Core Idea
God cannot be ignorant, whether by false belief or by lack of knowledge.

Sanusi now turns to a negative attribute: ignorance. He distinguishes two forms—compound ignorance (believing what is false) and simple ignorance (lacking knowledge). Both are impossible for God, because His knowledge is perfect and all-encompassing. This brief passage clarifies what we mean when we say God is not ignorant.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice how Sanusi defines each term concisely. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
The phrase 'كَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ' connects to the previous discussion on the impossibility of certain attributes.
2
Understand the two types of ignorance
Identify the definitions: compound ignorance is believing something contrary to reality; simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge. Reflect on the difference.
Compound ignorance is a positive belief that is false; simple ignorance is a privation.
3
Grasp the ruling: both are impossible for God
Sanusi states both types are impossible for God. This is because God's knowledge is perfect—He cannot have false beliefs nor lack any knowledge.
This upholds the divine attribute of omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْجَهْلُ) أي: سواء كان مركباً: وهو اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه، أو بسيطاً وهو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء
(His saying: 'And likewise ignorance is impossible for Him, exalted is He') i.e., whether it is compound: which is believing something contrary to what it is, or simple: which is absence of knowledge of something.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage states that ignorance is impossible for God and defines two types. Compound ignorance is believing something contrary to reality, while simple ignorance is simply lacking knowledge. Both are impossible for God, affirming His perfect knowledge.

Key Terms

جهل بسيط
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that simple ignorance is not blameworthy, so it might be possible for God because it is just a lack.
Correction: The passage explicitly states both types are impossible for God. Even the absence of knowledge contradicts His perfect omniscience.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is compound ignorance (الجهل المركب)?
A) Not knowing something.
B) Believing something contrary to what it actually is.
C) Doubting something.
D) Ignoring something.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is perfect, so both false belief (compound ignorance) and lack of knowledge (simple ignorance) are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be ignorant—neither by false belief nor by lack of knowledge—then what does it mean for you when you try to hide your sins from Him? Are you acting as if He is ignorant of what you do? How will you change that today?

Consider a time when you insisted on a belief without verifying it from revelation. Did you realize that compound ignorance (believing something contrary to reality) is impossible for God, yet you as a creature are susceptible to it? How does that contrast with God's perfection, and what step will you take to correct any false belief?
?
The passage defines simple ignorance as 'absence of knowledge.' If you neglect to learn what God has commanded, are you not choosing a state that is impossible for God? What commitment will you make this week to seek knowledge that you have been avoiding?

Death, Deafness, Blindness, Muteness: Existential Attributes According to Ahl al-Sunna
Al-Bajuri explains the Sunni view that death, deafness, blindness, muteness are existential attributes, with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni and Mu'tazili views on death, deafness, blindness, and muteness, and the Quranic evidence for the Sunni position.

Core Idea
For Ahl al-Sunna, death is a created existent, not a mere absence.

This lesson examines a key theological dispute: are privative attributes like death real entities or mere absences? Al-Bajuri presents the Sunni position that they are existential entities opposed to their positive counterparts, and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili view that they are privations. The Quranic verse 'He created death and life' serves as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its structure
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing that each of the four attributes (death, deafness, blindness, muteness) is treated in the same repetitive pattern. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the Sunni definition for death
Focus on death: Ahl al-Sunna say it is an existential entity (amr wujudi) that opposes life, while the Mu'tazila say it is the absence of life. Notice the contrast type: opposites vs. privation/possession.
3
Examine the Quranic proof and the Mu'tazili response
The Sunni evidence is from Quran 67:2, 'He created death and life', because creation applies to existents. The Mu'tazila respond by interpreting 'creation' as 'decree' (taqdir), which can apply to non-existents.
4
Apply the same pattern to deafness, blindness, muteness
Notice that the same structure is repeated exactly for deafness/hearing, blindness/sight, and muteness/speech. Ahl al-Sunna consider each existential, Mu'tazila each privative.
5
Reflect on why this distinction matters
This dispute affects how we understand God's creation and attributes. For Ahl al-Sunna, even privations like death are created by Allah, affirming His absolute sovereignty.
This connects to broader themes in kalam about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمَوْتُ) هو أمرٌ وجودِيٌّ يُضَادُّ الحياةَ عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ الحياة عما من شأنه أن يكون حيّاً. والتقابل بينه وبين الحياة من تقابل الصِّدِّين على الأول، ومن تقابل العَدَم والملكة على الثاني. ويدلُّ للأول قوله تعالى: خَلَقَ الْمَوْتَ وَالْحَيَوَةَ ) (۱) لأن الخلق إنما يتعلق بالأمر الوجودي. وأجيب من جهة القائلين بالثاني بأن المراد بالخلق التقديرُ ، وهو كما يكون للوجودي يكون للعدمي. (قوله: وَالصَّمَمُ) هو أمرٌ وجودي يُضَادُّ السمع عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ السمع عما من شأنه أن يكون سميعاً. والتقابل بينه وبين السمع من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْعَمَى) هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ البصر عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ البصر عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. والتقابل بينه وبين البصر من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْبَكَمُ هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ الكلام عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة: فهو عَدَمُ الكلام عما من شأنه أن يكون متكلّماً، والتقابل بينه وبين الكلام من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني.
(His saying: 'and death') is an existential entity that opposes life according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of life from that which is capable of being living. The contrast between it and life is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. And the first view is supported by His saying, exalted: 'He created death and life' (Surah al-Mulk, 2) because creation is only related to an existential entity. And it was answered from the proponents of the second view that by 'creation' is meant 'decree', and that is applicable to the existential as well as the non-existential. (His saying: 'and deafness') is an existential entity that opposes hearing according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of hearing from that which is capable of being hearing. The contrast between it and hearing is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. (His saying: 'and blindness') is an existential entity that opposes sight according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of sight from that which is capable of being seeing. The contrast between it and sight is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. (His saying: 'and muteness') is an existential entity that opposes speech according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of speech from that which is capable of being speaking. The contrast between it and speech is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a series of definitions and doctrinal comparisons regarding four attributes: death (mawt), deafness (samam), blindness (ama), and muteness (bakam). For each, Al-Bajuri states the position of Ahl al-Sunna (the Sunni school) and the Mu'tazila. According to Ahl al-Sunna, each of these is an existential entity (amr wujudi) that is the opposite (didd) of its corresponding positive attribute: death opposes life, deafness opposes hearing, blindness opposes sight, muteness opposes speech. According to the Mu'tazila, they are purely privations (adam) of those positive attributes. Thus, the relationship is one of opposition (tadadd) on the Sunni view, and privation/posession (adam wa mulka) on the Mu'tazili view. The passage then provides evidence for the Sunni view from the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (Surah al-Mulk:2), arguing that creation applies to existential things. It also presents the Mu'tazili response that 'creation' here means 'decree' (taqdir), which can apply to non-existents as well. The same pattern is repeated for deafness, blindness, and muteness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is merely the absence of life (Mu'tazili view).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna hold that death is an existential entity created by Allah, as evidenced by the Quranic verse 'He created death and life'.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, is death an existential attribute or a privation?
A) Existential attribute.
B) Privation of life.
C) Both existential and privation.
D) Neither existential nor privation.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that death, deafness, blindness, and muteness are real created entities that oppose their positive counterparts, as supported by the Quranic statement that God created death and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If death is a created existent that opposes life, how does that reshape your perspective on your own mortality? Recognizing that death is a real creation of God, are you actively preparing for it, or do you live as if it is merely an end to life? What concrete step will you take this week to remember death as a created reality?
?
Deafness, blindness, and muteness are described as existential opposites to hearing, sight, and speech. In what areas of your spiritual life are you 'deaf' to God's commands, 'blind' to His signs, or 'mute' in proclaiming His truth? Knowing that these are real deficiencies created by God as tests or punishments, how will you plead to Him to remove them from your heart, not just your body?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that 'creation' in the verse means 'decree' to avoid admitting death is an existent. Are you tempted to reinterpret clear texts of the Quran to fit your own assumptions about reality? Think of an area where you might be rationalizing away a difficult truth. Will you submit to the apparent meaning of the Quran, even when it challenges your intellectual comfort?

Are Death and Similar Attributes Positive or Absences?
Bajuri defines four attributes with Sunni vs Mu'tazila views and a proof for death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that death, deafness, blindness, and muteness are positive attributes (existents) that oppose their respective counterparts, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila view that they are mere absences.

Core Idea
Death is not nothing; it is a real created opposite of life.

In this passage, Bajuri explains a key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazila theology concerning divine attributes. The Sunnis affirm that death, deafness, blindness, and muteness are real, existent things (positive entities) that stand in opposition to life, hearing, sight, and speech. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, consider them mere absences. A Qur'anic proof is given for death, and a special objection is raised and answered for muteness. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Sunni doctrine of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full passage once in Arabic and then the English translation. Notice the repeated structure for each attribute definition.
The passage uses very technical terms like 'وجودي' (existent) and 'عدم' (absence).
2
Identify the Sunni and Mu'tazila definitions
For each attribute (death, deafness, blindness, muteness), note how both schools define it. The Sunni definition uses 'أمر وجودي' (an existent thing), while the Mu'tazila uses 'عدم' (absence).
Also note the type of opposition: 'تضاد' (real opposites) vs 'عدم وملكة' (privation and possession).
3
Examine the Qur'anic proof and response for death
Focus on the part about death. The verse 'خلق الموت والحياة' is used as proof. Understand how the Mu'tazila respond by interpreting 'خلق' as predestination.
This shows how scriptural evidence is used in theological arguments.
4
Analyze the objection on muteness
Read the objection that muteness only opposes verbal speech, not internal divine speech, and the answer that it can be used metaphorically for a defect preventing internal speech.
This nuance resolves a potential inconsistency in the doctrine.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters: If these attributes were mere absences, they would be uncreated, which conflicts with the Sunni belief that everything other than God is created.

(قوله: وَالْمَوْتُ) هو أمرٌ وجودِيٌّ يُضَادُّ الحياةَ عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ الحياة عما من شأنه أن يكون حيّاً. والتقابل بينه وبين الحياة من تقابل الصِّدِّين على الأول، ومن تقابل العَدَم والملكة على الثاني. ويدلُّ للأول قوله تعالى: خَلَقَ الْمَوْتَ وَالْحَيَوَةَ ) (۱) لأن الخلق إنما يتعلق بالأمر الوجودي. وأجيب من جهة القائلين بالثاني بأن المراد بالخلق التقديرُ ، وهو كما يكون للوجودي يكون للعدمي. (قوله: وَالصَّمَمُ) هو أمرٌ وجودي يُضَادُّ السمع عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ السمع عما من شأنه أن يكون سميعاً. والتقابل بينه وبين السمع من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْعَمَى) هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ البصر عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ البصر عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. والتقابل بينه وبين البصر من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْبَكَمُ هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ الكلام عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة: فهو عَدَمُ الكلام عما من شأنه أن يكون متكلّماً، والتقابل بينه وبين الكلام من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. واعتُرِضَ على المصنف بأن البَكَم إنما يضادُّ الكلام اللفظي، لا الكلام النفسي الذي كلامنا فيه، وأجيب بأن البكم كما يطلق حقيقةً على: آفَةٍ تمنع من الكلام اللفظي؛ يُطلَقُ مجازاً على آفةٍ تمنعُ من الكلام النفسي، وذلك هو المراد هنا.
His statement: 'and death' is an existent thing that opposes life according to Ahl al-Sunna. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of life in something that is by nature living. The opposition between them is of the type of opposites according to the first view, and of the type of privation and possession according to the second. And in support of the first view is His statement (Exalted): 'He created death and life' (1) because creation is only related to an existent thing. And it was answered from the side of those who hold the second view that by creation is meant predestination, which applies to nonexistent as well as existent. (His statement:) 'and deafness' is an existent thing that opposes hearing according to Ahl al-Sunna. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of hearing in something that is by nature hearing. The opposition between them is of the type of opposites according to the first, and of privation and possession according to the second. (His statement:) 'and blindness' is an existent thing that opposes sight according to Ahl al-Sunna. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of sight in something that is by nature seeing. The opposition between them is of the type of opposites according to the first, and of privation and possession according to the second. (His statement:) 'and muteness' is an existent thing that opposes speech according to Ahl al-Sunna. And according to the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of speech in something that is by nature speaking. The opposition between them is of the type of opposites according to the first, and of privation and possession according to the second. And an objection was raised against the author that muteness only opposes verbal speech, not internal speech which is our topic. And it was answered that muteness, just as it is used literally for a defect that prevents verbal speech, it is used metaphorically for a defect that prevents internal speech, and that is what is meant here.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines four attributes: death, deafness, blindness, and muteness, and presents the Sunni view that they are existent things (امور وجودية) that are opposites of life, hearing, sight, and speech respectively, while the Mu'tazila view them as mere absences (عدم) of those faculties. The author then discusses the type of opposition: according to the Sunni view, it is opposition between two real existents (تضاد), while according to the Mu'tazila it is opposition between existence and non-existence (عدم وملكة). For death, a proof from Qur'an (67:2) is given, and a response from the Mu'tazila is mentioned. For muteness, an objection is raised that muteness only opposes verbal speech, not the internal speech attribute of God. The answer clarifies that muteness can refer metaphorically to a defect preventing internal speech.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni school affirms that death, deafness, blindness, and muteness are positive, created opposites of their respective attributes, not mere privations.

?

Where in your life might you be treating a sin or a deficiency as a mere 'lack of good' (a Mu'tazila view) rather than as a real, active opposite that must be removed? How would your repentance change if you saw your sin as a real entity opposing faith?
?
This lesson clarifies that deafness, blindness, and muteness are existent opposites for Sunnis. Opposites of the Ma'nawiyya Attributes Derived from Attributes of Meanings
Bajuri explains the logical derivation of opposites for ma'nawiyya attributes and clarifies a pronoun reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opposites of the ma'nawiyya attributes are logically derived from the opposites of the attributes of meanings, and why a grammatical clarification is needed to avoid misinterpretation.

Core Idea
The opposites of divine attributes follow a logical chain from attributes of meanings.

In this passage, Bajuri explains the logical derivation of opposites for the ma'nawiyya attributes. He shows that if you know the opposite of a sifa ma'nawiyya (attribute of meaning), you automatically know the opposite of the corresponding sifa ma'nawiyya (the attributive state). Additionally, he clarifies a potential grammatical ambiguity in the matn regarding what the demonstrative pronoun refers to.

Guided Path
1
Understand the attribute distinctions
First, recall that divine attributes include 'attributes of meanings' (صفات المعاني) like power, will, knowledge, and 'ma'nawiyya attributes' (الصفات المعنوية) which are the states resulting from those, like being powerful, willing, knowing.
The ma'nawiyya attributes are not additional attributes but the very state of having the attribute of meaning.
2
Follow the derivation of opposites
Bajuri gives an example: if the opposite of power is incapacity, then the opposite of being powerful is being incapable. The same applies to will/aversion, etc. This shows a direct logical connection.
The pattern holds for all eight attributes of meanings and their corresponding ma'nawiyya attributes.
3
Examine the grammatical clarification
Bajuri then explains that the demonstrative pronoun 'هذه' in the matn refers to the opposites of the attributes of meanings, not the attributes themselves, as understood from al-Saktani.
If it referred to the attributes themselves, the phrase would require an omitted noun, which is less apparent.
4
Reflect on theological precision
This clarification shows the importance of precise language in theology to avoid misunderstanding the logical relationships between divine attributes and their opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَضْدَادُ الصَّفَاتِ الْمَعْنَويَّةِ وَاضِحَةٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ) أي: لأنك إذا علمت أن ضد القدرة العجزُ؛ علمتَ أن ضدَّ كونه قادراً كونه عاجزاً، وإذا علمت أن ضدَّ الإرادة الكراهة؛ علمتَ أن ضدَّ كونه مريداً كونه كارهاً، وهكذا. وعُلِمَ مما تقرر أن اسم الإشارة في كلام المصنف راجع لأضداد صفات المعاني، وهو ما يُؤْخَذُ من كلام السكتاني؛ وإن كان كلام بعضهم صريحاً في أنه راجع لصفات المعاني؛ لأنه يُخْوجُ إلى تقدير مضاف بأن يقال: واضحة من أضداد هذه، مع كونه خلاف المتبادر من كلام المصنف فتدبر
His statement: 'And the opposites of the ma'nawiyya attributes are clear from these' meaning: Because if you know that the opposite of power is incapacity, you know that the opposite of His being powerful is His being incapable; and if you know that the opposite of will is aversion, you know that the opposite of His being willing is His being averse; and so on. And it is understood from what has been established that the demonstrative pronoun in the author's speech refers to the opposites of the attributes of meanings, and this is taken from the speech of al-Saktani. Even though the speech of some is explicit that it refers to the attributes of meanings, that leads to estimating an omitted noun such that one says 'clear from the opposites of these', despite that being contrary to what is apparent from the author's speech, so reflect.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'the opposites of the ma'nawiyya attributes are clear from these.' The ma'nawiyya attributes are the attributive states like being powerful (القادرية) etc. Their opposites are derived from the opposites of the original attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني). For example, if the opposite of power is incapacity, then the opposite of being powerful is being incapable. The commentator then clarifies a grammatical point: the demonstrative 'هذه' in the matn refers to the opposites (أضداد) of the attributes of meanings, not to the attributes themselves, as understood from al-Saktani. If it referred to the attributes themselves, it would require a different interpretation.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية
صفات المعاني
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the demonstrative pronoun 'هذه' refers to the attributes of meanings themselves.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it refers to the opposites of the attributes of meanings, based on al-Saktani's interpretation and the consistent reading of the matn.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the demonstrative pronoun 'هذه' refer to in the matn?
A) The attributes of meanings themselves.
B) The opposites of the attributes of meanings.
C) The ma'nawiyya attributes.

Key Takeaway
The opposites of ma'nawiyya attributes are directly inferred from the opposites of attributes of meanings, and precise pronoun reference avoids theological confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that Allah's being powerful excludes all incapacity, how does this truth confront your feelings of helplessness in difficult situations? Will you commit to reciting 'Allah is powerful over all things' the next time you feel weak, and trust that He is not incapable?

?
If Allah's will is never tainted by aversion (karaha), what does that demand of you when you face trials you dislike? Are you resisting His decree in any area of your life? Write down one specific matter you will surrender to His will this week, and ask Him to give you contentment.

The Permissible in God's Rights: Definition and Scope
Bajuri defines the permissible for God and contrasts its scope with the necessary and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the category of the permissible in God's rights is limited to doing or leaving every possible action, unlike the necessary and impossible categories.

Core Idea
The permissible for God is exhaustive: any action He may do or leave is a single category.

Bajuri now explains the third category: the permissible. He points out why the author phrased it differently from the necessary and impossible—because the permissible is limited to the actions mentioned, while the other categories are not. This lesson clarifies the scope of divine permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ja'iz
Read the opening lines that identify this as the third section and note the author's phrasing choice.
Compare with how the necessary and impossible were introduced.
2
Understand why it is exhaustive
Bajuri explains that the permissible is limited (munhasir) to what is mentioned, whereas the necessary and impossible are not limited.
This is a key difference in the structure of the treatise.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why it matters that the permissible is a closed set: it means any action God does or leaves is equally possible and not intrinsically necessary or impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا الْجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى... إلخ) هذا هو القسم الثالث مما يجب على المكلَّف معرفته، وإنما لم يقل المصنف ومما يجوز في حقه تعالى كما قال: (فمما يجب في حقه (تعالى) ومما يستحيل في حقه تعالى)؛ لأن الجائز في حقه تعالى مُنْحَصِرٌ فيما ذكره، بخلاف كلّ من الواجب والمستحيل في حقه تعالى، فإن كُلاً منهما غيرُ منحصر فيما ذُكِرَ كما علم مما تقدم. واعترض على المصنف بأن الجائز والمُمْكِنَ مترادفان عند المتكلمين وحينئذٍ يكون في كلامه أَخْذُ الشيء في تعريف نفسه، فكأنه قال: وأما الجائز في حقه تعالى، ففِعْلُ كلّ جائز أو تَرْكُهُ، أو: وأما الممكنُ في حقه تعالى ففِعْلُ كلّ ممكن أو تركه، وذلك مُوجِبٌ  ِللدَّوْرِ لتوقف كلّ من المعرَّف والتعريف على الآخر حينئذ. وأجيب عن ذلك بأجوبة، أحسنُها أن كلاً من الجائز والممكن يُطلَقُ ويراد به: حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: أَحْسَنُهَا ... إلخ) حاصله أن الجائز المعرف معناه: الشيء الموصوف بالجواز والممكن الواقع في التعريف معناه الشيء الموصوف بالإمكان الذي هو الجواز، فيجعل الشيء الأول على تعلق القدرة، والشيء الثاني على المقدور، فيحل كلام
His statement: 'As for the permissible in His right, Exalted... etc.' This is the third section of what the responsible person must know. The author did not say 'and what is permissible in His right, Exalted' as he said 'thus what is necessary in His right (Exalted) and what is impossible in His right, Exalted' because the permissible in His right, Exalted, is limited to what he mentioned, unlike each of the necessary and impossible in His right, Exalted, for each of them is not limited to what was mentioned, as known from what preceded. And an objection was raised against the author that 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' are synonyms among the theologians, and in that case his speech involves defining a thing by itself, as if he said: 'As for the permissible in His right, Exalted, it is the doing of every permissible or leaving it' or 'As for the possible in His right, Exalted, it is the doing of every possible or leaving it', and that necessitates circularity because each of the definiendum and definition depends on the other. And it was answered with answers, the best of which is that each of 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' is used and intended to mean: —(marginal note of al-Ajhuri: 'His statement: the best of them... etc.')— Its summary is that 'al-ja'iz' as defined means the thing described with permissibility, and 'al-mumkin' occurring in the definition means the thing described with possibility which is permissibility, so the first thing is made to pertain to the attachment of power, and the second thing to the object of power, thus the speech is resolved.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage discusses the third category of what must be known about God: the permissible (al-ja'iz) in God's rights. The author notes that the permissible is exhaustive (حصري) in what was mentioned (i.e., doing or leaving any possible action), unlike the necessary and impossible which are not exhaustive in the attributes listed. An objection is raised that 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, leading to circular definition. The best answer distinguishes between the attribute of possibility (al-ja'iz as a description) and the possible thing (al-mumkin as the object of power). The passage is cut off, but the answer resolves the circularity by taking 'ja'iz' as the description and 'mumkin' as the thing described.

Key Terms
الجائز
الواجب
المستحيل
منحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's permissible actions are unlimited and cannot be exhaustively listed.
Correction: The text clarifies that the permissible is indeed limited to the single category of doing or leaving any possible action; it is exhaustive in that sense.

Checkpoint
Why did the author phrase the permissible differently (وأما الجائز) from the necessary and impossible (فمما يجب / فمما يستحيل)?
A) Because the permissible is more important.
B) Because the permissible is limited to what he mentioned, while the others are not.
C) Because the permissible is not a required knowledge.
D) Because the permissible includes only actions God does.

Key Takeaway
The permissible for God is a single exhaustive category: doing or leaving every possible action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The permissible for God is limited to a single definition: doing or leaving every possible action. This means every event in your life—every blessing, every trial, every missed opportunity—is either allowed or withheld by Him. Are you living today as if nothing escapes His permission, or do you take credit for your successes and bitterly blame yourself for failures? What specific thought or action can you change right now to internalize that ownership?
?
The author deliberately chose different wording to show that necessary and impossible attributes are not limited to a few examples. This means God's majesty is infinitely deeper than any list you can memorize. When you pray, do you truly sense the weight of His vastness, or have you reduced Him to a checklist of ten attributes? What will you do today to remind yourself of the sheer infinity of His perfection?
?
If the permissible is a single exhaustive category, then nothing in your life falls outside God's control. The thing you fear most—the job loss, the illness, the conflict—is only possible if He wills it. How does that knowledge change the way you face that fear tonight? Will you genuinely entrust it to Him, or will you continue carrying it alone? Name one concrete step you will take to surrender that fear to God.

Circularity Objection and Its Resolution
Bajuri presents an objection of circularity against the definition of al-ja'iz and gives the best answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the definition of al-ja'iz is circular because ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms, and the best answer that distinguishes between the attribute and the thing described.

Core Idea
The definition of al-ja'iz avoids circularity by distinguishing the attribute of permissibility from the possible action itself.

An astute objection is raised: since 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms among theologians, defining 'al-ja'iz' as 'doing or leaving every ja'iz' would be circular. Bajuri presents the best answer, which resolves the circularity by differentiating between the descriptive attribute and the object of power. This lesson examines the objection and the key to its solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, making the definition circular.
Notice how the objection paraphrases the definition with 'mumkin' to expose the circularity.
2
Understand why it is circular
If ja'iz and mumkin mean the same, then defining ja'iz in terms of 'every ja'iz' is like defining a thing by itself.
This is a logical pitfall: the definiens uses the definiendum.
3
Learn the best answer
The best answer distinguishes between 'ja'iz' as the attribute of permissibility and 'mumkin' as the thing described with that attribute.
Thus 'ja'iz' in the definition refers to the attribute, and 'mumkin' refers to the possible action, breaking the circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا الْجَائِزُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى... إلخ) هذا هو القسم الثالث مما يجب على المكلَّف معرفته، وإنما لم يقل المصنف ومما يجوز في حقه تعالى كما قال: (فمما يجب في حقه (تعالى) ومما يستحيل في حقه تعالى)؛ لأن الجائز في حقه تعالى مُنْحَصِرٌ فيما ذكره، بخلاف كلّ من الواجب والمستحيل في حقه تعالى، فإن كُلاً منهما غيرُ منحصر فيما ذُكِرَ كما علم مما تقدم. واعترض على المصنف بأن الجائز والمُمْكِنَ مترادفان عند المتكلمين وحينئذٍ يكون في كلامه أَخْذُ الشيء في تعريف نفسه، فكأنه قال: وأما الجائز في حقه تعالى، ففِعْلُ كلّ جائز أو تَرْكُهُ، أو: وأما الممكنُ في حقه تعالى ففِعْلُ كلّ ممكن أو تركه، وذلك مُوجِبٌ  ِللدَّوْرِ لتوقف كلّ من المعرَّف والتعريف على الآخر حينئذ. وأجيب عن ذلك بأجوبة، أحسنُها أن كلاً من الجائز والممكن يُطلَقُ ويراد به: حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: أَحْسَنُهَا ... إلخ) حاصله أن الجائز المعرف معناه: الشيء الموصوف بالجواز والممكن الواقع في التعريف معناه الشيء الموصوف بالإمكان الذي هو الجواز، فيجعل الشيء الأول على تعلق القدرة، والشيء الثاني على المقدور، فيحل كلام
His statement: 'As for the permissible in His right, Exalted... etc.' This is the third section of what the responsible person must know. The author did not say 'and what is permissible in His right, Exalted' as he said 'thus what is necessary in His right (Exalted) and what is impossible in His right, Exalted' because the permissible in His right, Exalted, is limited to what he mentioned, unlike each of the necessary and impossible in His right, Exalted, for each of them is not limited to what was mentioned, as known from what preceded. And an objection was raised against the author that 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' are synonyms among the theologians, and in that case his speech involves defining a thing by itself, as if he said: 'As for the permissible in His right, Exalted, it is the doing of every permissible or leaving it' or 'As for the possible in His right, Exalted, it is the doing of every possible or leaving it', and that necessitates circularity because each of the definiendum and definition depends on the other. And it was answered with answers, the best of which is that each of 'al-ja'iz' and 'al-mumkin' is used and intended to mean: —(marginal note of al-Ajhuri: 'His statement: the best of them... etc.')— Its summary is that 'al-ja'iz' as defined means the thing described with permissibility, and 'al-mumkin' occurring in the definition means the thing described with possibility which is permissibility, so the first thing is made to pertain to the attachment of power, and the second thing to the object of power, thus the speech is resolved.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage discusses the third category of what must be known about God: the permissible (al-ja'iz) in God's rights. The author notes that the permissible is exhaustive (حصري) in what was mentioned (i.e., doing or leaving any possible action), unlike the necessary and impossible which are not exhaustive in the attributes listed. An objection is raised that 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are synonyms, leading to circular definition. The best answer distinguishes between the attribute of possibility (al-ja'iz as a description) and the possible thing (al-mumkin as the object of power). The passage is cut off, but the answer resolves the circularity by taking 'ja'iz' as the description and 'mumkin' as the thing described.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممكن
الدور
المعرَّف
التعريف
تعلق القدرة
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin' are perfectly interchangeable without any distinction.
Correction: The best answer shows that 'ja'iz' can refer to the object (the possible action) while 'mumkin' refers to the attribute (possibility), so they are not identical in all contexts.

Checkpoint
According to the best answer, how is the circularity resolved?
A) By denying that ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms.
B) By distinguishing between the attribute of possibility and the possible action.
C) By redefining the permissible as only actions God does.
D) By removing the word 'ja'iz' from the definition.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-ja'iz is saved from circularity by distinguishing the attribute of permissibility from the possible action itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own core beliefs or purpose, have you ever caught yourself using circular reasoning—defining something by itself? How can you ensure your foundations are truly clear?
?
This lesson shows that a potential misunderstanding was avoided by a precise distinction. Think of a conflict you’ve had where both sides used the same word differently. Could you commit to seeking that kind of clarity next time, even if it’s uncomfortable?
?
Recognizing the difference between an attribute and the thing it describes helped resolve a logical problem. In your own life, are there areas where you confuse a quality you have (like being ‘good’) with your actual actions? What would it take to see the difference?

Defining Obligation and the Best
Al-Bajuri defines wujūb, jawāz, al-salāh, and al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili concept of obligation as impossibility of non-occurrence, the Sunni concept of permissibility, and the distinction between al-salāh and al-aslah.

Core Idea
Obligation for God would make His actions subject to fault; permissibility upholds His freedom.

Before engaging with the famous story of the three brothers, we need to grasp the key terms that frame the debate: what the Mu'tazila mean by 'obligation' (wujūb) and what Ahl al-Sunna mean by 'permissibility' (jawāz). Al-Bajuri also defines two related concepts: al-salāh (the beneficial) and al-aslah (the most beneficial). These definitions are the foundation for understanding why the Ash'ari argument refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage that defines wujūb from the Mu'tazili perspective and jawāz from the Sunni perspective. Notice the contrast: one is 'impossibility of non-occurrence', the other is 'no defect in act or omission'.
The Mu'tazili definition implies God must do certain things; the Sunni definition says He is free to act or not.
2
Identify the two types of 'best'
The passage then defines al-salāh and al-aslah. The first is opposed to corruption (e.g., faith vs. disbelief), the second is opposed to mere benefit (e.g., delicious vs. plain food). Note that some scholars say they are identical.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself: what is at stake in these definitions? For the Mu'tazila, obligation restricts God's action; for Ahl al-Sunna, God's actions are free from any obligation, and His wisdom determines what He does.
This sets the stage for the story that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلَاح وَالْأَصْلَح معنى الوجوب عندهم: عدم إمكان التخلف؛ لأن التخلف يعد خطأ، والله تعالى منزه عنه. ومعنى جواز ذلك عند أهل السنة: أنه لا خلل في فعله حتى يكون محالاً، ولا في تركه حتى يكون واجباً.
والأول هو ما قابَلَ الفساد، كالإيمان في مقابلة الكُفْر ، والصحة في مقابلة المرض. والثاني: هو ما قابَلَ الصَّلاح، كإطعامه أطعمة لذيذةً في مقابلة إطعامه أطعمة غير لذيذة. وقيل: هما شيء واحد.
وقد حكي أنه وَقَعَتِ المباحَثَةُ بين الشيخ أبي الحسن الأَشْعَرِي وبين أبي عَلِيّ الجُبَّائي، فسأله الشيخ عن ثلاثة إخوة: عاش أحدهم في الطاعة حتى مات كبيراً، وعاش الثاني في المعصية حتى مات كبيراً، والآخرُ مات صغيراً، فقال: يُثَابُ الأول، ويعاقب الثاني، والآخرُ لا يُثَابُ ولا يعاقَبُ. قال الشيخ: قد يقول الثالث: يا رَبِّ، هلا أَعْمَرْتَني، فاشتغل بالطاعة حتى أَثَابَ، قال الجبائي: يقول الله تعالى له: علمتُ أنك لو عِشْتَ لاشتغلت بالمعصية، فتعاقب قال الأشعري قد يقول الثاني: يارب، لِمَ لم تُمِثنِي صغيراً حتى لا أعصي، فلا أعاقب، فبُهِتَ الجُبَّائِيُّ.
Regarding the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ (the beneficial) and al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial): the meaning of obligation according to them [the Muʿtazila] is the impossibility of non-occurrence, because non-occurrence would be considered a fault, and God is exalted above that. And the meaning of permissibility according to Ahl al-Sunna is that there is no defect in His doing it such that it would be impossible, nor in His leaving it such that it would be obligatory. The first is what is opposed to corruption, like faith in opposition to disbelief, and health in opposition to sickness. The second is what is opposed to benefit, like His feeding delicious foods in opposition to His feeding non-delicious foods. And it is said: they are one thing. And it has been narrated that a debate occurred between Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī. The Shaykh asked him about three brothers: one lived in obedience until he died old, the second lived in disobedience until he died old, and the third died young. He [al-Jubbāʾī] said: The first will be rewarded, the second punished, and the third neither rewarded nor punished. The Shaykh said: The third could say, 'O Lord, why did You not let me live so I could engage in obedience and be rewarded?' Al-Jubbāʾī said: God would say to him, 'I knew that if you had lived, you would have engaged in disobedience and been punished.' Al-Ashʿarī said: The second could say, 'O Lord, why did You not make me die young so I would not sin and be punished?' Then al-Jubbāʾī was confounded.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage contains a definition and a proof. First, al-Bajuri defines the Muʿtazili concept of wujūb (obligation) as the impossibility of non-occurrence, and contrasts it with the Sunni view that God's actions are permissible (jāʾiz), not obligatory. Then he defines al-ṣalāḥ and al-aṣlaḥ: the former is what is opposed to fasād (corruption), like faith versus disbelief; the latter is what is opposed to mere benefit, like giving delicious versus plain food. He mentions that some say they are the same. Then he presents the famous argument of al-Ashʿarī against al-Jubbāʾī using the example of three brothers: if God were obligated to do what is most beneficial, He would have to make the second brother die young to avoid sin, but He did not, which would be unjust. The argument shows that the Muʿtazili position leads to absurdity, and al-Jubbāʾī was left speechless.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الجواز
الصلاح
الأصلح
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'the most beneficial' (al-aslah) simply means 'good' and is always required of God.
Correction: The passage shows that al-aslah is defined relative to benefit, not to corruption. The Sunni view is that God is not obligated to do even al-aslah; He may do what is merely beneficial (al-salah) or even less.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the meaning of 'obligation' (wujūb) regarding God's actions?
A) God must do what is most beneficial.
B) It is the impossibility of non-occurrence.
C) God may do or not do without defect.
D) It is the same as permissibility.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili claim of obligation on God is refuted by the Sunni concept of permissibility: God is not bound by any external duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you ever feel that God owes you relief? How does the Sunni teaching on jawāz (permissibility) challenge that sense of entitlement, and what would it look like to truly release that expectation?
?
Think of a time you believed God 'should' have given you something better. In light of this lesson's distinction between al-ṣalāḥ and al-aṣlaḥ, can you trust that His choice of the beneficial over the most beneficial is not a fault but a perfect act of wisdom? Will you consciously surrender that judgment today?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God must do what is best for His creation, restricting His freedom. Examine your heart: Do you hold an implicit belief that God's actions must meet your standard of 'best'? Are you willing to let go of demanding that from Him and instead accept His permissibility as perfect? What specific area of your life requires this surrender right now?

The Three Brothers: Ash'ari's Refutation
Al-Ash'ari's dialectical argument against al-Jubbā'ī on the obligation of the most beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari's story of the three brothers exposes the contradiction in the Mu'tazili position that God must always do what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
If God were obligated to do the best, He would be unjust to the sinner who could have died innocent.

Having understood the key terms, we now turn to the famous argument that al-Ash'ari used against the Mu'tazili scholar al-Jubbā'ī. This story is a classic example of dialectical reasoning in kalām. It shows that if God were obliged to create the most beneficial circumstances for every person, then His actions would lead to absurdities. Watch how al-Ash'ari turns al-Jubbā'ī's own logic against him.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the debate. Visualize the three brothers: one obedient, one disobedient, one died young. Al-Jubbā'ī gives their fates. Then al-Ash'ari raises an objection from the perspective of the third brother.
Notice that al-Jubbā'ī answers the first objection, but al-Ash'ari follows up with a second one that leaves him speechless.
2
Analyze the logic of the second objection
If the third brother could complain 'Why didn't You let me live to obey?', then the second brother (the sinner) could say 'Why didn't You make me die young so I wouldn't sin and be punished?' Al-Ash'ari shows that al-Jubbā'ī's own principle (that God must do the most beneficial) would force God to make the sinner die young, which did not happen.
This reveals an inconsistency: if God is obligated to do the best, He should have made the sinner die young, but He didn't.
3
Understand why al-Jubba'i was confounded
Al-Jubbā'ī could not answer because any answer would either violate his principle or blame God for injustice. The story demonstrates that the Mu'tazili position is self-contradictory.
The argument does not prove that God is unjust; it proves that He is not bound by an external obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلَاح وَالْأَصْلَح معنى الوجوب عندهم: عدم إمكان التخلف؛ لأن التخلف يعد خطأ، والله تعالى منزه عنه. ومعنى جواز ذلك عند أهل السنة: أنه لا خلل في فعله حتى يكون محالاً، ولا في تركه حتى يكون واجباً.
والأول هو ما قابَلَ الفساد، كالإيمان في مقابلة الكُفْر ، والصحة في مقابلة المرض. والثاني: هو ما قابَلَ الصَّلاح، كإطعامه أطعمة لذيذةً في مقابلة إطعامه أطعمة غير لذيذة. وقيل: هما شيء واحد.
وقد حكي أنه وَقَعَتِ المباحَثَةُ بين الشيخ أبي الحسن الأَشْعَرِي وبين أبي عَلِيّ الجُبَّائي، فسأله الشيخ عن ثلاثة إخوة: عاش أحدهم في الطاعة حتى مات كبيراً، وعاش الثاني في المعصية حتى مات كبيراً، والآخرُ مات صغيراً، فقال: يُثَابُ الأول، ويعاقب الثاني، والآخرُ لا يُثَابُ ولا يعاقَبُ. قال الشيخ: قد يقول الثالث: يا رَبِّ، هلا أَعْمَرْتَني، فاشتغل بالطاعة حتى أَثَابَ، قال الجبائي: يقول الله تعالى له: علمتُ أنك لو عِشْتَ لاشتغلت بالمعصية، فتعاقب قال الأشعري قد يقول الثاني: يارب، لِمَ لم تُمِثنِي صغيراً حتى لا أعصي، فلا أعاقب، فبُهِتَ الجُبَّائِيُّ.
Regarding the obligation of al-ṣalāḥ (the beneficial) and al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial): the meaning of obligation according to them [the Muʿtazila] is the impossibility of non-occurrence, because non-occurrence would be considered a fault, and God is exalted above that. And the meaning of permissibility according to Ahl al-Sunna is that there is no defect in His doing it such that it would be impossible, nor in His leaving it such that it would be obligatory. The first is what is opposed to corruption, like faith in opposition to disbelief, and health in opposition to sickness. The second is what is opposed to benefit, like His feeding delicious foods in opposition to His feeding non-delicious foods. And it is said: they are one thing. And it has been narrated that a debate occurred between Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī. The Shaykh asked him about three brothers: one lived in obedience until he died old, the second lived in disobedience until he died old, and the third died young. He [al-Jubbāʾī] said: The first will be rewarded, the second punished, and the third neither rewarded nor punished. The Shaykh said: The third could say, 'O Lord, why did You not let me live so I could engage in obedience and be rewarded?' Al-Jubbāʾī said: God would say to him, 'I knew that if you had lived, you would have engaged in disobedience and been punished.' Al-Ashʿarī said: The second could say, 'O Lord, why did You not make me die young so I would not sin and be punished?' Then al-Jubbāʾī was confounded.

Commentary
This passage contains a definition and a proof. First, al-Bajuri defines the Muʿtazili concept of wujūb (obligation) as the impossibility of non-occurrence, and contrasts it with the Sunni view that God's actions are permissible (jāʾiz), not obligatory. Then he defines al-ṣalāḥ and al-aṣlaḥ: the former is what is opposed to fasād (corruption), like faith versus disbelief; the latter is what is opposed to mere benefit, like giving delicious versus plain food. He mentions that some say they are the same. Then he presents the famous argument of al-Ashʿarī against al-Jubbāʾī using the example of three brothers: if God were obligated to do what is most beneficial, He would have to make the second brother die young to avoid sin, but He did not, which would be unjust. The argument shows that the Muʿtazili position leads to absurdity, and al-Jubbāʾī was left speechless.

Key Terms
الأشعري
الجُبَّائي
ثلاثة إخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari argument proves that God can be unjust.
Correction: The argument actually proves that the Mu'tazili position (obligation of the best) leads to a contradiction, not that God is unjust. God's actions are not bound by any external obligation.

Checkpoint
Why was al-Jubba'i confounded by the second objection?
A) He realized the third brother's complaint was valid.
B) He could not explain why God did not make the second brother die young without violating his principle of obligation.
C) He admitted that God is unjust.
D) He forgot the answer.

Key Takeaway
The story of the three brothers debunks the Mu'tazili claim that God is obligated to do the most beneficial, exposing a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at people around you—some living long in sin, others dying young—do you ever feel that God's distribution of life and death is unjust? How does this story of the three brothers expose the hidden assumption in your complaint?
?
The second brother could have asked to die young to avoid punishment. Have you ever wished for an easier way out of accountability rather than facing the consequences of your own choices? What does that reveal about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
Al-Jubbā'ī was confounded because his own logic trapped him. In your own life, are there beliefs about God that you hold that, if followed to their end, would lead to contradictions? Will you surrender those assumptions, or will you cling to them like al-Jubbā'ī?

The Permissibility and Reality of Seeing Allah
Sanusi states the permissibility of seeing Allah, its occurrence for believers in the afterlife, and its special occurrence for the Prophet on the night of Isra', as well as the possibility in dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing Allah is rationally permissible, occurs for believers in the Hereafter, for the Prophet on the night of Isra', and is possible in dreams, while being denied in this world for others.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible but restricted to specific contexts: the afterlife for believers, and the Prophet in this world during Isra'.

In this lesson, we explore the attribute of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Sanusi clarifies that while seeing Allah is permissible and occurs for believers in the Hereafter, it is not possible for disbelievers or hypocrites. He also addresses the special case of the Prophet's seeing on the night of Isra' and the possibility of seeing Allah in dreams, as reported by some Sufis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ruling on seeing Allah
Recognize that seeing Allah is classified as permissible (ja'iz) for Allah, meaning it is not impossible or necessary, but possible.
2
Identify who sees Allah in the afterlife
The passage states that seeing Allah occurs for believers in the Hereafter, not for disbelievers (by consensus) nor for hypocrites (according to the correct view).
3
Note the exception for the Prophet
In this world, seeing Allah does not occur except for our Prophet on the night of Isra', according to the preponderant opinion.
Some say he saw with the eye of his heart.
4
Recognize the prohibition for others in wakefulness
Anyone else claiming to see Allah in this world is misguided, as even Musa (as) was prevented from it. This applies only to wakefulness.
5
Consider the possibility in dreams
The passage mentions that seeing Allah in dreams may occur, citing a Sufi anecdote about seeing through inner sight.
The Sufi described it as seeing with the eye of the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجائز في حقه تعالى أيضاً: رؤيته، وهي تقع للمؤمنين في الدار الآخرة، لا للكفار اتفاقاً ولا للمنافقين على الصحيح وأما في الدنيا فلا تقع، نعم وقعت لنبينا ليلة الإسراء على الراجح. وقيل: رآه بعين قلبه فقط. ومن ادعاها ممن سواه فهو ضال مضل، كيف وقد منع منها موسى كليم الله، لكن هذا إنما هو في اليقظة. أما في النوم فقد تقع، وقد ادعى بعض الصوفية أنه رأى ربه في منامه، فقيل له كيف رأيته؟ فقال: انعكس بصري في بصيرتي فرأيت من ليس كمثله شيء.
And among that which is permissible with respect to Him (exalted be He) also is seeing Him. It occurs for the believers in the Hereafter, not for the disbelievers by consensus, nor for the hypocrites according to the correct view. As for this world, it does not occur. Yes, it occurred for our Prophet on the night of Isra' according to the preponderant opinion. It is said: he saw Him only with the eye of his heart. Whoever claims it from among others is misguided and misguiding; how could it be when Musa, the interlocutor of Allah, was prevented from it? But this is only in wakefulness. As for sleep, it may occur. Some Sufis have claimed that they saw their Lord in their sleep. It was said to him: How did you see Him? He said: My sight was reflected in my inner sight, so I saw the One who is unlike anything.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of seeing Allah (ru'ya). It is permissible (ja'iz) for God to be seen. It actually occurs for believers in the Hereafter, but not for disbelievers (by consensus) nor for hypocrites (according to the correct view). In this world, it does not occur, except for our Prophet on the night of Isra' according to the preferred opinion. Some say he saw Him only with the eye of the heart. Anyone else who claims to have seen Allah in this world is misguided and misguiding, since even Moses (kalim Allah) was denied it. However, this prohibition applies only to wakefulness. In dreams, it may occur. Some Sufis have claimed to see their Lord in dreams, and when asked how, they replied that their sight reflected in their inner vision so they saw the One who is unlike anything.

Correction: The passage clarifies that seeing Allah is possible: it occurs for believers in the afterlife and for the Prophet in this world during Isra', and even in dreams it may occur for others, though not in wakefulness.
