The Two-Syllogism Proof for God's Everlastingness
Al-Bajuri presents the two-syllogism proof for God's everlastingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's everlastingness using two syllogisms and why the author omitted one.

Core Idea
The impossibility of non-existence proves God's everlastingness.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the logical structure behind the proof for God's everlastingness (baqa'). The proof requires two syllogisms that together demonstrate that non-existence cannot possibly affect God. Al-Bajuri also clarifies why the original author simplified the presentation by omitting one of the syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two-syllogism argument.
Notice the logical flow: if no everlastingness then possibility of non-existence, but possibility of non-existence is impossible because it would negate pre-eternity.
2
Identify the two syllogisms
Break down the proof into its two conditional syllogisms. The first is: if not everlasting, then possible non-existence. The second is: if possible non-existence, then no pre-eternity; but pre-eternity is established, so possibility is impossible.
The second syllogism uses modus tollens.
3
Understand the author's omission
Al-Bajuri explains that the author (Sanusi) omitted the first syllogism and only gave the conditional of the second, but provided an indication from an earlier proof.
This is a common teaching strategy for conciseness.
4
Reflect on the use of 'possibility'
Consider why the proof uses 'imkan' (possibility) rather than actual non-existence. The commentator explains that impossibility of possibility is a stronger claim.
This subtlety shows precision in rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ... إلخ) هذا البرهانُ لا يتم إلا بقياسين، ونظمُهما هكذا لو لم يَجِبْ له البقاء لأمكن أن يَلْحَقَهُ العدمُ، لكن إمكانُ لُحُوق العدم له محال؛ لأنه لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدمُ لانتفى عنه القِدَمُ، لكنّ انتفاء القِدَم عنه محال. فالمصنفُ حَذَفَ القياس الأوَّلَ، وذكر شَرْطِية القياس الثاني، وحَذَفَ استثنائيته، لكن ذَكَرَ ما هو كالدليل عليها بقوله : (كيف وقد سبق قريباً... إلخ). وأما قوله: (لكون وجوده... إلخ) فتعليل لترتب انتفاء القدم على إمكان لحوق العدم كما لا يخفى. (قوله: لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ إنما عَبْرَ بالإمكان، ولم يقل: لو لَحِقَهُ العدم؛ لأن امتناع إمكان لحوق العدم يستلزم امتناع لحوقه من باب أَوْلى بخلاف عكسه فتدبر (قوله: لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ... إلخ) قد عرفت أنه تعليل لترتُبِ انتفاء القِدَم على إمكان لحوق العدم. (وقوله: حِينَئِذٍ أي حين إذ أمكن أن يلحقه العدم. (قوله: لَا وَاجِباً) توكيد لما قبله كما هو ظاهر.
(His saying: "As for the proof of the necessity of everlastingness for Him, exalted, it is because... etc.") This proof is only completed by two syllogisms, and their arrangement is thus: if everlastingness were not necessary for Him, then non-existence could possibly affect Him; but the possibility of non-existence affecting Him is impossible, because if non-existence could possibly affect Him, then pre-eternity would be negated for Him; but the negation of pre-eternity for Him is impossible. So the author omitted the first syllogism and mentioned the conditional part of the second syllogism, but omitted its categorical part; however, he mentioned what is like a proof for it by his saying (How, and it has recently preceded... etc.). And as for his saying (because His existence... etc.), it is a reason for the ordering of the negation of pre-eternity upon the possibility of non-existence affecting Him, as is not hidden. (His saying: "If it were possible for non-existence to affect Him") He expressed it with 'possibility' and did not say 'if non-existence affected Him', because the impossibility of the possibility of non-existence affecting Him entails the impossibility of its actual occurrence in a stronger way, unlike the converse. So reflect. (His saying: "because His existence... etc.") You have known that it is a reason for the ordering of the negation of pre-eternity upon the possibility of non-existence affecting Him. (And His saying: "then") i.e., when it is possible for non-existence to affect Him. (His saying: "not necessary") is an emphasis of what preceded, as is apparent.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the proof for God's everlastingness (baqa'). The commentator explains that the proof requires two syllogisms. The first is: if everlastingness were not necessary, then non-existence would be possible. The second is: the possibility of non-existence is impossible because it would negate pre-eternity, which is already established as impossible to negate (since God is pre-eternal). The author (Sanusi) omitted the first syllogism and only gave the conditional premise of the second, but he provided an indication of the proof in his saying "How, and it has recently preceded..." referring to an earlier argument. The commentator then explains why the author used the word 'possibility' (imkan) rather than actual occurrence: because the impossibility of the possibility of non-existence entails the impossibility of actual non-existence a fortiori, whereas the converse would not hold. He also clarifies that the phrase "because His existence" is a reason why the negation of pre-eternity follows from the possibility of non-existence. The final notes clarify the meaning of 'then' and that 'not necessary' is an emphasis.

Key Terms
بقاء
قدم
إمكان
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if non-existence is possible, then it occurs; but the argument only needs the possibility to refute pre-eternity.
Correction: The proof uses 'possibility' because the impossibility of possibility entails the impossibility of actual non-existence a fortiori, not because actual non-existence is being claimed.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof for everlastingness use the word 'possibility' (imkan) instead of stating that non-existence actually occurs?
A) Because the impossibility of possibility entails impossibility of actual occurrence a fortiori
B) Because actual non-existence is easier to refute
C) Because the author accidentally omitted the actual occurrence
D) Because possibility is more general and covers both actual and potential

Key Takeaway
God's everlastingness is proven by showing that the possibility of non-existence is impossible because it would contradict His pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that God's non-existence is logically impossible. But do you live as if His existence is a permanent reality, or do you sometimes act as if He is absent? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that He can never cease to exist?
?
When you face trials, do you ever doubt that God is still there? This lesson proves that His existence is absolutely necessary. How will you remind yourself of this truth the next time you feel abandoned or unheard?
?
If God's everlastingness is proven, then He is always present and aware. Are you comfortable with that? Or does the thought that He sees everything you do—and always will—make you uneasy? What would it take for you to welcome His constant presence rather than fear it?

The Proof for Necessary Permanence and Its Implicit Syllogisms
Bajuri explains the logical structure behind Sanusi's proof for necessary permanence and justifies the omission of a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof for God's necessary permanence is structured through two syllogisms, and why the omission of certain parts by Sanusi is justified by implicit references.

Core Idea
A proof can be complete even when parts are omitted, if the omitted parts are implied by what remains.

In this passage, Bajuri unpacks the logical structure behind Sanusi's concise statement on God's necessary permanence. He shows that what appears to be an incomplete proof is actually a complete argument when its implicit syllogisms are recognized. By understanding Bajuri's explanation, you will see how Islamic theologians often present compressed arguments that rely on the reader's familiarity with logical form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how Bajuri describes the two-syllogism structure.
2
Identify the two syllogisms
Bajuri presents two syllogisms. Find where he states the first and second syllogisms. Note that the first is about the impossibility of possible non-existence leading to negation of pre-eternity.
3
Understand Sanusi's omissions
Bajuri says Sanusi omitted the first syllogism and the apodictic conclusion of the second, but provided implicit evidence. Reflect on how 'how has it recently preceded' serves as that evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ... إلخ) هذا البرهانُ لا يتم إلا بقياسين، ونظمُهما هكذا لو لم يَجِبْ له البقاء لأمكن أن يَلْحَقَهُ العدمُ، لكن إمكانُ لُحُوق العدم له محال؛ لأنه لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدمُ لانتفى عنه القِدَمُ، لكنّ انتفاء القِدَم عنه محال. فالمصنفُ حَذَفَ القياس الأوَّلَ، وذكر شَرْطِية القياس الثاني، وحَذَفَ استثنائيته، لكن ذَكَرَ ما هو كالدليل عليها بقوله : (كيف وقد سبق قريباً... إلخ). وأما قوله: (لكون وجوده... إلخ) فتعليل لترتب انتفاء القدم على إمكان لحوق العدم كما لا يخفى.
And as for the proof of the necessity of permanence for Him, exalted, it is because... (etc.) This proof is only completed by two syllogisms, and their arrangement is as follows: If permanence were not necessary for Him, then non-existence could attach to Him, but the possibility of non-existence attaching to Him is impossible, because if it were possible for non-existence to attach to Him, then [His] pre-eternity would be negated, but the negation of pre-eternity from Him is impossible. So the author omitted the first syllogism and mentioned the conditional of the second syllogism and omitted its apodictic [conclusion], but he mentioned what serves as evidence for it by his saying: 'How has it recently preceded... etc.' And as for his saying: 'Because His existence... etc.' it is a justification for the entailment of the negation of pre-eternity on the possibility of the attachment of non-existence, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the proof for God's necessary permanence. Bajuri states that the proof requires two syllogisms. He presents the full argument: if permanence were not necessary for God, then non-existence could possibly attach to Him; but that possibility is impossible because if it were possible, it would negate His pre-eternity (qidam), which is impossible. Therefore, permanence is necessary. However, Sanusi only mentioned the conditional premise of the second syllogism and omitted the first syllogism and the explicit conclusion. Bajuri notes that Sanusi's phrase 'how has it recently preceded' serves as an implicit indication of the missing conclusion, and the phrase 'because His existence...' provides the justification for why the possibility of non-existence entails negation of pre-eternity.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
قِدَم (qidam)
إمكان (imkan)
قياس (qiyas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's proof for necessary permanence is incomplete because he omitted a syllogism.
Correction: Bajuri explains that Sanusi omitted the first syllogism and the explicit conclusion of the second, but he provided implicit evidence through phrases like 'how has it recently preceded', making the proof complete.

Checkpoint
According to Bajuri, why did Sanusi omit the first syllogism in the proof for necessary permanence?
A) Because the first syllogism is self-evident and does not need to be stated
B) Because he mentioned something that serves as evidence for it
C) Because the proof is only one syllogism
D) Because the first syllogism is invalid

Key Takeaway
The proof for God's necessary permanence relies on two syllogisms, and Sanusi's omission of explicit statements does not make the argument incomplete, as the missing parts are implied by the context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Universal Rule and the Objection of Pre-Eternal Non-Existence
Bajuri derives a universal rule and addresses an objection about pre-eternal non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal rule that whatever is necessarily beginningless cannot cease to exist, and how the objection concerning pre-eternal non-existence is resolved.

Core Idea
The rule about pre-eternal beings applies only to existents, not to non-existence.

Bajuri now extracts a universal principle from the earlier proof of God's pre-eternity: anything that is necessarily beginningless cannot undergo non-existence. He notes that all rational people agree on this rule. But an objector raises the case of our pre-eternal non-existence, which seems to have necessary beginninglessness yet does not cease. Bajuri presents two answers, inviting deeper reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from rule to objection to answer.
The passage uses technical terms like qidam and adam.
2
Locate the universal rule
Find the sentence that states: 'whoever's pre-eternity is necessary, its non-existence is impossible.' Note how it is derived from the earlier proof.
The derivation is explained in the marginal note.
3
Understand the objection
The objection points to our pre-eternal non-existence (adamuna al-azali) – which has necessary beginninglessness yet its non-existence (i.e., ceasing) is not impossible. Why would this be a problem?
The objector claims this violates the universal rule.
4
Examine the two answers
First answer: the rule applies only to existent things (wujudi), not to non-existence. Second answer: some deny the objection entirely by arguing that pre-eternal non-existence cannot cease. But this second answer is criticized.
Bajuri ends with 'tamal' (reflect), indicating room for further thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَريباً وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى يُؤخَذُ من ذلك أن كل من وَجَبَ قِدَمُه استحال عدمه، ولم تتَّفِقِ العُقَلاءُ على مسألة اعتقادية إلهية إلا هذه القاعدة الكلية. وأُورِدَ عليها: عَدَمُنا الأزلي، فإنه وَجَبَ قِدَمُه ولم يَسْتَحِلْ عدمه. وأجيب: بأن القاعدة مفروضة في الوجودي. وبعضهم منع الإيراد من أصله: بأن عَدَمَنا الأزلي يستحيلُ عَدَمُه؛ لأنه لو عُدمَ لوُجِدْنا في الأزل، ووجودنا في الأزل محال؛ لأنه لا يُوجَدُ فيه إلا الله وصفاته، وفيه: أنه إنما يستحيل عَدَمُه في الأزل لما ذكر، وهذا لا ينافي أنه ينعدم بتناهي الأزل، فيصدق عليه أنه وَجَبَ قِدَمُه ولم يَسْتَحِلْ عدمه. فتأمل. (قوله: يُؤخَذُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ... إلخ) وجه الأخذ أنه أثبت العناد بين إمكان لحوق العدم وبين القدم حيث قال: (لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدم لانتفى عنه القدم.)
His saying: 'And it has recently preceded the necessity of His pre-eternity, exalted.' It is taken from that that whoever's pre-eternity is necessary, its non-existence is impossible. And the rational people have not agreed on any doctrinal divine issue except this universal rule. And it was objected against it: our pre-eternal non-existence, for its pre-eternity is necessary and its non-existence is not impossible. And it was answered: that the rule is predicated of the existent. And some prevented the objection from its root: that our pre-eternal non-existence's non-existence is impossible, because if it were non-existent (ceased), we would have existed in pre-eternity, and our existence in pre-eternity is impossible because nothing exists there except God and His attributes. But it is said: its non-existence is only impossible in pre-eternity for the mentioned reason, and this does not contradict that it ceases (comes to an end) at the termination of pre-eternity, so it is true that its pre-eternity is necessary and its non-existence is not impossible. So reflect. (His saying: 'It is taken from that... etc.') The face of the derivation is that he established the opposition between the possibility of the attachment of non-existence and pre-eternity where he said: 'If it were possible for non-existence to attach to Him, then pre-eternity would be negated from Him.'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage extracts a universal rule from the previous proof: whoever has necessary beginninglessness (qidam) cannot undergo non-existence. The author notes that all rational people agree on this except for this one foundational rule. An objection is raised: our pre-eternal non-existence (adamuna al-azali) has necessary beginninglessness (it has always been non-existent) but it does not cease to be non-existent. Two answers are given: first, the rule applies only to existing things (wujudi); second, some deny the objection entirely, arguing that our pre-eternal non-existence is itself impossible to cease because if it ceased we would have existed in eternity, which is impossible. However, this second answer is criticized because it only shows impossibility of ceasing in eternity, but non-existence could still cease at the end of eternity, so it still satisfies the condition of having necessary beginninglessness without impossibility of ceasing. The author ends with 'reflect' (tamal). The marginal note clarifies that the derivation of the rule comes from Sanusi's statement establishing the opposition between possibility of non-existence and pre-eternity.

Key Terms
قِدَم (qidam)
عدم ('adam)
استحالة (istihala)
وجودي (wujudi)
أزلي (azali)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that pre-eternal non-existence violates the universal rule, proving the rule false.
Correction: The rule applies only to existent beings; non-existence is excluded by the first answer.

Checkpoint
How is the objection regarding our pre-eternal non-existence resolved according to the first answer?
A) The rule applies only to existent things, not to non-existence
B) Our pre-eternal non-existence is actually impossible to cease
C) The rule is not universal
D) The objection is invalid because non-existence cannot be described as eternal

Key Takeaway
The universal rule that necessary pre-eternity implies impossibility of non-existence holds only for existent things, not for non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence began in time. According to this lesson, your pre-eternal non-existence was necessary, but it ceased when you were created. Reflect on the fact that you were nothing and God brought you into existence. How does this humble you and increase your gratitude? What will you do today to show that gratitude?
?
The universal rule says that if something is necessarily pre-eternal, it cannot cease. Only God is necessarily pre-eternal. Are you living as if God is the only truly eternal being, or do you cling to things that will inevitably cease? What attachment do you need to break today?
?
Bajuri ends with 'tamal' (reflect). He leaves the objection partially unresolved, teaching that some truths require deep contemplation rather than simple answers. Is your faith built on intellectual certainty or on a willingness to reflect on mysteries? What belief about God are you afraid to think deeply about?

The First Proof: Independence from a Locus
Al-Bajuri explains the first proof for qiyam bi-nafsihi and reconstructs the omitted syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Sanusi's first proof for Allah's self-subsistence, specifically the argument that He cannot need a locus because that would make Him an accident.

Core Idea
Needing a locus would make Allah an accident, which is impossible.

Al-Bajuri reveals the full syllogism behind Sanusi's condensed proof. By unpacking the omitted premises, we see how the impossibility of accidents applies to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Al-Bajuri's explanation of the first proof.
Notice that the passage also introduces the two meanings of self-subsistence.
2
Identify the two meanings of qiyam bi-nafsihi
Sanusi interprets self-subsistence in two ways: independence from a locus and independence from a specifier. This lesson focuses on the first.
3
Reconstruct the full syllogism
Al-Bajuri provides the full logical steps. Try to write them out: conditional premise, then exceptive premise leading to impossibility.
The full argument: If not self-subsistent -> would need a locus -> impossible because that would be an accident.
4
Understand why Sanusi omitted parts
Sanusi condensed the argument by omitting the first syllogism and the exceptive premise, relying on the statement 'the accident does not qualify...' as a shortcut.
This condensation is common in dense theological texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المصنف جَرَى فيما تقدَّم على تفسير قيامه تعالى بنفسه بـ: عدم افتقاره إلى المحلّ، وب: عدم افتقاره إلى المخصص، ولذلك أَفْرَدَ كلّاً بدليل، فاستدل على الأول بقوله: (لو احتاج إلى محل... إلخ)، وعلى الثاني بقوله: (ولو احتاج إلى مخصص... إلخ)، لكن حَذَفَ من كلّ منهما القياس الأول واستثنائية القياس الثاني اكتفاء بدليليهما. ونَظْمُ الدليل الأول هكذا لو لم يكن قائماً بنفسه - أي: مستغنياً عن المحل - لاحتاج إلى محلّ يقوم به، لكن احتياجه إلى محلّ محال؛ لأنه لو احتاج إلى محل لكان صِفَةً، لكن كونه صفة محال. فحَذَفَ المصنف القياس الأول بتمامه، وطوى استثنائية الثاني استغناء عنها بدليلها، وهو قوله: (والصفة لا تتصف... إلخ). ونظم الدليل الثاني هكذا لو لم يكن قائماً بنفسه - أي مستغنياً عن المخصص - لاحتاج إلى مخصص، لكنّ احتياجه إلى مخصص محال؛ لأنه لو احتاج إلى مخصص لكان حادثاً، لكنّ كونَه حادثاً محال. فحَذَفَ المصنف القياس الأول بتمامه، وطوى استثنائية الثاني استغناء عنها بدليلهما، وهو قوله: (كيف وقد قام البرهان... إلخ).
The author proceeded in what preceded to interpret His self-subsistence by: His lack of need for a locus, and: His lack of need for a specifier, and therefore he treated each with a proof. So he adduced for the first with his statement: 'If He needed a locus... etc.', and for the second with his statement: 'If He needed a specifier... etc.', but he omitted from each the first syllogism and the exceptive premise of the second syllogism, contenting himself with their evidences. The arrangement of the first proof is as follows: If He were not self-subsistent – i.e., independent of a locus – He would need a locus in which to subsist, but His need for a locus is impossible, because if He needed a locus He would be an accident, but His being an accident is impossible. So the author omitted the first syllogism entirely, and suppressed the exceptive premise of the second, dispensing with it due to its evidence, which is his statement: 'and the accident does not qualify... etc.'. The arrangement of the second proof is as follows: If He were not self-subsistent – i.e., independent of the specifier – He would need a specifier, but His need for a specifier is impossible, because if He needed a specifier He would be contingent, but His being contingent is impossible. So the author omitted the first syllogism entirely, and suppressed the exceptive premise of the second, dispensing with it due to its evidence, which is his statement: 'How, while the proof has been established... etc.'.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage summarises Sanusi's proof for the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyam bi-nafsihi) of Allah. Al-Bajuri explains that Sanusi interpreted this attribute in two ways: (1) independence from a locus (al-mahall) – meaning not subsisting in a subject, and (2) independence from a specifier (al-mukhassis) – meaning not needing something to specify its existence. For each, Sanusi provided a proof but condensed the syllogisms. For the first proof, the full syllogism would be: if He were not self-subsistent (independent of a locus), He would need a locus; but needing a locus is impossible because that would make Him an accident (sifa); but being an accident is impossible. Sanusi omitted the initial conditional premise and the exceptive premise that affirms the impossibility of being an accident, replacing the latter with his statement 'and the accident does not qualify...' which implies that attributes cannot be described with the same attributes as the Necessary Being. For the second proof, the full syllogism is: if He were not self-subsistent (independent of a specifier), He would need a specifier; but that is impossible because it would make Him contingent; but contingency is impossible. Sanusi omitted the first syllogism and replaced the exceptive premise with 'How, while the proof has been established...', which refers to the already-established proof that the Necessary Being cannot be contingent. Al-Bajuri notes that these omissions were for conciseness, relying on the evidentiary context.

Key Terms
قيام بنفسه
افتقار إلى محل
صفة
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bajuri, why did Sanusi omit the first syllogism in the proof for independence from a locus?
A) Because he relied on the evidence that the accident does not qualify for such attributes
B) Because the first syllogism is invalid
C) Because the proof is too complex to state fully
D) Because he wanted to confuse readers

Key Takeaway
The first proof for Allah's self-subsistence shows that He cannot need a locus, as that would make Him an accident, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on countless loci every day—your health, your wealth, your relationships. But have you ever truly internalized that Allah depends on absolutely nothing? How will you let this truth reshape your reliance on created things starting today?
?
When life collapses and your 'loci' fail, do you seek Allah in the same ways you used to seek them? If He cannot be contained by any place or person, what new direction will you turn to find Him tonight?
?
You have read that Allah being an accident is impossible. But do you live as if He is just another 'attribute' of your life—something that adds to you rather than being the very ground of your existence? What concrete step can you take tomorrow to shift from treating Allah as a quality of your world to acknowledging Him as its sole foundation?

The Second Proof: Independence from a Specifier
Al-Bajuri explains the second proof for qiyam bi-nafsihi concerning independence from a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for Allah's self-subsistence, which argues that He cannot need a specifier because that would imply contingency, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Needing a specifier would make Allah contingent, which is impossible.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the second proof. Just as needing a locus is impossible, so is needing a specifier, because that would entail contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second proof
Focus on Al-Bajuri's reconstruction of the syllogism for independence from a specifier.
Note that this proof parallels the first but uses the term mukhassis.
2
Identify the key term 'mukhassis'
A specifier is something that determines existence; needing one implies contingency.
In kalam, a contingent being (hadith) needs a specifier.
3
Reconstruct the full syllogism
Write out the conditional and the exceptive premise as Al-Bajuri presents them.
The full argument: If not self-subsistent (no specifier) -> would need a specifier -> impossible because that would be contingent.
4
Note Sanusi's condensation
Sanusi omitted the first syllogism and the exceptive premise, relying on the statement 'How, while the proof has been established...' which refers to the already established impossibility of contingency for the Necessary Being.
This shows how later proofs build on earlier ones.

The author proceeded in what preceded to interpret His self-subsistence by: His lack of need for a locus, and: His lack of need for a specifier, and therefore he treated each with a proof. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Combined Proof for Power, Will, Knowledge, and Life
Sanusi offers a single proof for four divine attributes linked by entailment chains ending in the impossibility of no world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the necessity of four divine attributes through a single unified argument that shows their negation leads to the impossibility of any contingent existence.

Core Idea
A single proof links power, will, knowledge, and life: deny any one, and the world cannot exist.

Sanusi presents a compact, powerful proof that bundles four essential divine attributes into one logical chain. Instead of separate arguments, he shows how the negation of any one attribute inevitably leads to the impossibility of any created thing existing. This lesson walks through each link in that chain and explains why it holds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the unified structure
Notice that all four attributes are proved together because they share the same impossible consequence: the non-existence of any contingent being.
The proof is a single argument with four branches.
2
Trace the chain for power
Follow the reasoning: if power is negated, its opposite (incapability) is established, and an incapable being cannot create anything, so no world exists.
3
Follow the chain for will and knowledge
Understand how will depends on knowledge (no will without knowledge) and power depends on will (power's operation is a branch of will). Negating knowledge leads to ignorance, which negates will, then power, then incapability, then no world.
Simple ignorance negates will; compound ignorance does not.
4
See the role of life as a condition
Life is a condition for the other attributes; its negation negates all three, leading again to the impossibility of any events.
The argument concludes with a formal syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ اتِّصَافِهِ تَعَالَى بِالْقُدْرَةِ وَالْإِرَادَةِ وَالْعِلْمِ وَالْحَيَاةِ فَجُمِعَتْ فِي دَلِيلٍ وَاحِدٍ لِاتِّحَادِ اللَّازِمِ عَلَى نَفْيِهَا وَهُوَ عَدَمُ وُجُودِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ، وَوَجْهُ اللُّزُومِ فِي الْقُدْرَةِ: أَنَّهُ إِنِ انْتَفَتْ ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهَا، وَهُوَ الْعَجْزُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يُوجَدُ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ. وَوَجْهُ اللُّزُومِ فِي الْإِرَادَةِ: أَنَّهُ إِنِ انْتَفَتْ ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهَا، وَهُوَ الْكَرَاهَةُ بِمَعْنَى: عَدَمِ الْإِرَادَةِ، وَإِذَا ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهَا بِهَذَا الْمَعْنَى انْتَفَتِ الْقُدْرَةُ لِأَنَّهَا فَرْعٌ عَنِ الْإِرَادَةِ فِي التَّعَقُّلِ، وَإِذَا انْتَفَتِ الْقُدْرَةُ ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهَا وَهُوَ الْعَجْزُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يُوجَدُ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ. وَوَجْهُ اللُّزُومِ فِي الْعِلْمِ: أَنَّهُ إِنِ انْتَفَى ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ، وَهُوَ الْجَهْلُ، وَإِذَا ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ انْتَفَتِ الْإِرَادَةُ لِأَنَّهُ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ إِرَادَةٌ مِنْ غَيْرِ عِلْمٍ، وَإِذَا انْتَفَتِ الْإِرَادَةُ ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهَا إِلَى آخِرِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَوَجْهُ اللُّزُومِ فِي الْحَيَاةِ: أَنَّهُ إِنِ انْتَفَتِ انْتَفَتِ الثَّلَاثَةُ قَبْلَهَا بَلْ جَمِيعُ الصِّفَاتِ لِأَنَّهَا شَرْطٌ فِيهَا، وَإِذَا انْتَفَتِ الثَّلَاثَةُ الْمَذْكُورَةُ ثَبَتَ أَضْدَادُهَا وَمِنْهَا الْعَجْزُ إِلَى آخِرِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ. فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ، لَكِنْ عَدَمُ وُجُودِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ مُحَالٌ، فَمَا أَدَّى إِلَيْهِ وَهُوَ انْتِفَاءُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهَا مُحَالٌ. (قوله: لِأَنَّهَا فَرْعٌ عَنِ الْإِرَادَةِ فِي التَّعَقُّلِ) فِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ: (فِي التَّعَلُّقِ) وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ. وَمَعْنَاهُ أَنَّ تَعَلُّقَ الْقُدْرَةِ فَرْعٌ عَنْ تَعَلُّقِ الْإِرَادَةِ، فَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ الْقُدْرَةُ إِلَّا بِمَا تَعَلَّقَتْ بِهِ الْإِرَادَةُ، وَإِنَّمَا اسْتَلْزَمَ انْتِفَاءُ الْإِرَادَةِ انْتِفَاءَ الْقُدْرَةِ لِأَنَّ انْتِفَاءَ الْإِرَادَةِ يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَاءِ تَعَلُّقِهَا، وَانْتِفَاءُ تَعَلُّقِهَا يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَاءِ تَعَلُّقِ الْقُدْرَةِ لِأَنَّهُ فَرْعٌ عَنْهُ، وَانْتِفَاءُ تَعَلُّقِ الْقُدْرَةِ يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَائِهَا لِأَنَّ تَعَلُّقَهَا مِنْ لَوَازِمِهَا وَانْتِفَاءُ اللَّازِمِ يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَاءِ الْمَلْزُومِ. (قوله: لَكِنْ عَدَمُ وُجُودِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ مُحَالٌ) الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ بِالْإِحَالَةِ عَدَمُهَا تَقْرِيرَاتِ الْعَلَّامَةِ مُحَمَّدِ الْأَنْيَابِيِّ. (قوله: وَوَجْهُ اللُّزُومِ فِي الْعِلْمِ: أَنَّهُ إِذَا انْتَقَى ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ، وَهُوَ الْجَهْلُ، وَإِذَا ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ انْتَفَتِ الْإِرَادَةُ) هَذَا ظَاهِرٌ إِذَا أُرِيدَ بِالْجَهْلِ الْجَهْلَ الْبَسِيطَ، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا أُرِيدَ بِهِ: الْجَهْلَ الْمُرَكَّبَ، وَمِثْلُهُ: الظَّنُّ وَالشَّكُّ وَالْوَهْمُ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا تَنْتَفِي الْإِرَادَةُ مَعَ هَذِهِ الْأُمُورِ، فَيَحْتَاجُ فِي ذَلِكَ لِبَيَانٍ.
As for the proof of the necessity of His description, exalted, with power, will, knowledge, and life, they are combined in a single proof due to the unity of the necessary consequence of negating them, which is the non-existence of anything from the world. The aspect of entailment in power: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is incapability, and then nothing from the world exists. The aspect of entailment in will: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is aversion in the sense of non-will; and when its opposite is established in this sense, power is negated because it is a branch of will in the intellectual order; and when power is negated, its opposite, incapability, is established, and then nothing from the world exists. The aspect of entailment in knowledge: if it is negated, its opposite, ignorance, is established; and when its opposite is established, will is negated because no will is related without knowledge; and when will is negated, its opposite is established, continuing as before. The aspect of entailment in life: if it is negated, the three before it are negated, indeed all attributes, because it is a condition for them; and when the three mentioned are negated, their opposites are established, including incapability, continuing as before. So because if any of them were negated, nothing from the events would exist; but the non-existence of anything from the events is impossible; therefore that which leads to it, namely the negation of any of them, is impossible. (His saying: 'because it is a branch of will in the intellectual order') in some copies: 'in relation' (fi al-taʿalluq), and that is evident. Its meaning is that the relation of power is a branch of the relation of will; so power is only related to what the will is related to. Indeed, the negation of will necessitates the negation of power because negation of will leads to negation of its relation, and negation of its relation leads to negation of the relation of power because it is a branch of it, and negation of the relation of power leads to its negation because its relation is among its necessary consequences, and negation of the necessary leads to negation of the necessary ground. (His saying: 'but the non-existence of anything from the events is impossible') what is judged as impossible is their non-existence, according to the determinations of the scholar Muhammad al-Annabi. (His saying: 'and the aspect of entailment in knowledge: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is ignorance; and when its opposite is established, will is negated') this is evident if by 'ignorance' simple ignorance is meant, unlike compound ignorance. Similar are assumption, doubt, and illusion, for will is not negated with these matters; so an explanation is needed for that.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage provides a combined proof for four divine attributes—power (qudra), will (irāda), knowledge (ʿilm), and life (ḥayāh)—showing that their negation leads to impossibility. The reasoning is unified: each attribute's absence leads to the non-existence of any contingent event (ḥawādith), which is impossible; therefore each attribute must exist. The structure is a chain of entailments. For power: if negated, its opposite (ʿajz, incapability) exists, and an incapable being cannot create anything, so no world exists. For will: if negated, its opposite (karāha, aversion in the sense of non-will) exists, which necessitates the negation of power (because power is conceptually subordinate to will—power only acts on what is willed), leading back to incapability and no world. For knowledge: if negated, its opposite (jahl, ignorance) exists; ignorance prevents will (since will requires knowledge of what is willed), so will is negated, and the chain continues. For life: if negated, all three preceding attributes are negated (life is a condition for them), leading again to incapability and no events. The argument concludes with a formal syllogism: (1) If any of these attributes were negated, no events would exist; (2) The non-existence of events is impossible; (3) Therefore, the negation of any attribute is impossible—hence they must exist. The marginalia add crucial clarifications. The note on 'branch (farʿ) of will' explains that power's operation (taʿalluq) depends on will's operation; thus negation of will entails negation of power through a chain of necessary consequences. Another note addresses the type of ignorance: simple ignorance (lack of knowledge) negates will, but compound ignorance (false belief), doubt, assumption, etc., do not necessarily negate will, so further explanation is needed. This shows the precision of kalam reasoning about these entailments.

إرادة

حياة

شرط
فرع

الجهل البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is four separate arguments for each attribute.
Correction: The proof is unified: all four attributes are linked to the same impossible consequence, and the chains of entailment connect them together.

Checkpoint
What is the necessary consequence (اللازم) that unites the proof for all four attributes?
A) The impossibility of ignorance.
B) The non-existence of anything from the world (عدم وجود شيء من العالم).
C) The necessity of life.
D) The multiplicity of gods.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of power, will, knowledge, and life is proven by a single argument: their negation leads to the impossibility of any contingent existence, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof begins with the undeniable fact that the world exists, including you. If you truly believed that your very existence depends on God possessing these four attributes necessarily, how would that change your awareness of Him throughout your day? Identify one moment today where you will pause and affirm: 'Because I exist, Allah is Powerful, Willing, Knowing, and Living.'
?
The proof shows that power is a branch of will; God does not act without purpose. In your own life, where have you acted without knowledge or will, treating God's power as if it were mere force? What would it look like to submit your own will to His, knowing that His will is always with knowledge and power?
?
If the world's existence itself proves these attributes, then every blade of grass is a testimony. When did you last look at creation and feel the weight of that proof? Commit to a specific act of gratitude today that acknowledges not just God's power, but His will and knowledge behind it.

Divine Attributes

Proof of the Necessity of Divine Attributes
Al-Bajuri gives a unified rational proof for four divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof for the necessity of God's attributes of power, will, knowledge, and life, and how the negation of any one leads to the impossibility of the existence of any events.

Core Idea
Denying any divine attribute leads to an impossible world.

Al-Bajuri now presents a coherent proof that God must possess the attributes of power, will, knowledge, and life. He combines them in one argument because denying any one of them ultimately results in the same absurdity: nothing in the world could exist. This lesson walks through the reasoning for each attribute and the overall syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Go through the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structured argument for each attribute.
The passage includes glosses; focus on the main text first.
2
Understand the proof for each attribute
Identify the four attributes: power, will, knowledge, life. For each, Al-Bajuri explains what follows from its negation.
Each has a chain: negation → opposite → further consequences.
3
Grasp the overall syllogism
The final part (قوله: فَلِأَنَّهُ...) presents the formal argument: if any attribute is missing, no event exists; but events do exist; so the attributes must exist.
The author omits the exceptive premise because it's obvious.
4
Reflect on the theological import
Consider why this proof is foundational. It shows that God's attributes are not arbitrary but rationally necessary.
This connects to the larger Islamic theological project.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ اتِّصَافِهِ تَعَالَى بِالْقُدْرَةِ ... إلخ) إنما جَمَعَها في دليل واحد لاتحاد اللازم على نفيها وهو عَدَمُ وجود شيء من العالم، ووَجْهُ اللزوم في القدرة: أنه إذا انتتْ ثَبَتَ ضِدها، وهو العجز، وحينئذ لا يُوجَدُ شيءٌ من العالم. ووجه اللزوم في الإرادة أنه إذا انتفَتْ ثَبَتَ ضدها، وهو الكراهة، بمعنى: عدم الإرادة، وإذا ثَبَتَ ضِدُّها بهذا المعنى؛ انتفَتِ القدرة؛ لأنها فَرْعٌ عن الإرادة في التعقل، وإذا انتفَتِ القدرةُ ثَبَتَ ضدُّها، وهو العجز، وحينئذ لا يوجد شيء من العالم. ووجه اللزوم في العلم أنه إذا انتفى ثَبَتَ ضده، وهو الجهل، وإذا ثَبَتَ ضده انتفَتِ الإرادة؛ لأنه لا يتعلَّقُ إرادةً من غير عِلْمٍ، وإذا انتفت الإرادة ثَبَتَ ضدها إلى آخر ما تقدم. ووجه اللزوم في الحياة: أنه إذا انتفت انتفت الثلاثة قبلها، بل جميع الصفات؛ لأنها شَرْط فيها، وإذا انتفت الثلاثةُ المذكورةُ ثَبَتَ أضدادها، ومنها العجزُ إلى آخر ما تقدم. (قوله: فَلِأَنَّهُ... إلخ) تقريره هكذا: لو انتفى شيءٌ منها لما وُجِدَ شيء من الحوادث، لكن عَدَمُ وجودِ شيء من الحوادث محال، فما أدى إليه ـ وهو انتفاء شيء منها - محال، وإذا استحال انتفاءُ شيءٍ منها ثَبَتَ وجودها، وهو المطلوب. فالمصنف ذَكَرَ الشرطية، وحَذَفَ الاستثنائية لظهورها. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: لأَنهَا فَرْعٌ عَنِ الْإِرَادَةِ فِي التَّعَقُلِ في بعض النسخ: (في التعلق)، وهو ظاهر. ومعناه أن تعلق القدرة فرع عن تعلق الإرادة، فلا تتعلق القدرة إلا بما تعلقت به الإرادة، وإنما استلزم انتفاء الإرادة انتفاء القدرة؛ لأن انتفاء الإرادة يؤدي إلى انتفاء تعلقها، وانتفاء تعلقها يؤدي إلى انتفاء تعلق القدرة؛ لأنه فرع عنه وانتفاء تعلق القدرة يؤدي إلى انتفائها؛ لأن تعلقها من لوازمها وانتفاء اللازم يؤدي إلى انتفاء الملزوم. (قوله: لَكِنْ عَدَمُ وُجُودِ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ مُحَالٌ) المحكوم عليه بالإحالة عدمها تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنيابي. (قوله: وَوَجْهُ اللُّزوم فِي الْعِلْمِ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا انْتَقَى ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ، وَهُوَ الْجَهْلُ، وَإِذَا ثَبَتَ ضِدُّهُ انْتَفَتِ الْإِرَادَةُ هذا ظاهر إذا أريد بالجهل الجهل البسيط، بخلاف ما إذا أريد به: الجهل المركب، ومثله: الظنُّ والشكُ والوهم، فإنه لا تنتفي الإرادة مع هذه الأمور، فيحتاج في ذلك لبيان.
His statement: 'As for the proof of the necessity of His attribute of power... etc.' He combined them in one proof due to the unity of the necessary consequence of their negation, which is the non-existence of anything from the world. The aspect of necessity in power: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is incapacity, and then nothing from the world exists. The aspect of necessity in will: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is aversion, meaning non-will; and when its opposite is established in this sense, power is negated because it is a branch of will in intellect; and when power is negated, its opposite is established, which is incapacity, and then nothing from the world exists. The aspect of necessity in knowledge: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is ignorance; and when its opposite is established, will is negated because will does not attach without knowledge; and when will is negated, its opposite is established, up to what preceded. The aspect of necessity in life: if it is negated, the three before it are negated, rather all attributes, because it is a condition for them; and when the mentioned three are negated, their opposites are established, including incapacity, up to what preceded. His statement: 'For it... etc.' Its formulation is thus: If any of them were negated, no event would exist; but the non-existence of any event is impossible; so what leads to it—which is the negation of any of them—is impossible; and when the negation of any of them is impossible, its existence is established, and that is the desired conclusion. So the author mentioned the conditional and omitted the exception proposition due to its obviousness. A gloss by the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His statement: 'Because they are a branch of will in intellect' in some recensions: 'in attachment,' and it is apparent. Its meaning: that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will; power attaches only to what will has attached to; and the negation of will entails the negation of power because the negation of will leads to the negation of its attachment, and the negation of its attachment leads to the negation of the attachment of power because it is a branch of it, and the negation of the attachment of power leads to its negation because its attachment is among its necessary concomitants, and the negation of the necessary concomitant leads to the negation of the necessitated. His statement: 'But the non-existence of any event is impossible' what is judged as impossible is its non-existence. The elaborations of the scholar Muhammad al-Annabi. His statement: 'And the aspect of necessity in knowledge: if it is negated, its opposite is established, which is ignorance; and when its opposite is established, will is negated.' This is apparent if by ignorance is meant simple ignorance, contrary to if what is intended is compound ignorance, and similarly doubt, suspicion, and illusion, for will is not negated with these matters, so this requires clarification.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage explains the proof for the necessity of God's attributes of power, will, knowledge, and life. The author begins by stating that these four attributes are combined in one proof because their denial leads to the same impossible consequence: the non-existence of anything from the world. He then details the specific reasoning (wajh al-luzum) for each attribute. For power: its negation implies its opposite (incapacity), which would prevent any event from existing. For will: its negation implies aversion (non-will), which in turn negates power because power's attachment depends on will; then incapacity follows, again preventing events. For knowledge: its negation implies ignorance, which negates will because will cannot attach without knowledge; then the chain proceeds as before. For life: its negation negates all other attributes because life is a condition for them; so the three earlier attributes are negated, leading to their opposites and ultimately to incapacity. After explaining the necessity for each, the author formulates the complete logical argument: a conditional statement ('If any of them were negated, no event would exist') and an exceptive statement ('But the non-existence of any event is impossible'), yielding the conclusion that the negation of any attribute is impossible, therefore the attributes exist. The author mentions only the conditional, considering the exceptive obvious. The glosses clarify technical points: the relationship between power and will as a branch, the meaning of 'attachment,' and a nuance about ignorance—whether simple or compound—affecting the negation of will.

الإرادة (al-irāda)
العلم (al-ʿilm)

العجز (al-ʿajz)
الجهل (al-jahl)
الشرطية (al-sharṭiyya)
الاستثنائية (al-istithnāʾiyya)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of the world as a premise, which is not proven.
Correction: The proof does not assume the existence of the world; it uses the impossibility of the non-existence of the world as a given fact (i.e., the world obviously exists, so its non-existence is impossible).

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the immediate consequence of denying God's power?
A) The world would not exist
B) God would be unjust
C) Power would be transferred to another
D) Nothing happens

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that the four essential divine attributes are logically necessary for the existence of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if God lacked any of these attributes, nothing could exist—including you. Do you truly live as if every moment depends on His power, will, knowledge, and life? What would change if you internalized that your very existence is a direct reflection of these attributes?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of events not existing. Look at your own life: can you point to any event that does not rely on God's will? How does this chain of dependencies confront your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
If simple ignorance negates God's will, how much more should you flee from ignorance about Him? Are you content with simple ignorance of His attributes, or do you actively seek knowledge that deepens your reliance on His will? What step will you take today to replace ignorance with certainty?

The Mu'tazila Objection to the Proof of Attributes
Mu'tazila objection to the proof's conditional connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that the absence of the attributes of meanings does not necessitate the absence of contingent things, because they attribute contingency to meaning-related attributes (al-ma'nawiyya).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny the link between absence of attributes and absence of contingency.

In this lesson, we examine the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila challenge the proof's conditional statement. They argue that even if God lacks the seven attributes of meanings, contingent things could still exist because they could be attributed to meaning-related attributes (ma'nawiyya) – a key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazila theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'قوله: لَوِ انْتَفَى...' to '...لا على انتفاء المعاني'. Notice how the objection is framed as a denial of the conditional connection.
The objection is introduced by 'اعتُرِضَ'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila affirm that God is powerful, willing, etc., by His essence, not by superadded attributes. They call these meaning-related attributes (al-ma'nawiyya).
3
Identify the reformulation
The Mu'tazila reformulate the major premise: absence of contingent things is linked to absence of ma'nawiyya, not to absence of ma'ani.
This is the crux of their objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ اعتُرِضَ: بأن هذه الملازمة ممنوعةٌ؛ لأنه لا يلزم من انتفاء صفات المعاني عَدَمُ وجود شيء من الحوادث، بل يجوز انتفاؤُها وتُوجَدُ الحوادِثُ لاستنادها إلى المعنوية كما تقول به المعتزلة، فإنهم لا يُثبتون صفات المعاني، وإنما يثبتون المعنوية، فيقولون: هو قادرٌ بذاته لا بقدرة زائدة عليها، مُرِيدٌ بذاته لا بإرادة زائدة عليها، وهكذا. ولذلك رَتَّبَ في الكبرى عَدَمَ وجود شيء من الحوادث على انتفاء المعنوية لا على انتفاء المعاني حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري - مع مشاهدة وجودها لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين أما عدمها السابق واللاحق؛ فجائزان كما لا يخفى (قوله: وَإِنَّمَا يُثْبِتُونَ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةً) فيثبتون الكون قادراً، والكون مريداً، وهكذا إلى آخر السبعة، لكن هذه الأكوان عندهم غيرها عند أهل السنة، فعندهم (الكون قادراً) عبارة عن تمكنه من إيجاد الممكنات وإعدامها لو لم يتصف بها بذاته، و(الكون مريداً) عبارة عن تخصيصه الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه بذاته، والكون عالماً عبارة عن إحاطته بالأشياء على ما هي عليه بذاته، والكون حياً عبارة عن اتصافه بهذه الأمور السابقة بذاته، والكون سميعاً عبارة عن إحاطته بالموجودات بذاته، وهكذا يقال في الكون بصيراً، والكون متكلماً عبارة عن خلقه الكلام في بعض الأجسام كالشجرة التي سمع موسى عندها الكلام، فإنهم يزعمون أن الكلام الذي سمعه موسى قائم بالشجرة التي وقع تكليمه عندها، وأما أهل السنة؛ فهذه الأكوان عندهم عبارة عن قيام الصفات بالذات، فالكون قادراً عبارة عن قيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس، وهكذا بناء على نفي الأحوال، وعبارة عن صفات ثابتة للذات لازمة للمعاني بناء على إثبات الأحوال. ثم هذه الأكوان عند المعتزلة أمور اعتبارية إن كانوا يقولون بنفي الأحوال، فإن كانوا يقولون بثبوت الأحوال احتمل أن تكون عندهم أحوالاً وأن تكون أموراً اعتبارية. وأجيب بأن القول بإثبات المعنوية دون المعاني، فيكون قادراً بلا قدرة ومريداً بلا إرادة، وهكذا؛ واضح البطلان، فلذلك لم يكترِثِ المصنف به، وبهذا الجواب يندفعُ الاعتراضُ أيضاً بمنع الملازمة المذكورة لجواز انتفائها وتوجد الحوادث لكَوْنِ مُوجِدِها عِلَّةً أو طبيعة كما يقول الطبائعيون ومن في معناهم لعنهم الله تعالى على أن كلام المصنف مبنى على بطلان العلة والطبيعة، فلا يرد عليه ما ذُكِرَ حتى يحتاج للجواب عنه.
His statement: 'If any of them were absent, none of the contingent things would exist' was objected to: This conditional connection is denied because the absence of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) does not necessitate the non-existence of any contingent thing; rather, it is possible for them to be absent and yet contingent things exist, due to their attribution to the meaning-related attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) as held by the Muʿtazila. For they do not affirm the attributes of meanings, but rather affirm the meaning-related attributes, saying: He is powerful (qādir) by His essence, not by a superadded power; willing (murīd) by His essence, not by a superadded will, and so on. Therefore, in the major premise, he arranged the non-existence of any contingent thing upon the absence of the meaning-related attributes, not upon the absence of the attributes of meanings. Annotation of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī - with witnessing their existence due to what necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories; as for their non-existence prior and subsequent, both are permissible, as is not hidden. His statement: 'They affirm only the meaning-related attributes' - so they affirm the state of being powerful (kawn qādiran), the state of being willing (kawn murīdan), and so on to the seventh. But these states (akwān) according to them are different from those according to Ahl al-Sunna. According to them, 'the state of being powerful' is an expression for His ability to bring into existence possible things and to annihilate them, even if He were not described by that with His essence; and 'the state of being willing' is an expression for His specification of a possible thing with some of what is permissible for it by His essence; and 'the state of being knowing' is an expression for His encompassing things as they are by His essence; and 'the state of being living' is an expression for His being described with these previous matters by His essence; and 'the state of being hearing' is an expression for His encompassing existents by His essence; and likewise is said for 'the state of being seeing'; and 'the state of being speaking' is an expression for His creating speech in some bodies like the tree from which Moses heard the speech. For they claim that the speech which Moses heard inhered in the tree at which his speaking occurred. As for Ahl al-Sunna, these states according to them are expressions for the inherence of attributes in the essence: 'the state of being powerful' is an expression for the inherence of power in the most holy essence, and thus, based on the negation of modes (aḥwāl), and expressions for attributes subsisting in the essence, necessary for the meanings, based on the affirmation of modes. Then these states according to the Muʿtazila are mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya) if they held the negation of modes; if they held the affirmation of modes, they might be modes for them or might be mental considerations. The answer is: The statement affirming the meaning-related attributes without the attributes of meanings, such that He is powerful without power, willing without will, and so on, is clearly false. That is why the author did not pay attention to it. By this answer, the objection is also repelled by denying the aforementioned conditional connection, because it is possible for them to be absent and yet contingent things exist, due to their originator being a cause (ʿilla) or nature (ṭabīʿa) as claimed by the naturalists and those like them—may God curse them—given that the author's discourse is based on the falsity of cause and nature, so what is mentioned does not apply to him such that he would need to respond to it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila against the proof of divine attributes and provides the response. The passage begins by quoting the proof's conditional statement: 'If any of the attributes were absent, none of the contingent things would exist.' The objection denies this implication, arguing that the absence of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) does not necessitate the absence of contingent things, because contingent things could instead depend on the meaning-related attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) as the Mu'tazila hold. The Mu'tazila affirm that God is powerful, willing, etc., by His essence, not by superadded attributes. They therefore reformulate the major premise to link non-existence of contingents to the absence of meaning-related attributes, not the attributes of meanings. A marginal gloss by Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī is inserted, clarifying that the Mu'tazila's affirmation of the existence of meaning-related attributes would lead to a contradiction, but that prior and subsequent non-existence are permissible. The text then explains the Mu'tazila concept of 'states' (akwān): for them, 'being powerful' means the ability to create and annihilate by essence, 'being willing' means specification by essence, etc. They deny that these states are attributes inhering in the essence; rather, they are either mental considerations or modes (aḥwāl), depending on their position on modes. For Ahl al-Sunna, these states are expressions for the inherence of the real attributes (power, will, etc.) in the essence, either as modes or as descriptions of the inherence itself. The answer to the objection is then given: affirming meaning-related attributes without the actual attributes (i.e., being powerful without power) is clearly false, so the author (Sanusi) did not deem the objection worthy of consideration. Furthermore, the implication is also denied by the possibility that contingent things could arise from a cause or nature, as the naturalists claim, but the author's argument presupposes the falsity of cause and nature, so this is not a valid counterexample.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
المعنوية
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Mu'tazila deny all divine attributes entirely.
Correction: Mu'tazila affirm meaning-related attributes (ma'nawiyya) – God is powerful, willing, etc., by His essence – but deny that these are superadded attributes inhering in the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Mu'tazila objection as presented in this passage?
A) They deny the existence of God.
B) They claim that contingent things can exist without any divine attributes.
C) They deny the implication that absence of attributes leads to absence of contingent things, because contingent things can be attributed to meaning-related attributes.
D) They argue that the proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila deny the proof's conditional connection by attributing contingent things to meaning-related attributes (ma'nawiyya) instead of inhering attributes (ma'ani).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just encountered a deep theological dispute about how God's attributes relate to His actions. Ask yourself: In your own understanding of Allah, do you ever feel a tension between His oneness and His many attributes? Have you, knowingly or unknowingly, leaned toward a rationalistic view that downplays His attributes to protect tawhid? Where is your heart in this? Will you pledge to accept the descriptions of Allah as taught by Ahl al-Sunna, even when your reason struggles?
?
The Mu'tazila affirmed that God is powerful by essence, not by a separate attribute. This seems subtle, but it changes how we relate to Allah. Consider: Do you truly worship Allah as described in the Qur'an and Sunnah, with all His names and attributes, or have you reduced Him to a philosophical concept? If you find the latter, what step will you take today to return to a worship that is built on revelation, not speculation?
?
The conditional connection denied by the Mu'tazila is about cause and effect in the divine realm. In your daily life, do you sometimes think your actions (causes) automatically produce results (effects) without acknowledging Allah's ongoing involvement? How does this lesson's emphasis on Allah's direct and unmediated power over all events challenge your practical reliance on means and causes? Will you consciously attach your heart to Allah's will in every endeavor today?

Mu'tazila and Sunni Concepts of Divine States (Akwan)
Explanation of Mu'tazila and Sunni views on divine states (akwan).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of divine states (akwan) and how it differs from the Sunni understanding.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, divine states are mental considerations; for Sunnis, they are real attributes inhering in God's essence.

This lesson delves into the explanation of 'states' (akwan) as described by the Mu'tazila and contrasted with the Sunni position. The Mu'tazila define states like 'being powerful', 'being willing', etc., as descriptions of God's essence without real attributes. The Sunni understanding, on the other hand, sees these states as expressions for the inherence of real attributes in the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on akwan
Read from 'قوله: وَإِنَّمَا يُثْبِتُونَ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةً' to 'وأن تكون أموراً اعتبارية'. Pay attention to the definitions of each state.
The passage lists seven states (akwan) corresponding to the seven attributes.
2
Understand Mu'tazila definitions
For each state, note how the Mu'tazila define it purely in terms of God's essence, without inhering attributes. For example, 'being powerful' means ability to create and annihilate by essence.
3
Compare with Sunni view
The passage then explains that for Ahl al-Sunna, these states are expressions for the inherence of attributes in the essence, and they are either modes (ahwal) or mental considerations depending on one's position.
This highlights the fundamental difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ اعتُرِضَ: بأن هذه الملازمة ممنوعةٌ؛ لأنه لا يلزم من انتفاء صفات المعاني عَدَمُ وجود شيء من الحوادث، بل يجوز انتفاؤُها وتُوجَدُ الحوادِثُ لاستنادها إلى المعنوية كما تقول به المعتزلة، فإنهم لا يُثبتون صفات المعاني، وإنما يثبتون المعنوية، فيقولون: هو قادرٌ بذاته لا بقدرة زائدة عليها، مُرِيدٌ بذاته لا بإرادة زائدة عليها، وهكذا. ولذلك رَتَّبَ في الكبرى عَدَمَ وجود شيء من الحوادث على انتفاء المعنوية لا على انتفاء المعاني حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري - مع مشاهدة وجودها لما يلزم عليه من اجتماع النقيضين أما عدمها السابق واللاحق؛ فجائزان كما لا يخفى (قوله: وَإِنَّمَا يُثْبِتُونَ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةً) فيثبتون الكون قادراً، والكون مريداً، وهكذا إلى آخر السبعة، لكن هذه الأكوان عندهم غيرها عند أهل السنة، فعندهم (الكون قادراً) عبارة عن تمكنه من إيجاد الممكنات وإعدامها لو لم يتصف بها بذاته، و(الكون مريداً) عبارة عن تخصيصه الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه بذاته، والكون عالماً عبارة عن إحاطته بالأشياء على ما هي عليه بذاته، والكون حياً عبارة عن اتصافه بهذه الأمور السابقة بذاته، والكون سميعاً عبارة عن إحاطته بالموجودات بذاته، وهكذا يقال في الكون بصيراً، والكون متكلماً عبارة عن خلقه الكلام في بعض الأجسام كالشجرة التي سمع موسى عندها الكلام، فإنهم يزعمون أن الكلام الذي سمعه موسى قائم بالشجرة التي وقع تكليمه عندها، وأما أهل السنة؛ فهذه الأكوان عندهم عبارة عن قيام الصفات بالذات، فالكون قادراً عبارة عن قيام القدرة بالذات الأقدس، وهكذا بناء على نفي الأحوال، وعبارة عن صفات ثابتة للذات لازمة للمعاني بناء على إثبات الأحوال. ثم هذه الأكوان عند المعتزلة أمور اعتبارية إن كانوا يقولون بنفي الأحوال، فإن كانوا يقولون بثبوت الأحوال احتمل أن تكون عندهم أحوالاً وأن تكون أموراً اعتبارية. وأجيب بأن القول بإثبات المعنوية دون المعاني، فيكون قادراً بلا قدرة ومريداً بلا إرادة، وهكذا؛ واضح البطلان، فلذلك لم يكترِثِ المصنف به، وبهذا الجواب يندفعُ الاعتراضُ أيضاً بمنع الملازمة المذكورة لجواز انتفائها وتوجد الحوادث لكَوْنِ مُوجِدِها عِلَّةً أو طبيعة كما يقول الطبائعيون ومن في معناهم لعنهم الله تعالى على أن كلام المصنف مبنى على بطلان العلة والطبيعة، فلا يرد عليه ما ذُكِرَ حتى يحتاج للجواب عنه.
His statement: 'If any of them were absent, none of the contingent things would exist' was objected to: This conditional connection is denied because the absence of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) does not necessitate the non-existence of any contingent thing; rather, it is possible for them to be absent and yet contingent things exist, due to their attribution to the meaning-related attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) as held by the Muʿtazila. For they do not affirm the attributes of meanings, but rather affirm the meaning-related attributes, saying: He is powerful (qādir) by His essence, not by a superadded power; willing (murīd) by His essence, not by a superadded will, and so on. Therefore, in the major premise, he arranged the non-existence of any contingent thing upon the absence of the meaning-related attributes, not upon the absence of the attributes of meanings. Annotation of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī - with witnessing their existence due to what necessitates the conjunction of two contradictories; as for their non-existence prior and subsequent, both are permissible, as is not hidden. His statement: 'They affirm only the meaning-related attributes' - so they affirm the state of being powerful (kawn qādiran), the state of being willing (kawn murīdan), and so on to the seventh. But these states (akwān) according to them are different from those according to Ahl al-Sunna. According to them, 'the state of being powerful' is an expression for His ability to bring into existence possible things and to annihilate them, even if He were not described by that with His essence; and 'the state of being willing' is an expression for His specification of a possible thing with some of what is permissible for it by His essence; and 'the state of being knowing' is an expression for His encompassing things as they are by His essence; and 'the state of being living' is an expression for His being described with these previous matters by His essence; and 'the state of being hearing' is an expression for His encompassing existents by His essence; and likewise is said for 'the state of being seeing'; and 'the state of being speaking' is an expression for His creating speech in some bodies like the tree from which Moses heard the speech. For they claim that the speech which Moses heard inhered in the tree at which his speaking occurred. As for Ahl al-Sunna, these states according to them are expressions for the inherence of attributes in the essence: 'the state of being powerful' is an expression for the inherence of power in the most holy essence, and thus, based on the negation of modes (aḥwāl), and expressions for attributes subsisting in the essence, necessary for the meanings, based on the affirmation of modes. Then these states according to the Muʿtazila are mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya) if they held the negation of modes; if they held the affirmation of modes, they might be modes for them or might be mental considerations. The answer is: The statement affirming the meaning-related attributes without the attributes of meanings, such that He is powerful without power, willing without will, and so on, is clearly false. That is why the author did not pay attention to it. By this answer, the objection is also repelled by denying the aforementioned conditional connection, because it is possible for them to be absent and yet contingent things exist, due to their originator being a cause (ʿilla) or nature (ṭabīʿa) as claimed by the naturalists and those like them—may God curse them—given that the author's discourse is based on the falsity of cause and nature, so what is mentioned does not apply to him such that he would need to respond to it.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila against the proof of divine attributes and provides the response. The passage begins by quoting the proof's conditional statement: 'If any of the attributes were absent, none of the contingent things would exist.' The objection denies this implication, arguing that the absence of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) does not necessitate the absence of contingent things, because contingent things could instead depend on the meaning-related attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) as the Mu'tazila hold. The Mu'tazila affirm that God is powerful, willing, etc., by His essence, not by superadded attributes. They therefore reformulate the major premise to link non-existence of contingents to the absence of meaning-related attributes, not the attributes of meanings. A marginal gloss by Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī is inserted, clarifying that the Mu'tazila's affirmation of the existence of meaning-related attributes would lead to a contradiction, but that prior and subsequent non-existence are permissible. The text then explains the Mu'tazila concept of 'states' (akwān): for them, 'being powerful' means the ability to create and annihilate by essence, 'being willing' means specification by essence, etc. They deny that these states are attributes inhering in the essence; rather, they are either mental considerations or modes (aḥwāl), depending on their position on modes. For Ahl al-Sunna, these states are expressions for the inherence of the real attributes (power, will, etc.) in the essence, either as modes or as descriptions of the inherence itself. The answer to the objection is then given: affirming meaning-related attributes without the actual attributes (i.e., being powerful without power) is clearly false, so the author (Sanusi) did not deem the objection worthy of consideration. Furthermore, the implication is also denied by the possibility that contingent things could arise from a cause or nature, as the naturalists claim, but the author's argument presupposes the falsity of cause and nature, so this is not a valid counterexample.

C) The creation of knowledge in some body.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila define divine states (akwan) as descriptions of God's essence without real attributes, while Sunnis see them as expressions of real attributes inhering in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'Allah is All-Powerful,' do you mean it as a real description of His essence (as Sunnis affirm) or just as a mental concept (as Mu'tazila argue)? Examine your belief: Has your understanding of divine attributes been influenced by shallow conceptualization? Commit today to study the attributes as real and seek to know God through them.
?
The Mu'tazila reduce God's speech to a created event in a tree. In your daily life, do you treat the Qur'an as the literal speech of Allah inhering in His essence, or do you sometimes regard it as a mere created book? How does your view affect your reverence when reciting it? Pledge to honor the Qur'an as the uncreated word of Allah.

The Refutation of the Mu'tazila Objection
Refutation of the Mu'tazila objection and dismissal of naturalist counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila objection is considered invalid and how the naturalist counterexample is also dismissed.

Core Idea
Affirming meaning-related attributes without real attributes leads to clear absurdity.

After presenting the Mu'tazila objection and their concept of states, the passage provides the definitive refutation. The idea that God is powerful without power or willing without will is logically untenable. Additionally, the possibility of contingent things arising from nature or a cause is dismissed because the proof presupposes the falsehood of such notions.

Guided Path
1
Read the response section
Read from 'وأجيب بأن القول بإثبات المعنوية دون المعاني' to the end of the passage. Notice the structure: first the main refutation, then a supplementary dismissal of the naturalist objection.
The response includes two parts: the clear falsity of the Mu'tazila position, and the irrelevance of naturalism.
2
Understand the main refutation
The passage argues that attributing meaning-related attributes without the actual attributes (e.g., being powerful without power) is clearly false. That's why the author (Sanusi) ignored the objection.
3
Understand the dismissal of naturalism
Even if one objects that contingent things could come from cause or nature, the proof is built on the falsity of that, so it doesn't apply.
This is a secondary point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author dismisses the Mu'tazila objection?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever catch yourself trying to rationalize them away to avoid the weight of His absolute perfection? How does this passage's insistence that 'powerful without power' is absurd confront your own tendency to reduce God to manageable concepts?
?
The author dismisses the naturalist objection because his argument assumes its falsehood. In your own spiritual life, are there comfortable doubts or ideas about God that you have never genuinely examined because you assumed they were invalid? What would it cost you to honestly scrutinize them?
?
If you truly believed that denying God's real attributes leads to logical absurdity, how would that change the way you speak about and teach His names and attributes to others? Are there areas where you have been vague or hesitant out of fear of complexity?
